November
The Red Page.
The sonnets on our November are hardly
up to the October sample, and still there are
too many entries that are not sonnets at all.
It should be surely unnecessary to state
that a sonnet must have 14 lines ; that its
rime-scheme for the first eight lines must
be: a, b, b, a, a, b, b, a; for the last six
lines there may be two or three rimes,
almost in any order, except that, under the
conditions laid down for these competitions,
the last two lines must not have the same
rime; and that each line must consist of
five iambic feet. These are the mere tech-
nical requirements: the winning sonnet'
must have more than technical correctness.
Eleven of the entries were cast out because
in form they were not sonnets, and several
that had a chance of winning were rejected
on some technical ground.
E- Sparrow, like the Commonwealth
Statistician, rimes dawn with corny but
not even the authority of George Handley
Knibbs suffices for the acceptance of this
vagary. And “Mick Much” complains.
(For all complete sonnets quoted space-
rates are paid) :
Take it frum me the flamin’ time’s not up
Ter wot it's cracked ter be; I feel dead crook
With summer blazin’ on me since I took
Me tip among the spielers at the Cup.
The flies ’a’ got me bunged blind as a pup
One side, yet I must alius ’ave a look
Fer fear the mutton’s movin’ on the hook,
An’ screw the drink before I take a sup.
Them blokes wot skite o’ summertime an’ spring
They oughter do some yakker—ain’t it dirt
Thet dust an’ hotwind blaze should make ’em sing?
The way the days ’ang- out ! —me bit o’ skirt
Won’t smoodge till dark, no, not fer anything!—
Me socks ’ave burrs—there’s speargrass in me
shirt!
W.S.D. rimes beat with crime in a son-
net that had a chance, as will be seen from
its conclusion: —
She plucks the young rogue summer from his eot,
Gives him a kiss, downsets him on his feet,
Then with the cradle vanished from sight.
The lack of distinction in the octette
casts out L. Lee. His conclusion merits
print
Ah me! December lies in wait for thee ;
His warm hreath even now thy pearls embrown ;
His burning eyes note all thv ripening charm.
Will he. imnatient, hot, too eager be
And kill thy promises?—or softly drown
In tearful sighs the power to do thee harm?
Other sonneteers of merit are B. C.
Marriott Watson, George Powick Brown
(with a too categorical geographical son-
net), Duncan Brown (who becomes too
prosaic), L. A. Eoylett (who is too fond of
vague, high-sounding phrases), and Rudolf
N. Augstein (whose charming allegory has
little relation to November).
So, out of the 43 sent in, we find five for
final decision. M. Forrest paints an en-
chanting picture; but her palette is not
big enough for Australia.
The tired hills have drawn a lilac veil.
Like odalisques they sleep, while here and there
Glints as a jewel in the dreamy air
An iron roof through gum boughs blurred and
pale.
Beyond the purple pine-fringe of the dale
From the cool crooning of some weedy lair,
Flicks as a beckoning finger far and fair,
The wizard snaring of a rose-white sail....
Oh, we remember, all the breathless day
The highway of the river, smooth and green,
The rippled racing of the wind-caught hay
The islands where the ragged palm trees lean,
Brown seaweeds lying amber breasts between,
And long wet locks in darling disarray !
“Kuaka” comes very near the thing
desired. His sonnet is workmanlike and
pleasant, yet just lacks a note of distinc-
tion.
A maid of moods and wayward fancies this,
Who looks with frowning eyes upon the world,
Around her feet the roses, rain-impearled,
Uplift their glowing petals for her kiss ;
Nought of her largesse gives her passing bliss,
Unheeded round her- are the leaves unfurled,
Unseen the cloud of falling petals whirled
Of poppy red and purple clematis.
So broods she, knowing well she may not see
The harvest of the corn she knew at birth,
The full fruition of the laden tree,
Nor hear the sound of summer’s joyous mirth;
With sighs she goes, and watches, jealously,
December’s flaming feet o’ertread the earth.
D. Allen’s delightful effort is disqualified
by glaring faults in the rime-scheme of the
octette; but his humor places him in this
column :
I not so long a little sonnet made,
and headed it “October, rose-lipped lass,”
and sent it her (I was a perfect ass),
for now (misfortune!) I have wed a jade,
tormentful, troublous, though a virgin sure,
a spinster soured by adverse beauty shows,
all smart and ripe, and witted quick for blows,
a suffragette—she has no other flaw.
Seeing me yesterday October-sad,
she cast at me her fiery dartful eye
and knocked my hat off, filled my eyes with dust
and straight west-winded me, the mad
Virago ; look ! with hair blown all awry,
her sallow visage vanished in that gust.
So we are left with two: S. Elliott
Napier and Nina Murdoch (77 Ben Boyd-
road, Neutral Bay, Sydney). The former
came a good second in the October competi-
tion. And the latter was a winner
in another recent sonnet competition. And
neither of these sonnets is up to 4he
level of Gladys Kernot’s “October.”
(This month she did not compete.) S.
Elliott Napier is again placed second. His
octette is admirable, but his sestette thinly
spreads a thin idea. The sixth line but
repeats the fourth —in idea.
The winning sonnet has a weak phrase,
“on either hand,” where no weak phrase
should be. Yet she has painted, perhaps
with too much detail, a landscape that is
distinctively Australian; and then in broad
strokes put against that background the fine
figure of our November.
Deflower’d Spring lies haggard to the sky,
Dead and despoil’d, her golden tresses shorn
To make a tassell’d trophy for the corn ;
Her cheek’s fair flush, the azure of her eye
Purloin’d to paint the rank convolvuli;
The locust-sycophants, with strident scorn,
Now mock at her who freed them vestermorn
And hail November, come to see her. die.
November, glowing in her glad attire,
Young summer’s mistress, with love-loosen’d
hair,
Shows no soft shyness; her assertive charms
She proffers freely to her lover’s
Drunken with youth and warm with perfum'd
air
She yields herself and wantons in his arms.
NOVEMBER.
By NINA MURDOCH.
Swift have cicadas wakened through the
land,
With endless thridding; every mangrove
creek ,
Teems with a myriad ctabs, that ravenous
seek
To stay their hunger in the oozy sand.
While shrill mosquitoes in a noisy band
Hanging above the waterholes bespeak
The Summer time; and countless parrots
streak
The scrub with vivid red on either hatid.
So, now her offspring spaivned, and harvests
done,
Nothing more splendid can the year attain ,
And, resting for a tittle space, she lies
Stretched at full length beneath the scorch-
ing sun,
With flanks still heaving from her travail
pain,
And calm of great achievement in her eyes.
The Australian Woman
We learn, with gratitude, from Jessie
Ackermann’s Australia from a Woman’s
Point of View (Cassell, per Dymock’s
Library) that Kenneth Mackay is Aus-
tralia’s National Poet. We learn other
things: “Australians are mere imitators.
They are positively unable to originate.
Everything is a copy, with some small
alteration, usually a disadvantage to
the subject.” And : “It cannot be said that
there is yet a generation of Australians
who bear the imprint of Australian atmo-
sphere.” Yet the author makes many-
sane and penetrating observations. The
Church comes in for vigorous criticism
—its callous neglect of the backblocks in
favor of our brown and mottled brother
overseas, its inadequate financial provision
for the ministry, its aloofness from modern
social problems, the neglect of the vote by
the Church woman. And she speaks a vig-
orous word for the parson's wife in the back
country. “I would rather be a doormat in
the house of a.> political foe than a minister’s
wife in Australia. In the home she is first
of all wife, Which usually means mother of
an abnormally large flock, for which she is
housekeeper, charwoman, cook, dressmaker
and general factotum, to say nothing of
scavenger-at-large for the Church, where she
becomes the receptacle ,of gossip, scandal
find all other deadly moral germs which
would corrupt a statue, but from which she
cannot flee, because she has no rights which
anyone is bound to respect, She is the
preacher’s wife, the unoaid curate of the
Church, and the target of the whole com-
munity. Her home is an inn for all mem-
bers, open day and night, and meals, especi-
ally teas, at all hours. The only difference
between a preacher’s house and a properly-
constituted inn is that no profits are de-
rived from a bar. The first duty of the
women of a country parish is to form them-
selves into a Humane Society—a society for
the prevention of cruelty—to preachers’
wives.”
The author finds that in Australia wives
and daughters systematically and success-
fully manage their husbands and fathers,
while the men seem sublimely unconscious
of the fact. But Australian women are
“royal mothers,” though mothers of all
classes are overworked. Australian children
are “sadly deficient in the personal charm
of pleasing manners,” are very excitable by
temperament, and extremely noisy. But in
time “the romp wears off,” and there is de-
veloped a grace of manner. Commenting on
the fact of the extremely small percentage
of Australian boys that were rejected as
unfit for training under our Defence regula-
tions, Miss Ackermann justly says: “It is
very doubtful if any other country could
clear so large a percentage of physically
sound lads between the of 13 and
14 years. There seems little danger of the
decay of the race in Australia.” Also, “the
women of Australia are. so delightful, and so
full of common-sense in most things, one
never ceases to admire them.”
Jt
“The great difficulty is the lack' of a
national (woman) leader who could carry
the women out of the dense ‘anti’ atmosphere,
and unite them, regardless of party or re-
ligion, in the bonds of a common cause, to
secure just and honest laws, fat women and
children. Strange to say, among the many
gifted women there is not one with the genius
of organisation and the ability of generalship
in the entire Commonwealth who could
direct the women of the nation —not one!”
The au!''or fhsls remuneration to shop
and factory g is criminally low —but does
not compare • r rates of nay with those in
other eoUntr: s. Alts! it. ha. she says, pro-
bably is the working my,. y n-radise, but it
is far from a celestial on-A ~n for working
and business girls. “I' m -.e.stic occupation is
the only well paid occupation for women.”
The real Australian girl, we find to our
surprise, has not yet arrived. Speaking
generally, however, she is a “really delight-
ful creature.” But though there is a lot
of common sense about her, she has never
applied it to originating dress suited to the
climate and setting of the country. She is
the freest girl in the world, yet she allows
her wedding gifts to be displayed to the
wedding guests, and throngs Government
House. She can cook, play games and sew,
but she is not s) udious. She has a marvel-
lous genius for organisation, she is not self-
ish. and she realises that this is Her Chance.
The world will hear much of her in years
to come : she will make history. But among
shop-girls “a larger degree of ‘class distinc-
tion’ is found than that which exists be-
tween those sections of society termed
‘classes and massed’ No one would suppose
that a shop-girl, or a set of shop-girls, would
take their social status from the class of
people, the social rank, of the customers
dealing with the houses where they are em-
ployed. Yet such is the case.” As one
said, “You know, we never mix socially with
the girls from other shops.” And the vanity
of dress is the besetting sin of the Aus-
tralian girl—and, let us. add, of every other
girl.
Finally, struck by the fact that Aus-
tralia is the largest importer, in proportion
to its population, of any country in the
world, Jessie Ackermann points the Bulle-
tin moral: “Australia, according to its
population, is the largest consumer of
sweated slave labor in the world. I say
slave, because, if workers are obliged to sub-
mit to sweating wages in order to keep on
earth—that is slavery.”
Jessie Ackermann writes sloppily, but her
outlook is that of an intelligent democrat,
and her sympathies are predominantly Aus-
tralian. It is a pity, however, that she did
not confine her studies to her special subject
—the Australian Woman..
A Statistician’s Stanzas
George Handley Knibbs, Federal Statis-
tician, is an author of whom lately we have
despaired. Each year he courageously issues
a big volume, The Commonwealth Year
Book; but both in matter and style there
seems no annual improvement in his work.
The latest Year Book actually repeats much
of the contents of the previous issue; and
there is a noticeable lack of inspiration in
many of his prose poems. He treats of a
wide range of subjects, but in a matter-of-
fact way: his literary style has been evi-
dently founded on that of Euclid. Some of
his effusions are no more exalted than a
grocer’s bill or a bank-book. However, in
his new volume, Voices of the North and
Echoes of Hellas (Alston, Rivers, 5s.), he
gives evidences of improvement. Thus,
turning from such dull subjects as birth-
rates and trade, he scientifically investigates
the alluring origin of the kiss.
What subtle sprite or happy wight,
Or phantom limp or joyful imp,
Taught us to kiss?
Or was it naught but happy thought,
Some flash of light from Heaven bright,
Where all is bliss?
Did Adam know or Eve bestow
In Paradise, ere man was wise,
Such bliss as this?
Or was it just—I think it must
Have chance; a lightning glance
Of eye, then kiss?
Must we Relieve that first ’twas Eve
Who taught that lips, in Love’s eclipse,
Must join their bliss?
That sweet caress must ever bless?
That Heaven lies in Love’s surprise,
A true-heart kiss ?....
Perhaps.... but it is Knibbs’s business to
know. The Commonwealth Statistician has
all the statistics at hand; yet he fails to
present us with: (1) a comparative table of
the relative frequency of kisses in (1) Aus-
tralia, and (2) other countries: he does not
give the percentage of kisses to the density of
population in different countries, nor even a
graph showing clearly the influence of kisses
upon (1) marriages. (2) divorces, (3) the
birth-rate; he draws no conclusions from a
comparison of the numbers of kisses given by
average lovers (1) in daylight, and (2) in
darkness: he does not even record all the
known varieties of kisses, or their relative
frequency eacli decade. Instead of these,
this statistician gives us merely stanzas, and
where we look for conclusions he hazards
suppositions. There is not even an appen-
dix wherein we might.pursue this subject our-
selves. Similarly his treatment of another
interesting subject is merely conjectural:—
Do you remember, dearest,
That pight beneath the stars;
When heart to heart was nearest;
When naught was there that mars;
When glow of all existence
Seemed living in our veins;
When dear Love’s mad insistence
Threw to the winds the reins
That guide our poor, frail beings
O’er Life’s strange roads and ways?. ..
Jt
However. Knibbs is a poet. In his hours
of relaxation his hobby leads him to the pro-
duction of sad statistics: innumerable vol-
umes of Year Books testify to his misan-
thropic and mistaken industry. But this
big volume Comprises free translations and
paraphrases of Finnish poetry, a senes
of “original poems based upon a num-
ber of the most beautiful of the folk
songs of Finland,” , a free translation
from the German of Ernst Ihnkp’s
poem, Eros and Psyche , and—stranee
conjunction !—echoes from the Greek. “i t ; s
for the reader to say how far the spirit of
the volume is Hellenic, modified by that more
profound insight into the hopelessness of un-
alloyed Hedonism which characterises phi]
osophy to-day, and transformed by that
enlarged conception of Time and Space which
constitutes one of the most splendid heritages
of modern thought.” And, despite the poet’s
elaborate definition, there are several of the
poems in his “Songs of Death” that are more
interesting than the exquisite and poig-
nant tables of death-rates that cast
a gentle and refined melancholy over
so many of the most beautiful pages
of The Commonwealth Year Book. “The
Statue of Memnon,” the “Ode to
Night,” and “The Phantom Universe” con-
tain many stanzas worthy of inclusion in
Knibbs's forthcoming yearly volume. Stanzas
such as these illumine work that is talented
and painstaking, but seldom inspired.
When pall of night o'erspreads this hapless
earthly sphere,
With awe we look on high at blaming suns,
and feel
That tee behold but motes in tether-ocean,
here,
In our own little realm of spatial deeps.
We lcnecl
Overcome, bewildered, and with touch of
things immense
We seem to see that Life and Hope and
Thought are naught;
That underneath the sun, in Web of Maya
caught,
The greatest things are those of Space and
Time and Sense. ...
Will Soul from Worlds on Worlds then ever
see
Some wondrous glowing Sun, whose
brighter beams
Shall wake within it richer melody?
And shall at last there flow from it, like
streams
Of Music, some diviner flood of Sound
That makes the newer world with Joy
abound?
Ah Hope! Art thou then only a trickster
strange,
And but a mocking sprite that speaks in
rain?
With all the (eons witness naught but
Change,
That changeless spurns the Soul with high
disdain?
(treat Soul! Why hidest thou from us the
Truth,
That could endow our broken souls with
Youth?
All this is hopeful. It is evident that
Knibbs can let himself go if he likes. If he
will dismount from his tiresome hobby, and
put into his statistics some “Voices of the
North” and an assorted lot of “Echoes of
Hellas,” the forthcoming Commonwealth
Year Book will be something more than a
depressing scrap-heap of statistics. Let
Knibbs but trust to his inspiration, let him
free himself from the carking shackles of
mathematics, and he will give us a Year
Book that will proudly lie beneath many a
flapper’s sentimental pillow, that will be
read and re-read, with tears, by many a
Paolo and Francesca, “A book of Knibbs’s
underneath the bough,” a book of statistics
that lie too deep for tears.

The practised hand of Ambrose Pratt
makes Wolaroi’s Cup (N.S.W. Bookstall
Co.) . an excellent racing yarn —infinitely
better than the usual Nat Gould. Sodger
Sandy’s Bairn, in the same series, by Alex.
Bathgate, is a forceful narrative of the early
gold-diggings of Otago, mixed with the usual
love interest and a considerable amount
of Scotch.
For the Term of His Natural Life, Marcus
Clarke’s famous book, is now obtainable in a
large-type shilling edition. The publishers,
Ward Lock, Melb., have made arrangements
with i\lrs. Marcus Clarke, to whom royalties
are paid under the provisions of the new
Copyright Act of 1911.
Designed mainly to “place in the hands of
everyone interested a handy little volume,
which can readily be referred to when ques-
tions arise in connection with the consign-
ment, sale, or shipment of wool,” A Clip of
Wool from Shearing Shed to Ship, by Alex.
Sinclair (Wm. Brooks, Sydney), turns out
to be. for the layman, a fascinating volume
of information upon a highly interesting sub-
ject.-
The Reminiscences of Detective-Inspector
Christie, related by J. B. Castieau (George
Robertson, Melb.), are interesting. Christies
career was a varied and strenuous one; he
was successively a leading Victorian detec-
tive. a “shadow” to royal personages —a per-
sonage is a person whose job. is a bigger
thing than himself —a champion oarsman
and boxer of Victoria, and a detective-in-
spector of Customs during a specially inter-
esting period. The volume fills its place in
this firm’s useful “Told-by-the-Pioneers
series.

Time was when disease was thought to be due to the
direct influence of evil spirits, and exorcism and magic
were invoked to cast it out.
Science has taught us wisdom. The evil spirits exist
still. We call them “Disease Germs,” and they also
must be cast out. Once lodged in the stomach or
intestines, fever with its hallucinations, or biliousness with
its aches and pains, is the result.
ENO S ‘FRUIT SALT’
is the approved remedy for driving out disease germs.
Its action is quick and thorough. It clears the intestines,
rouses the torpid liver to new life, stimulates the mucous
membrane to a healthy action, and cleanses and invigorates
the whole digestive tract.
Be prepared for emergencies by always keeping a
bottle in the house.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Rectification.
Not always now—within his times —
The true man’s worth is weighed,
But what of that? His flame shall soar
When lesser glories fade.
I know a House not built of stone,
Nor yet of airy dream,
Six portals front the sunlit seas,
The stars upon it gleam.
Not yet complete—its turrets rise,
Star-seeking, year by year;
Pray God its walls shall stand the shock
When foemen draw a-near!
I know who laid its basic stones,
r Who drew its vast design,
Who gave it shape and massiveness,
And curve and gracious line.
Yet. some were paid in fuller meed
Than Justice would allot;
And some who toiled by day and night
Are known and mentioned not.
The flaunted name, the roselit pose
Have ta’en the times in thrall;
For them a witless age designs
A wreath and pedestal.
A wreath, a pedestal, a place
Above the nameless horde!
O witless Age, O fickle Age,
Not yours the true award!
The Court shall sit when we are dust.
And try each name anew;
The Future on the Judgment seat
Shall render each his due.
What each has earned he shall be paid
In righteous years anon;
And some shall find conspicuous peaks,
And some oblivion.
And some who wrought to build the House,
Wherein we breathe and move,
Shall win a nation’s high esteem,
And find a people’s love.
N.s.w.
RODERIC QUINN.
Way-Worn.
Give me to sleep, O mother Night,
For I am weary of the day,
The broken idols of delight,
The little gods with feet of clay.
Give me to sleep, O mother Night,
For I am weary of the day !
O mother Night, give me to sleep,
Nor vex my sleep with any dream ;
Beyond the blackness soft and deep
The false, alluring waters gleam.
O mother Night, give me to sleep,
Nor vex my sleep with any dream !
The children toiled, a merry band,
And then there came a wind at play
And found their cities on the sand
And flung them to the far-away.
The children toiled, a merry band,
And then.... there came a wind at play.
The roses and the rue are dust,
On winds of all the yesterdays;
The sword is bitten through with rust,
There’s blood upon the trampled bays.
The roses and the rue are dust
On winds of all the yesterdays.
I have no common boon to crave !
There is no boon that I would take
With tyrant or with driven slaves,
With those that build or those that brer,'
I have no common boon to crave,
There is no boon that I would take.
But give me sleep, O mother Night,
And I am richer far than they
Who follow phantoms of delight.
Or win the wreath of tattered bay.
Yea, give me sleep, O mother Night,
And I am richer far than they!
victoria.
MARIE E. J. PITT.
The Saving Tuft.
On burning India’s torrid strand
The Lalas live —a goodly caste
Whose pious works on every hand
Endear them all to first and last;
Ye know them by the badge they bear,
A single tuft of rope-like hair.
And when the Lala leaves this earth
And makes for Gates of Pearl and Gold,
The god who guardeth him from birth
The duty hath of taking hold
And hauling him in half a trice
Across the bridge to Paradise.
Alas, how vain are human schemes!
Our mightiest fabric is but frail,
Our finest plans dissolve in dreams,
Our deepest craft will often fail;
And so the Gala’s tuft of hair
Is oft the Lala’s deadly snare;
For fiends there be who watch and wait
Beside the bridge the soul must cross
Before it reach the Heavenly Gate
Beyond the deep, dividing fosse ;
And when the Lala lifts his head
A fiend will grip the tuft, instead,
Of him whom earthly sacrifice
And pious life had daily pleased ;
And straight away from Paradise
Will tear the saint whom he hath seized
And bear him off and thrust him far
Where evil men in torment are.
And so it is in Life to-day:
We coddle up our little soul
And keep it in the narrow way
And head it for the shining goal;
To find at last, when all is told.
Our Saving Tuft the Demons-hold !
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-
Six Shillings Sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable. acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 18, 1913.
CONGESTION AND THE COST OF
LIVING.
In every big Australian city, and more par-
ticularly in Sydney and Melbourne, the wail
of the frequent victim of the increased cost
of living is to be heard. Royal Commissions
have been appointed to inquire into it;
party politicians have promised with vague
expansiveness that if they are returned to
power it will become a horror of the past;
the Laborites claim that it is all due to the
Trusts ; the Trusts allege that it is the result
of the wage-avarice of the toiling classes;
and meanwhile nothing is done and every-
one is dissatisfied. The would-be reformer’s
main difficulty, as is the case with so many
other abuses, is to unearth the basic cause
of the trouble. No doubt the Trust evil is
partially to blame. The Royal Commissioner
who was appointed by the N. S. Wales
Government to inquire into the local food-
supply question found that all sorts of
primary industries were affected by vends
and “gentlemen’s agreements,” to the finan-
cial detriment of the consumer. A millers’
combine, for example, artificially regulates
the price of bran, pollard and “shorts” in
N. S. Wales. The effect is to make the
running of dairy-farms, poultry-ranches and
the like more costly, with the result that
the public has to pay more than it should
for its milk, butter, poultry and eggs. New
combines of the sort are constantly being
formed in other industries; on the other
hand, old ones burst from time to time. The
indigenous Trusts, unlike the vast and all-
powerful American concerns, are mostly too
small for the present to do much public
damage. This accounts probably for the
fact that Government Statistician Knibbs
was able to show recently that the actual
necessaries of life (which, with rent and
luxuries, represent the essential factors on
which the cost of living depends) have gone
up in price only to a trifling extent from
year to year. Food and groceries which cost
50s. in 1903 in Sydney would, he showed,
cost 535. Id. in the third quarter of this
year, 1913; and in Melbourne, instead of
the increase being 6 per cent., as in Sydney,
it is only a trifle more than 3 per cent. Last
year foodstuffs were extremely dear; this
year, as a Sydney daily pointed out joyfully
a day or so back, the Christmas dinner is
going to be comparatively cheap. And so
the tide ebbs and flows.
* * * *
The luxuries factor is necessarily a diffi-
cult thing to dogmatise about. But some
features of it are obvious. In N. S. Wales
the Holman Government’s policy of lavish
borrowing; the continued good seasons; and
the arrival of the motor-car on a big scale
—an occurrence that for some mysterious
reason always causes temporary financial
recklessness in the country affected —have
all combined to produce an era of extrava-
gance. The same remark applies more or
less to the other States. People own
expensive limousines or touring cars or
both who, a decade ago, would have thought
twice before investing in a one-horse
brougham. Every other cottage home con-
tains its £4O piano. But Australia is boom-
ing at the moment. Presently will come
the inevitable slump, and the first items in
the increased cost of the national household
accounts to get it in the neck will be those
which come under the heading “Luxuries.”
This feature of the problem may, therefore,
if only because of its inherent transitoriness,
be dismissed as hardly worth analysis. There
remains the factor of rent, the offspring of
Congestion, and this is at the bottom of the
increased cost of living, everywhere, but
more especially in the big cities.
While the price of necessaries has moved
up from 5 to 8 per cent, in Sydney, official
figures show that the rent of four- to six-
roomed cottages has leaped from 30 to
40 per cent. And these figures apply in a
general way also to Melbourne. The Wages
Boards are unanimous on the point that the
necessity for those increased wages which
so madden the conservative employer is due
chiefly to the raised rents of the cottages—
owned in many cases by the maddened em-
ployer himself —in which the employee is
compelled to live or exist. The rent-increase
is not confined to the dwellings of the poor-
est class, though it is most noticeable, for
the reason that it is most exorbitant, there.
As a matter of fact, it is pretty
well universal. Shops, offices, factories, all
cost far more to inhabit than they did 10 or
even five years ago. The traders, profes-
sional men, etc., who occupy these places
naturally extract from the suffering public,
who deal with them, the extra sums which
they are called on to hand to the landlord
week by week. In the final upshot the
worker discovers to his despair that he is
little better off, in a material sense, than he
was before he achieved an increased wage;
while the wretched citizen who toils on a
fixed salary is infinitely worse off. The
public at large looks at its steadily swelling
bills in a stupefied way, and curses feebly.
But that, under the party system, is about
all the public ever does do, or is able to do.
In this paper’s opinion, the Australian
people, and especially the people of N. S.
Wales and Victoria, will continue to curse
in a futile manner just so long as
they are unable to induce their State
Governments, Labor or Liberal, to desist
from pandering to a handful of city land-
lords by deliberately bloating Sydney and
Melbourne at the expense of the potential
cities on the at present undeveloped harbors.
* # * *
The crowning and horrible example in the
way of city congestion is afforded, of course,
by Sydney. In existing circumstances
the whole of the wheat, wool, hides, carcases
and dairy produce of N. S. Wales —in brief,
every blessed thing that the eastern three-
fourths of the State grows or makes or digs
out of the earth —must come to Port Jack-
son for shipment. On its shores is a super-
fluous army numbering many thousands —the
workers and others who are engaged in hand-
ling these products, most of which should
never be seen within many miles of Sydney.
It is the presence of this army that is send-
ing up rents by leaps and bounds, and the
more Sydney is bloated by the introduction
of fresh workers (a matter merely of in-
creasing prosperity on the part of the State)
the more intolerable will grow the local rent
problem, and the more the cost of living
will bound up in consequence, not merely
as regards Sydney but as regards the whole
of the State. The insanity or the dishonesty
or both of successive N. S. Wales Adminis-
trations in this respect is almost incredible,
if only for the reason that the excuse for
the bloating of the harbor-side city is so
remarkably poor. It is bounded on the one
side by the sea and on all the others by at
least 100 miles of country in which practic-
ally nothing is produced—country which is to'
all intents and purposes, dead. There is
no sheep or cattle or wheat country
nearer to Sydney than Lithgow on the
Western line, or Goulburn on the Southern
line, or Newcastle on the Northern line.
Whoever in N. S. Wales has beasts or sheep
or wheat to sell has therefore to
pay, at the very least, for 100 miles of need-
less haulage. If Port Stephens were opened
no to the export trade, the producers of the
North would benefit incalculably, inasmuch
as they would save hundreds of thousands
annually in freight charges, while their pro-
perty would arrive at its destination in con-
siderably better order. And the same remark
applies to the dwellers on the hinterland of
Jervis Bay, Western Port and elsewhere.
* * * *
A trifling expenditure on the part of the
States concerned would bring about the
establishment of meal works on the shores of
several of their ports. Wool sales
would start at these places, just as they
have already begun in Tasmania —a possi-
bility that was scoffed at loudly a year or
so ago by folks who argued that, because
Tasmanian wool had been sold for many
years in Melbourne, it would necessarily be
sold there always. Practically all the
workers who are now employed in Sydney
and Melbourne in connection with the frozen
meat industry, many of those who are en-
gaged in the wool trade, and thousands of
others associated with the bulk handling of
goods would, with their wives and families,
shift to the newly-opened ports, and there
form the nucleus of fresh cities. The capi-
talist would stand to gain, inasmuch as he
would have less freight to pay; in fact, in
many cases he could drove his sheep or
cattle to, and personally deliver his wheat at
the port of departure—economies that are now
the wildest of impossibilities. The workers
in the new towns and cities would gain to
the extent of being able readily to acquire
good cottages at 50 per cent, less than they
are required at present to pay for bad ones.
The people whose businesses legitimately de-
mand that they shall reside in or near Syd-
ney would find that, owing to the easing up
°f the rent problem, they were once more
able to get shelter at a rational figure. The
artificially high prices charged at present by
those who, because of city congestion, are
obliged to pay absurdly high rents, would
automatically fall. A part, at least, of the
problem of the increased cost of living in
Australia would be solved. And the only
sufferers would be an insignificant coterie of
Sydney and Melbourne landlords whose woes
are not worth considering at the price.
THE JONAH OF BRITISH CAPITAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the mayor, and the equally small
Liberal politician, and the little Prime
Minister of the Joseph Cook size rave of
the necessity for the introduction of British
capital to Australia as part of the scheme of
making Australia for ever one with what
they call “the British Umpiah,” I am moved
to request that the British capital shall at
least be expended by Australians or by Eng-
lish of such long Australian experience that
they have ceased to be English, so that Aus-
tralian institutions and "Socialistic legis-
lation” shall not unjustly receive the blame
for the failure which is mostly inevitable.
As a rule, in these days, when British Capi-
tal sends to this country the Britisher with
swollen notions as an administrator, that
capital becomes as lost as the sound of a
great Amen. All the empty pretensions of
the Britisher puff him out to bursting ; yet
therein he merely follows the lunatic example
set by the average British flotation. The
overloaded proposition, ballooned with hot
air and water, is the material London sup-
plies for maladministration by the British
manager. One Australian mine, which had
not paid on the basis of £lO,OOO working
capital and £27,000 debentures, was bought
at the receiver’s sale for £l2OO and promptly
refloated in London for £300,000. The 20s.
stock was as promptly bucked to 325. 6d. —
and now stands at lOd. These lunatic pro-
positions gauge the value of a manager, not
by ability or experience, but by the size of
the salary he contrives to get by a very
courteous confidence trick —so polite that it
must come from either Oxford or Cam-
bridge. Sometimes in addition to salary the
fool-directors pay him a commission of 5 per
cent, on all the capital he spends, so that
the more expensive he makes railway-build-
ing, mills, furnaces and other reduction
works, and the higher wages he pays, the
more increment to himself. The fool-directors
never stop to consider that with these methods
it would pay a crooked manager to foment a
strike, and then raise wages to heal the
strike; to take railway works by devious
routes; to buy engines by the score and un-
necessary picks and shovels by the thousand,
and, generally, to throw away the good old
capital of the British Umpiah with both
hands and a tank scoop.
Where the commission is not directly paid,
the imported manager has been known to
take it direct from the supplier, so that on
some of these English mines, on a still day
with the wind in the right quarter, you may
hear the exhausts of unnecessarily-segregated
steam plants puffing “Comeeshun! Comee-
shun !” and shovels with the manager’s draw-
back on the invoice screeching “C’m’ish’n!”
as they scrape over the flat-sheets. In the
days of the imported managements was there
no commission on the millions pounds’ worth
of unnecessary plant now scrapped at Broken
Hill? And as their methods were so they
are.
It is a vital question to the Australian
mine or other concern which must go out-
side of Australia for its capital; and Aus-
tralia, like the U.S.A., despite its marvellous
internal development under High Protec-
tion, must continue to be a debtor nation;
the more so while Australia continues to
send out its gold to pay for manufactures
which it could make within itself, while it
continues to export raw wool only instead of
raw wool and some tweed, and wheat instead
of wheat and some flour, and zinc con-
centrates while importing spelter, and cop-
per ingots while importing manufactured
metal. A foreigner’s mining failure in Aus-
tralia is never charged to the foreigner, but
to the country that had to suffer him.
Maladministration; rigging and misuse of
working capital which, with Australian
shareholders, would probably lead a man to
the dock; plain perjuries in prospectus re-
ports ; hot air and water added to the propo-
sition to give the shadow of a reason for
the plunder; a stupid, overpaid and often
carelessly corrupt directorate, with a fat-
headed management to cap all —these are the
rewards the Australian mostly gets for find-
ing and developing a mine which his fool-
ishly small idea of himself makes him
believe is too big for anything but imported
capital. The imported capital, like the
camel, is necessary in Australia; the
imported management—management in the
hands of men of a country which never had
a metal mine worth speaking of- —is in almost
every case as unnecessary as the Afghan
camel driver.
The case of the Commonwealth Oil Cor-
poration is one of the most glaring instances
of stupidity and mismanagement; and nothing
worse is insinuated, although, as a million
and a half of money has disappeared hv
reason of it, nothing worse than stunidifv
and mismanagement can be imagined fi,;
American oil-engineers—who know more 7,1
oil-mining than any other people—been con
suited, they would have advised the expendi-
ture of £50,000 or so to prove by boring the
oil which is always assumed in the United
States to be below the oil shales. But
that wouldn’t do for the hot-air merchant,
of London, and boring with £50,000 doei
not provide a sufficiently large basis for a
Great Steal. So an unnecessary break-neck
railway drops off a plateau into a gorge and
ends in a cul-de-sac 2000 feet deep, and on
the wrong side of the mountain for cheat)
mining and for the winning of the best
shale; and in the same cul-de-sac another
half-million goes in plant—some proved
worthless and some now worth nothing
through idleness and exposure—and in a fine
smoke-stack which the eagle of the mountain
may build her nest in, though she may dis-
cover that her egg has dropped into a pit
as bottomless as the management provided
for the shareholders’ capital.
And the C.O.C. is only one. Australia is
dotted with them —furnaces that blew in only
to blow out; and mills of 80-head
which replaced 50-head that had crushed
a million tons, ,and shut down after
running a month on mullock from an
open cut, though there were and are
20.000 tons of lidwt. ore standing above the
200 ft. level and a mine virgin below 200 ft
Certain the 50 discarded stamps were com-
mon Australian stamps, and were sold for
scrap ; but they crushed ore for 40 years at
a profit, and the new 60 head ran on mullock
and left lldwt. ore to be submerged. As the
60-head mill is a good new mill, it cannot
be charged with shutting the mine down, so
the fault was with the imported manage-
ment. No wonder my Corsican guide at
Calvi called the abandoned Argentella mine
Caprice d'lnglesi— the madness of the Eng-
lish.
There is no excuse for these imported man-
agements. Australia has all minerals and some
world-beater examples of most of them—
Broken Hill, Bischoff, Mount Morgan, Mount
Lyell, Kalgoorlie. And from these unique
examples have grown experience and practice
which cannot be bettered. The glib Eng-
lishman copies the British trick of Kipling
in literature — Foster Fraser is a cheaper
exponent of it in his very low-grade journal-
ism :
He cleans his nails over Brisbane
And he sneezes three times at Perth,
And then lie goes home on the English mail
And lies of the big, wide earth.
A week in Johannesberg makes him an
authority on gold mining sufficiently high to
secure in London the job of making an ass
of himself on an Australian mine at vast
expense to his company ; and presumably 10
days at Kalgoorlie would get him a manage-
ment in Rhodesia. We have even had
prattlers in geology from Kensington who
knew the petrology of the Thames Embank-
ment from Chelsea to Blackfriars, but are so
madly optimistic, or so ignorant of arithme-
tic, that in a shaft 4ft. wide and 25ft. deep
they can find the outcropping of a lode 14ft.
wide. Marvellous is the Englisher and his
fat and cunning head. More marvellous
indeed the simple Englishers who trust him.
But the Australian is very simple, too.
In the middle of this year an Australian min-
ing co. was formed. It had a working capital
of less than £20,000, and at the behest of
certain alleged underwriters in London (who
afterwards got so far under that their writ-
ing became as invisible as their money, thus
throwing the allegedly underwritten shares
back on the Australian syndicate) it
appointed a very young and inexperienced
but quite courteous and softly-spoken gentle-
man as manager at £2OOO per year—or more
than 10 per cent, of the total working capital.
The English youth came to Australia, and
went very softly to the mine, and worked
there so softly that not a ton of ore was
shifted during his term of 12 months, though
there was plenty of ore forthcoming, dress-
able to over 50 per cent, copper, to eke out
the working capital. A small rock-drill
plant stayed at the railhead for nine months
—though the comparative unreliability of the
labor available on the mine made it impera-
tive to treat that compressor plant as freight
to be rushed.
And some day an Australian manager will
find something peculiarly English in the wild
hills behind Cloncurry. A mine couldn’t ship
ore because it couldn’t get transport; but.
its English management could find transport
for manv drayloads of organic earth from
“antbeds”—the said antbed being just the
thing for making a tennis court! _
How much longer are we to take seriously
in Australia the stupidest representatives or
the people whose best brains cannot control
a hundred or two of squealing suffragettes.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Folly of Local Option.
The expensive folly of local option was made
clear by the result of the poll taken in N. •
Wales a week ago. On the figures counte
at time of writing, reduction has been
carried in about 16 electorates, mostly in
the outer suburbs of Sydney. In the c
reduction has been carried nowhere,
upshot will be, as it has always been, me y
to inconvenience a few people. It will
reduce the consumption of drink in
slightest. Residents in the neighborhood oi
the hotels to be closed will get their supplies
elsewhere; boarders will move on; the 00
who sits in the corner and mops up P 1
every night will find another corner tur
down the street and continue to mop up
the old enthusiasm ; the citizen who, return
ing home in the evening, was accustom • ,
take his sherry and bitters at the quiet i
pub in his own suburb, will turn into a i
hotel instead, and meet half a dozen fri ,’
and have half a dozen drinks he do
want, and miss his boat or train and a
home at 8 p.m. to find an embittered
with a spoiled dinner on her hands. » ’
it will please the Dry Sheep, whose
nounced policy is not to prevent dm *
but to down the publican. In Mo®? ’
where there are two well-conducted no •
“reduction” has been carried; in Darims
Harbor, where there are 87 more or ~
superfluous drinking “joints,” “continuan


has been decided upon. In Ashfield, where
there are five hotels, widely scattered the
reduction foolery will lop off one or two - in
Beimore, with about the same population,
the 89 pubs will jostle each other’s back
yards as usual. On every occasion the
result of the reduction issue is the same
The deep-thinking S. M. Herald advances
the theory that if reduction is carried on
long enough there will be no hotels left, and
after complaining that the operation does
not take place where it is really needed, says
there is no escape from the “ridiculous de-
velopment.” Well, since 1907 Victoria’s
Licenses’ Reduction Board has closed just
about twice as many public houses as have
been closed in N. S. Wales under local
option.. And the job has been done with
discrimination. By far the greater portion
of the Board’s closing work has been done in
districts where a large excess existed. To
the end of 1912 (and the policy is still
being maintained) the hotels put out of
business were situated as follows:
In districts more than 2 to 1 overstocked. . 468
In districts less than 2 to 1 overstocked.. 146
Total . . .. . . . 614
In 1912, 23 pubs were rubbed out in the
metropolitan area, 29 in the urban dis-
tricts, and 50 in the countrv. Since the
Board got to work in 1907 40 per cent, of the
413 hotels reckoned to be in excess in the
city and suburbs have been delicensed, 44
per cent, of the 344 in the urban districts
and 48 per cent, of the 620 in the countrv.
The admirable results of the system
were testified to last March by A. J. Derrick,
who had then been secretary of the Central
Mission in Melbourne for about 20 years.
The Argus reported him as saying, at
one sitting of the Board :
In 20 years under local option no hotels in the
slums were closed. I doubt if we had local option
without compensation whether we could close any in
that quarter. The Board, by closing so many hotels
in this neighborhood [Gipps Ward], has done a great
deal of good and helped our work considerably.
It is true that Victoria intends to go back
to local option. The short-sighted theorists
clamored for it and the party politicians,
seeing Votes, yielded. But it is a retrograde
step. Next to State or municipal control
of the system, it is infinitely the most sen-
sible solution offered —infinitely more sen-
sible and vastly more reformative than local
option. And the fact that the S. M. Herald
has never heard of it doesn’t reduce its value
by tuppence.

Round Pegs in Square Holes.
There is bitter irony in these few words
of recent Victorian news : “Mr. J. Lemmon,
Minister for Labor and Education.” Lem-
mon, representative of Williamstown, came
into Parliament charged with brains, zeal
and ambition ; he was the bright boy of the
Labor Party. He can compare with Watt
for tenacity of purpose, and compare well.
Besides being born to the political game,
Lemmon is a student, and he set himself to
make two subjects specially his own—educa-
tion (general and technical), and the organi-
sation of labor and employment, particularly
in apprenticeship. He was one of the first
politicians in Australia to see how closely
these two departments should be related in
a well-ordered State. He became a real,
blown-in-the-glass expert in both. Few of
the top-notch “educationists” in Victoria
can teach him much about the systematising
of education, and his work on the Apprentice-
ship Commission was masterly. It is at least
10 years since Victoria had a Minister of
Education who knew the rudiments of his
job, and for most of those 10 years Lemmon
has had knowledge which would make him a
real star Minister. And here he is, enjoying
the empty title for a few days, to be kicked
out for some dunderhead because the Party
System orders it. The Labor party has never
had a majority in Victoria. It looks as if
it won’t in the next 10 years, anyway. For
that reason the man who is the pick of Par-
liament for a department must stay out of
the job for 20 years. How putrid is party
administration !
The Awful North.
The Stewart lectures, delivered recently in
Melbourne by Dr. Breinl, Director of the
Australian Institute of Tropical Medicine at
Townsville, represent real, meaty stuff. In
the past the physical characteristics of the
Northern-born have been wrangled over by
dozens of doctors and hundreds of politi-
cians, with little more than prejudice and
casual observations on which to base all the
talk; and a wild lot of opinions have been
shouted into the ear of Australia as a re-
sult. Dr. Nisbet has described the North-
ern-born as hopeless weeds tainted from
birth by the fell climate, and has called his 25
years of Northern experience as proof that
he knows what he is talking about. On the
other hand, MacDonald, also with 25 and
more years of medical experience in the
North, maintains that the Northern-born
are equal in physique and intelligence to
any race in the world. It has remained
for Breinl to tackle the subject by exact
methods of systematic research. The chief
factors affecting a white race in the tropics
are those of climate, surroundings, and in-
fection by disease. Climate is a matter of
sunlight, heat and humidity; surroundings
include such things as housing, food and
drinking habits ; and infection depends on the
presence or absence of certain disease-pro-
ducing parasites which are apt to flourish in
any part of the tropics, if once introduced.
Until the recent development of tropical
medicine revealed the truth, most of the
more serious health-dangers of the tropics
were ascribed to climate; but nowadays tney
are known to be due to minute parasites
which can be readily dealt with by suitable
preventive measures. The summary fashion
in which the Americans rooted yellow fever
out of Havana and the Canal Zone, and the
way that malaria has been practically abo-
lished at Ivlang and Port Swettenhani. in
the Malay States, are instances of this. Such
diseases as ankylostomiasis (hookworm dis-
ease) and malaria are deadly influences on
young children. Even if they do not kill,
the child is often stunted, mentally and phy-
sically, Adults also degenerate under
their baneful influence. In these and similar
cases infection is not “bred” by local condi-
tions, but is spread from person to person.
The colored brother is the great reservoir of
infection for the white man, and to the
absence from Northern Australia of this
dusky relative of Brug? Smith, Breinl
ascribes the country’s freedom from tropical
disease of the more serious kinds. Climate
and surroundings are secondary factors, but
so much has been ascribed to them in the
past that they are receiving close attention.
The Americans in Manila have carried on
extensive investigations on the chemical rays
in tropical sunlight, and similar observations
have been made in Queensland. The results
show a somewhat higher reading for Queens-
land than for Manila, and indicate that
these rays have little or no effect on health
comfort. The most interesting part
of Breinl’s investigations dealt with the
blood and general body condition of over
250 Northern-born children, many of whom
were of the second or even third generation.
No appreciable difference was found between
the number of corpuscles in their blood and
in that of children born and living in tem-
perate climates. If anything, the advantage
was with the Northerners. The tendency
to sallowness of complexion which was once
regarded as a sign of “tropical anaemia”
has been shown by these investigations to
afford no indication whatever of any weak-
ness or poverty of blood. On the other hand,
Breinl displays as much enthusiasm as is
permitted to a cautious scientific man con-
cerning the numerous examples of exception-
ally robust and wholesome children who have
come_ under his notice; and so far from en-
tertaining any doubts about the possibilities
of Northern settlement, his investigations
thus, far lead him to regard the Territory as
offering a positively unique opportunity for
development by a healthy white race.
The Lady with the Dress Basket.
Last week a few more of Melbourne’s not-
wanted babies were accounted for through
the police digging up a “farm” at Mor-
dialloc, where Isabella Newman had sui-
cided. Two little corpses were found buried
in the fowl-house, and there is a large sus-
picion that others are concealed elsewhere
about the premises. The deceased woman,
by the way, showed little wisdom in planting
the infants. True, she kept no fowls; but
the next occupier of the property v was very
likely to do so, and the first thing a fowl
does is to scratch. But according to the
police theory she carried on business in such
a wholesale way that she had no time to
choose suitable sites for her infants’ resting-
places. She seems to have been in the habit
of leaving the “farm” almost daily, going in
the direction of Melbourne from Mordialloc
station, and always with a sizeable dress
basket. She had the basket also on her re-
turn, and if questioned as to its contents
always answered, “Groceries.” As the
Newmans were only four in family, the
fiolice theory is that the “groceries” were
argely newly-adopted babies, which she had
presumably smothered on the way home.
And Mrs. Newman wasn’t the only baby-
farmer in Melbourne. On the very day that
the police overhauled the cemetery in the
fowLhouse, someone left in a Prahran right-
of-way the body of a very young Australian
who had apparently reached his end through
starvation. Baby-farming is pre-eminently
an industry where the lack of means to do
good deeds makes ill deeds done. These in-
fants were each worth from £lO to £5O to
Mrs. Newman. Now, supposing the State
had sufficient sense and humanity to
establish and maintain a Foundling
Hospital and to advertise itself as
a “kind lady willing to adopt new
babies,” the position would be that the
parents would save the £lO or £5O now paid
for “adoption” ; the babies would save their
lives ; and the State would save the cost of
digging up fowl-houses, wear and tear of
police, maintenance of convicted baby-
farmers in gaol, and a lot of other items, be-
sides which it would gain a large number
of citizens in embryo. Certainly, if a Found-
ling Hospital were in existence the other
“kind lady willing to adopt” would disap-
pear utterly and finally. But that is hardly
a matter for keen regret. Also the trade in
dress-baskets might experience a slight Tem-
porary slump, but neither need that worry
anybody. Most babies are more interesting
than dress-baskets, and more valuable as
long as they are not planted in the fowl-
house.

The Gospel of Gloom.
Sharp Cry of Annoyance from “Henri
Singemoine” :
A conference said to be representative ef the
Sydney Anglican and Noncomformist churches de-
cided last week to start a crusade against the sale of
newspapers on Sunday and the opening of fruit and
sweet shops at Circular Quay, Manly Beach and
elsewhere on the same day of partial gloom; also,
the conference pledged itself to do away with street-
trading cries on the Sabbath, wipe out boxing con-
tests, and in general hit things up in the way of
utter mournfulness.
The matter is of interest to the type of citizen
who inquires, with an affectation of naif wonder,
“What is a Wowser ?” It will likewise serve as an
answer to the disingenuous variety of cleric who
says proudly, “If to be a Wowser is to be an enemy
of drunkenness ; if to be a Wewser is to be an as-
sailant ef the White Slave trade and impurity gener-
ally, then am I proud,” etc. To be a Wowser is, of
eourse, te be not merely an upholder of the ordinary
conventions of society, but also a hypocrite, con-
scious or otherwise, who seeks, mostly with the object
of personal gain of one sort or another, to drive people
to extremes in Puritanism. Most of the clerics at
last week’s conference are notoriously unable to
keep their churches sufficiently full to return a divi-
dend in those cases where the sacred fanes are ex-
posed to any sort of competition. The surfing resorts
represent competition. The Sunday papers represent
competition. So Wowserism lays itself out to render
the one enemy too comfortless for Sabbath habita-
tion, and to make the existence of the other an im-
possibility. It is not holiness half so much as it is
business.
The Wowser proves his quality by persuad-
ing himself, and half persuading the public, that
the sordid question of gain has nothing to do with
the affair, and that his whole concern is with the
nation’s soul. His claptrap would not matter so
much if it did not react so perniciously on the
comfort and prosperity of the community. It is the
votes of people influenced by these misguided pietists
—pietists who are now battling to prevent the Sun-
day vending of tea and lemonade at places like
Manly and Coogee—that keep Sydney’s theatres and
music-halls the comfortless places they are. In
other parts of the world such establishments are
given wine and spirit licenses. This system does not
provoke any more drunkenness than Sydney’s habit
of going out in the interval to buy a drink at the
nearest bar. What it does, though, is to enable
theatrical managers overseas to give their patrons
comforts that local managers will never be able to
afford so long as 'the present regime lasts. The
position represents quite a problem to a large class.
The theatres have got well behind the standard of
comfort that has been attained by the bulk of the
play-going community of the day. To the average
modern man, for example, three hours in the average
Sydney theatre, with its narrow and board-like seats,
its awful atmosphere, and its restrictions in regard
to tobacco and alcohol, represent a dose of purgatory.
The victim goes, for various reasons—mostly family
ones—but he goes under the whip, as it were. In
London, Paris and New York he goes resignedly,
even joyously, knowing that if his destination is a
music-hall he will have a wide and comfortable arm-
chair to sit in, and, if he feels that way, a drink at
his elbow and a cigar between his lips ; while if his
fate is a theatre, he will at least enjoy that spacious-
ness and luxury in the auditorium which are made
possible chiefly by the existence of lounge bars
attached to the playhouse.
The more ambitious restaurants have been going
insolvent, or closing up to avoid insolvency, in Syd-
ney of late years. The rise in the cost of living,
due chiefly to rings formed by those who deal in
locally-produced foodstuffs, has had something to do
with the calamity, no doubt. But the fact that 95
per cent, of them have no wine and spirit licenses
is a good deal more to blame. No restaurant can
live by the sale of first-rate food alone. The initial
cost of the provender comes too near to the maxi-
mum price which the diner is willing to pay for the
meal in its prepared state. The compulsory closing
of Sydney hotels and restaurants at It p.m. has two
detrimental effects on the fortunes of the establish-
ments concerned. In the first place it robs them of
an opportunity to do a trade which is highly profit-
able elsewhere, and could presumably be made so
m Australia; and in the second—a result of the
first handicap—it helps to make our best hotels the
comparatively cheerless and out-of-date places they
notoriously are. There is nothing inherently evil
about late feeding in public. Some of the most
estimable people in the world indulge in it habitu-
ally. It doesn’t cause folks, to become drunkards or
rakes. There is a steady supply of labor ready and
a hle to meet the demand, at the enhanced wages
which those who work by night are able to command.
But the Wowser—he who would close every soft-
drinks shop on the Sabbath—has laid it down that
late suppers are wicked, and that the drinking of
strong liquors, if it can’t be prevented at other
times, shall at any rate be barred in the early hours
of the morning.
It is this spirit which keeps Sydney provincial.
Despite its natural beauty, its glorious cli-
mate and its other assets it makes very
little appeal to those who have known the ways of
European cities, with the result that the capital of
foreign trippers—the most paying of national guests
-—is not captured in anything like the quantities it
should be The Wowser is largely to blame; and
when he has killed the Sunday papers, and made
the seaside resorts places where it is an offence
against the law to sell or buy a bottle of ginger-
ale, he will have been the cause of still further loss
to people who at present never dream of regarding
him as an enemy. If Sydney could only be brought
to realise the price it pays, directly as well as in-
directly, for its Wowser, it would probably kill that
blight upon the social landscape—kill it as dead as
Hannibal.

Tasmania’s Frilled Electoral System.
The Bill which Premier Solomon, of Tas-
mania, introduced with a view to the aboli-
tion of by-elections exposes another weakness
of the Hare system of balloting. The trouble
is that to fill an extraordinary vacancy in a
six-member constituency, six times as many
electors as elected the old member are dragged
into the trouble. For instance, at last general
election (January of this year) the late
Speaker Davies was elected one of the six
members for Denison by 2000 persons, mostly
personal friends; but the by-election to fill
his seat must be taken in a constitu-
ency containing some 15,000 active
voters. Even if Davies’s 2000 per-
sonal friends all voted for one can-
didate, and persuaded 4000 other electors to
vote for the same candidate, they would be
utterly swamped at the poll by the 9000
electors who would prefer another candidate.
Thus it might happen, and probably would
happen, that in a straight-out contest
between two candidates in a 15,000
district, a quite different sort of man
to Davies, and of a quite different
party color, would secure the seat.
Davies belonged to the Liberal party. In
Denison last January the Libs, polled some
500 more primary votes than the Laborites,
but each party secured three quotas, and
three seats. It is considered almost a cer-
tainty that in the wider constituency at a by-
election a Labor man will beat the best
Liberal candidate that is at present in sight.
This is because the Solomon party doesn’t
happen to have a man available who is well-
known enough to attract the necessary SOOO
votes required to win the seat, while Labor
happens to have a well-known ex-member,
Sheridan (who became an ex-member by a
sort of accident last January), ready to take
the field. If Sheridan wins the by-election
on January 10 next, the Solomon party will
have lost a seat, and parties in the Assembly
will be 15 all. That, of course, means a dead-
lock and an immediate general election. Un-
less the Labor vote has Increased to a phe-
nomenal extent during the past year in Deni-
son, which is not likely, the seat would be
regained by the Libs, at the general mix-up,
and Solomon would count 16 to Earle’s 14,
as at present. And if, as seems probable,
another Lib. seat in Denison becomes vacant
by reason of Elliott Lewis’s elevation to a
judgeship within a month or so, and Labor
has another win, Earle will have a clear
majority. But the majority thus gained would
almost assuredly vanish at the first general
election, and parties would be at the same
old 16 to 14 again, or, at best, 15 all. Under
the Hare system the only true test of party
strength is a general election, and seats won
by either party at by-eleetdons are not worth
powder and shot.
Australia’s Defence Obligations.
“The Old Soldier” : Defence- Minister
Millen said in the Australian Parliament
the other day :
I disagree with the action of the late Govern-
ment in having increased the permanent forces 100
per cent, beyond what Lord Kitchener recom-
mended. Lord Kitchener's report was in faVor of
a citizen force with a permanent force at a mini-
mum.
Whereupon the considerably more knowledg-
able ex-Defence Minister Pearce inter-
jected :
Will you deny that he recommended two reliefs
for the Permanent Artillery ?
Millen’s somewhat feeble response was
that the permanent force is in advance of
what Kitchener recommended; but he
refrained from mentioning the numbers that
the great man did recommend —chiefly be-
cause it was impossible to mention them.
The permanent forces include the A. and I.
staff, a number of all ranks belonging to
the medical, supply and transport services,
and, finally, the permanent artillery. As
Pearce told his unfortunate successor, it
was only the last-named that had its strength
definitely fixed by the Kitchener report,
and that was not a maximum, but an irre-
ducible minimum—two reliefs, two detach-
ments for every gun mounted, and two sets
of magazine men, storemen and artificers for
each battery. The actual number would
depend on the number of guns mounted, or
to be mounted. It would be at least 25
men per gun for each relief, or 50 for the
two reliefs. That is much below European
standards, which require three reliefs of
fortress artillerymen for every work. As
for the other permanent forces, it must be
evident that, as the total numbers of Mil-
len’s citizen forces grow by the natural
operation of the Act, the number of the
A. and I. staff will have to be increased;
so will the medical service, the transport
drivers and the men of all ranks in the
Army Service Corps. The Government of
the day will have no choice in the matter in
regard to every branch of the permanent
forces, except, perhaps, the artillery. The
numbers who are swept into the ranks
every year require more men in the A. and
I. staff to drill and “administer” them ; and
as there are complaints that the supply_ of
satisfactory drill-masters is already running
short, there is talk of importing them whole-
sale from Britain or India. But the British
army has no men of the desired stamp to
spare ; at any rate, it certainly hasn’t nearly
enough to handle 14 or 35 thousand fresh
trainees every year. The lesson of which is
that one of Australia’s most important and
instant jobs is to train a body of capable
regimental officers.

The most satisfactory feature of the N.S.W.
elections lay in the fact that the buzzing of
the clerical insects had a totally different
effect from the one intended. Father
O’Reilly, of Bathurst, sooled the Catholic
Federation on to llolman, and spoke person-
ally in opposition to him in Cootamundra.
And Holman, with a strong local man in
the field against him, just about trebled his
previous majority. Miller, who, for the
last six years or more has represented
Bathurst, answered “Yes” to the Federa-
tion’s questions. His opponent maintained a
wary silence; and has, for (he first time, won
the seat for Labor. The defenders of the
Walls of Derry, heartened 4)y the uprising of
the Catholic Federation, hauled the Yellow
Pup out of his kennel in a hopeful
spirit, and directed him to flesh his
fangs in the legs of the Karthlick
enemy wherever found. The anachronistic
old animal was particularly active in Bel-
more, Phillip and Yass. And Romanists se-
cured these seats by 1000, 3500 and 1000-odd
votes respectively. The lesson appears to be
that the present generation declines to have
any flavoring of religion in its political meals.
It doesn’t much mind having to file its
schedule ultimately, but it draws the line
firmly at Sectarianism.

Over 12 months ago the Queensland Parlia-
ment passed an Act dealing with shoddy
boots. It was declared illegal to “load”
leather soles with cardboard or other stuffing.
Davey (of Davey, llalliday and Co., boot
manufacturers) was father of that Bill; he
had had practical experience of the injury
done to the Australian boot industry by this
cardboard-stuffing. The law made it illegal
not only to manufacture such shoddy, but
also to stock it for sale. When the Act was
passed a howl went up from the retail boot
merchants. They fell on Denham’s neck
and begged for time —much time —in which
to work off stocks (and d —n the public!).
Denham’s ear was stretched to the extent
of granting a six months’ suspension. The
same old deputation stood on the doormat
when Digby opened his office six months
later. They wanted another extension, and
they got it. For the third time Digby’s ear
has lately been bitten for yet another exten-
sion. But on this occasion the deputation
was cheekier. It threw off all disguise and
openly demanded the repeal of the obnoxious
measure, which was alleged to be a “restraint
on trade” —trade in this connection being
clearly another name for robbery. Also, it
more than hinted that a good, hard-shell Tory
like Denham shouldn’t monkey with such
dangerous Socialistic legislation. And
Digby is promising to look into the matter.
Which goes to show that Digby Denham and
not the Parliament rules the State; because
obviously a law which has the breath of life
withheld from it is no law at all, even though
Parliament had passed it 20 times.
News is cabled that Frerjch pathologist Basc
has located the little devil that causes cancer :
it turns out to be a parasite germinated in
stagnant water, and capable of infecting
fish, insects and warm-blooded animals. The
medical people expected to find it in that
uuarter. having long noted that wherever
frogs abound cancer is rife, and, of course,
stagnant water is the breeding-place of the
frog. If Basc’s announcement is verified,
the long battle with a disease which is par-
ticularly rife in Australia enters upon an
altogether new and more satisfactory phase.
Hitherto the scientist has been struggling in
the dark to locate the enemy. Now, with
the brute in the eorner, he will no longer
grope—he will attack.
On with the Spree!

The guttering candles were almost out;
The spilt wine dabbled the House of Grief,
When Dawn peeped in at the drunken rout
Through window blinds like a lurking
thief.
The wastrel host from a corner reeled
And roused with curses each drowsy guest.
“Come on, you blighters!” (His laughter
pealed)
‘There’s time enough in the grave to rest!”
“Here ! start the music and let’s have light!
With wine and laughter we’re not yet
done!
We had a jolly good time last night!
Get up and drink with me — everyone /”
They rose and drank, with their eyes half-
closed ;.
They clasped and danced with uncertain
feet.
And Bailiff Time on the mat reposed
And grimly smiled at the wond’ring street.

W.
One ‘ effect of the N. S. Wales election
should be to kill for ever that gag about
“Liberal apathy” which has been trotted out
regularly for years to explain away Conser-
vative defeats, both in the State and
Federal arenas. The operation of the second
ballot established last Saturday that the
more apathetics who are dragged to the poll
the worse it is for Liberalism. In Enmore,
for example, 8954 voted at the first ballot.
Of these 129 were Independents, and there
was a majority for Liberalism of 89. At the
second ballot 9663 people went to the poll,
and Liberalism was defeated by 741. In
Hurstville 10,038 people voted at the first
as against 11,144 at the second ballot. While
the Liberal was adding 421 to his original
total, the Laborite increased his by 1105.
And the same tendency was manifested in
every other constituency to a greater or
lesser degree. There are apparently but
two ways in which Liberalism can possibly
hope to save itself in the future. One is
by concealing as far as possible from the
country that there is anything in the shape
of an election pending. The other is to get
hold of something that resembles a construc-
tive policy. About the one definite plank the
party had to offer during the recent struggle
was electoral reform: that is to say, it an-
nounced that its first move, if i - eturned to
power, would be to exterminate the system of
balloting which the Wade party itself intro-
duced four years ago. This sort of attitude
is no earthly good to a young and progressive
country. By coming out boldly for decen-
tralisation, the abolition of the State Gover-
nor, the wiping out of the Legislative Council
and a few more" equally necessary reforms,
the party would not only stand to gain a
number of radical-minded young people,
but it would, in addition, make certain of
shaking off the Conservative barnacles that
at present infest and hamper the battered old
Liberal hulk.
At last the Australian Government
is doing something in the matter of ’
aviation, tenders having been called for
the construction of hangars at Point
Cook, Altona Bay (Vic.). So, pre-
sumably, if the aviators don’t get too fat
doing nothing, or forget their art in the long-
time waiting, we shall have some flights in
about another year. Still, it is better than
nothing.
A Maoriland pressman’s view of it: —
Maoriland cockies are busy at present building
their own funeral pyre. Their folly is about equal
to that which has let Labor into the present smash,
and the consequences will be much worse ; for whereas
Labor will recover from its present roasting, Cocky’s
ashes will be swept away in a whirlwind of Union-
ism. The newly-formed cockies’ wharf union at
Auckland is turning down strikers desirous of join-
ing ; and, shut off from the wharves, these workers
are making out into the country. This leaven
of unionism will very soon result in a rural
workers’ union, which union will rise up and hit
Cocky under the ear.
Simon Fraser, late of the Federal Senate,
is still going strong in his 80th year or
thereabouts. Last week he gave a luncheon
party at the Australian Club (Melb.) by
wajr of farewell to Major-General Kirk-
patrick. Old Simon’s hospitality naturally
inclined to Jokook, Forrest and others of
the Fusion party; but he didn’t forget to
include the Labor President of the Senate
in the feed. As an ardent Orangeman the
veteran Fraser cultivates the regard of all
official figureheads outside of the R.C.
Church, whereas the profundity of his dis-
respect for the Pope is quite pathetic.
The Adelaide City Council solemnly
drank the health of Colonel Light last week,
using an old silver bowl presented for the
purpose in 1858. Light was the man who,
in face of the opposition claims of Port Lin-
coln, Kangaroo Island and other spots,
decided that Adelaide should be fixed just
where it is; and his statue stands by the
G.P.O. with a thumb turned down, as of one
saying “Let it be there." The veteran
Charles Goode, congratulating A. A. Simp-
son, the new mayor, remarked that it is
pleasant to see sons coming along to take
the part of their fathers. It is not a thing
one can often say in municipal matters, but
in Adelaide there is a fancy for going into
the City Council while young, and both the
new mayor and his predecessor, Lavington
Bonython, have fathers much better known
than themselves.
“Petrol”: The motor-launch blaze I pre-
dicted 12 months ago occurred last week.
By good luck it took place in that salubrious
estuary, Cook’s River (N.S.W.), where one
sinks with difficulty, owing to the soupiness
of the water. The launch contained 10
women and two men, and when the long-
expected match was struck there was no
difficulty about the rest of the programme.
In one minute the launch was on fire from
end to end. The passengers took the only
course possible by jumping into Cook’s River,
whence they were rescued the worse for
wear but unharmed. The accident might
well have happened in a place like the Haw-
kesbury, Port Hacking, Botany, or some
other deep-water resort, when the result
would have been appalling. Now that the
Ma Government has been given this hint, it
might do something about regulating motor-
launches and providing for the safety of the
public. The next hint it gets will be when
a dozen or more voters are fried or drowned.

Thanks to the happy idea of the Sydney
Repertory Society, and the support given
to it by the J.C.W. firm, Wilton Welch and
George Willoughby, Sydney had the chance
on Monday to see Miss Muriel Starr in a
play that gave her artistry a chance. Pinero’s
tragedy, “Mid-Channel,” was performed at a
crowded matinee at the Criterion by a cast
of exceptional strength, admirably handled
by the Repertory Society’s producer, Leslie
Casey. All the characters in this fine ex-
ample of the Pinero of the later period be-
long to that unique race of the London mid-
dle-class known as flat-dwellers. All of
them seem to do nothing except dwell in
flats, call on each other in flats, and have
afternoon-tea and assignations and alterca-
tions and nerves and flirtations and suicides
in flats. None of them have children, unless
a grown-up girl is counted, and all of them
have too much time to think or moon or
smoke or drink. Yet this tragedy of middle-
class flat-dwellers is a real tragedy—a clash of
temperaments, due in the first instance to a
lack of children. The fall in the birth-rate
is the best thing that has happened to
dramatists.
