Zoe Blundell is a woman well on in the
thirties who has nothing to do but quarrel
with her blatant husband. The rows are
“LOHENGRIN," AT HER MAJESTY’S,
SYDNEY.
Villain Graham Marr swears before King
William Anderson that Elsa has murdered
her little brother.
real rows, and at the end of one of them
Theo Blundell leaves his house. Zoe tries
travelling, ‘gets lonely, sends for one of her
“tame robins,” and he joins her —with the
usual results. Meantime Theo is having a
rather wearisome “gay” time with a lady of
battered rectitude. A mutual friend, with
the theory that in every marriage the part-
ners reach a rough spot in their passage,
situated in mid-channel —a brief period that
leads, once safely passed, into calm water —
attempts to patch up the broken pieces. Both
have got tired of their temporary partners,
and are eager to come back. But first Theo
confesses his lapse with the light lady:
then, in a scene of brilliant construction, he
passes from his light raillery of Zoe’s “tame
robin” to a conviction that the robin hadn’t
been at all tame. Zoe confesses that she,
too. has not been faultless —and at once the
reconciliation is off. Zoe, despetate, goes to
her “tame robin’s” flat to implore him to
take her, but finds that he has engaged him-
self to the usual nice girl. Zoe pitiably re-
cognises that she has “made a hash” of her
life, and hearing her husband coming, re-
tires to another room. Then While the three
men—her husband, her lover and the mutual
friend—discuss a way out of the situation, it
apnears she has thrown herself over a bal-
conv.
The interpretation was as brilliant as the
writing. Miss Aluriel Starr gave to the
neurotic character of Zoe the full intensity
and pathos required. Her work was a reve-
lation of a temperament which can only be
indistinctly guessed at in the few chances
allowed her in “Within the Law.” Aliss
Grace Palotta was a charming chic matron.
THE TUNEFUL LOVER AND THE
SAD SWAN.
Spencer Thomas Lohengrin (Elsa’s melodious
young man) asks the magic swan to transform
itself into the missing boy and confound
villainy.
Miss Nancie Stewart displayed none of a
beginner’s gaucherie. Miss Gwen Burroughs,
as the “gay” female, hardly suggested the
mercenary hardness beneath a seductive
manner. As the “tame robin” Eric Maxon
rather forced his, emotion, but otherwise his
manner was delightful, and he succeeded in
making the weakness, rather than the cad-
dishness, of his part usefully plain. Regin-
ald Wykeham, perfectly cast for the # part
of the crude husband, was thoroughly con-
sistent both in his temper and in his re-
morse. The success of the play, however,
was Pinero’s favorite character of the man-
of-the-world who acts as chorus to the eter-
nal triangular mix-up. The mutual friend,
played by Arthur Styan, gave him the
opportunity to depict with fine subtlety the
charming club-man, whose good heart makes
him a bit of a bore, but whose sincerity
shines out behind his quaint mannerisms
and his conventional mind. Styan, relin-
quishing the White Slave traffic for one
afternoon, presented a character as unfor-
getable as is that of Miss Starr’s impetuous
and terribly human Zoe.

John McCormack was programmed for just
five songs at his farewell concert last Satur-
day night. But his devoted Town Hall
audience thundered and shouted at him till
he finished up with a score of 14 numbers,
all of them well and truly sung. Soprano
May Huxley, violinist Mcßeath and accom-
panist O’Brien earned their share of riot
and disorder ; but it was after each McCor-
mack item that the real avalanche crashed
down. It must grieve him to be leaving,
asthore. There may be money in other lands,
but och ! how aisy it is to pick it up in Aus-
tralia, me jool!

“East Lynne,” the imperishable Little
Williedrama, will snuffle through its final per-
formance at Sydney Royal on Friday night,
when the Andersdn season closes. The
wretched old thing only started last Satur-
day evening, so its run has been brief. 11. R.
Roberts works a litle novelty into the part
of Sir Francis Levison, and the rest of the
co. act well enough to escape a vote of cen-
sure. The citizens at large have not at-
tended in large quantities at Willie’s bed-
side, and the funeral service is likely to be
unimpx-essive. On Saturday night, Julius
Knight and Irene Brown start serious busi-
ness with Bernard Shaw’s “Alan and
Superman.”
At the Little Theatre, Wilton Welch’s
admirable production of the White Slave
melodrama, “The Warning,” is being well
patrpnised.
William Anderson, who has the Christmas
dates at the Palace (Sydney), will induce
his American Musical Burlesque Co. to re-
appear in the theatre on Saturday with “A
Day at the Races.” Last time the combina-
tion was at the Palace it opened with “The
Grafters,” and hilarity is alleged to be just
as pronounced in the racecourse piece.

The big-typers in the panto are to be Grace

a combination that ought to do things well.’
If the Tivoli could find a few T more turns
like Hawley’s drama, “The Bandit,” this
paper would buy up poor old Dan O’Con-
nor’s supply of compliments. The playlet
is easily the best thing in its line that has
happened this year, and should hold its own
against all-comers for some time to come.
The Pasquali Bros, provide a rare supply 0 f
tumbling and hand-balancing; and Ferry
Corwey, the musical clown, is responsible
for a good mixture of music and piffle. The
five Petleys and Du Calion are the principals
in the remainder of the programme.

There is another interesting animal turn
at the National Amphitheatre (Sydney)
this week, supplied by Howard’s bears and
dogs. Audiences at the Fuller music-hall
are always ready to welcome well-trained
Leasts, and brother Howard has the goods.
In the musical line the Five Lasses have been
rustling the applause, and Mantell’s mario-
nettes have been keeping the warmth circu-
lating. The co. is all-American and as
versatile as Uncle Sam’s canned meats.
The floating population of the White City
at Rushcutter’s Bay (Sydney) increases as
the big amusement park becomes better
known and the Gum Leaves drift in from
the country for the holiays. The side-shows
have been running to big business during the
week, particularly the scenic railway, giant
hurdy-gurdy, fun factory, puzzle house and
mirror maze ; and the numerous free enter-
tainments have been generously supported.

At the J. D. AVilliams theatres this week
the pick of the films were “Zuza,” a two-
reel Keystone comedy, at the Crystal Palace;
“The Lost Millionaire,” a mixture of love
and cash, at the Empress ; and a Balkan War
picture at the Lyric, “The Secret of Adrian-
ople,” chock-full of excitement, aeroplanes,
racing motors and machine guns. “The
Miracle,” to be screened at West’s on Satur-
day, looks like being a record-breaker; and
“Our New Minister” is putting in some good
work at the Lyceum. “Sister Beatrix.”
which is an alias for “The Miracle,” will
head the bill at the Imperial (William-street)
on Saturday.
“Miss Elizabeth's Prisoner,” new to Aus-
tralia, is filling in the nights before
Christmas at Melb. Royal. It belongs to
the poor but pleasant order of comedies,
which waver between drama and farce, and
never rise to realism or fall to utter foolish-
ness. If there were any atmosphere of truth
about this purely theatrical story of the
American “rebel” stranger who eventually
“cuts out” a tall, blustering “loyalist” from
the esteem of the lady of the house (time
a.d. 1778), it would be a bracing atmosphere.
But there is no truth in it, nor any in-
genuity of complication, nor room for doubt
as to how the plot will eventuate. More-
over, the easy unearnestness of Lewis Waller
“MISS ELIZABETH’S PRISONER.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When ’Murka, in mad George's days,
Renounced the British yoke,
Madge Titheradge with eyes ablaze
Looked down on any bloke
Who took up arms against the King
To set the country free,
And thought him only fit to swing
From British gallows-tree.
Lew. Waller, lame and out of breath,
Fell into Madge’s clutch,
And might have met a, felon's death,
She hated him so much ;
But Waller, brave and debonair.
Was what the Boers call “slim,”
And when he praised her beauty rare
Madge fell in love with him.
Anon she knew he had befooled
Her eager self-esteem ;
She thought the douche of truth had cooled
The ardor of her dream ;
So Waller, warming to his part,
Swore further love for her.
And in the fortress of her heart
She held him prisoner.
Dear boy, address a wench as Queen
Who reckons you a brute ;
Cry “Plum.” or “Peach” or “Nectarine,”
Or other kind of fruit.
For let a Madge be old or young,
Or in her golden prime,
A Waller with a winsome tongue
Can fool her all the time.
VICTORIA.
O. K.

Labor’s strenuous fight in Parramatta
against Moxham almost added to the sur-
prises of the N.S.W. elections. On the first
ballot. Liberal had a majority of 403 ; at the
second, the lead dwindled to 216. It looks
as if Joe Cook (who represents Parramatta
in the Federal Parliament) will find a bull-
dog on the doormat the next time he calls.

characters are a tall, lurid burglar

Saturday (20th) at the early hour of 7.30
p.m. The Forty Thieves” will be formally
committed to popularity for a long term.
Popularity for a W'illiamson panto, is a
foregone conclusion, whether it be deserved
or otherwise. The approaching medley en-
tertainment is classed by the management as
a “stupendous ’ affair, and this journal hopes
for the best.
Also the King’s, whence “The Grafters”
have just departed, is closed for rehearsal of
Anderson’s coming event. “The Land of
Nod” —which is the event in question—in-
troduces itself as a musical fantasy from
America, and several members of the co.
will be making, their first apnearanee in this
country on Saturday next (20th).

Gregan McMahon’s amateurs had a couple
of evenings with Bernard Shaw’s “Candida,”
at Melbourne Athenaeum, last week. Gregan
himself played Burgess, and gave a con-
vincing picture of the coarse-grained fraud.
As on a former occasion, Lyle Christian was
a graceful, unearthly Marchbanks, while Miss
Allie Robson had the right clue to Candida.
Her acting wasn’t always perfect, but she
presented “Candy’s” domineering affability

admirably. The Rev. Lexy Mill, by R. 11.
McFadzean, was appropriately calf-like.
Miss Dorothy Hiscock’s “Prossy” Garnett
was marred by the lady giving a touch of
militancy to the natural impertinence of
the character. Norman Carter’s wings were
too short to raise him to the moderate his-
trionic heights demanded of parson Morell,
who was represented as an effeminate sniv-
eller instead of a manly humbug. “Candida”
was preceded by “The Mate,” wherein Arthur
Schnitzler makes a fuss of a professor’s dis-
covery that what he had regarded as his
wife’s grande passion was merely a passage
of rorty dalliance with a young roue of her
acquaintance. The piece is not the most
notable sample of Schnitzler’s art, and it
contains a very theatrical curtain; but it
was safely interpreted by a party of six,
among whom Gregan McMahon had most to
say, and said it with solemn recognition of
the fact that he was burdened with a great if
fanciful grief.

“East Lynne” is again failing to keep the
peace at Melbourne Princess’s. The revival,
which is for six nights only, is merely inter-
esting because it pushes another Lady Isa-
bel on Melbourne’s notice. Miss Elwyn
Harvey is the lady in question, and she is
never quite equal to the tearful possibilities
of the part. Vivian Edwards faithfully rea-
lises the perfidy of Francis Levison, and
Cyril Mackay gives due weight to Carlyle's
respectability. Miss Pearl Helmrich con-
tributes a bright red wig to Aunt Cornelia’s
make-up; Miss Pearl Smith, Miss Holroyd,
and Max Clifton figure in other grown-up
parts; while the job of portraying Willie
is the. unreasonable excuse for keeping a
small infant named Huggett out of her bed.
To-morrow is the last night of the calamity.
On Saturday George Willoughby will exhume
“Uncle Tom’s Cabin.”

Chief features of novelty at the Opera
House this week are amateur ragtime con-
tests, engineered by Gene Greene, the self-
crowned emperor of the most monotonous
form of entertainment that ever happened
hereabouts.. Greene, even with the assist-
ance of his' clever pianist, is incapable of
keeping reasonable persons amused during
the full length of his turn. His six, or
eight, or more ragtime chants are practic-
ally one long string of niggah balderdash,
with a few foreign noises and cheap grimaces
attached. The most that can be expected
of the ragtime contestants is that some of
them will display all the qualifications of
Greene, bar his amazing audacity. Other
items on the present bill are Rosa Roma, a
comely young violinist in a bright, abbre-
viated frock ; Parish, the jumper; the Rich-
ards acrobats; Lucille Savoy; the lantern-
clothed Venus, who presumes to sing; and
Doody and Wright. There are redeeming
absurdities in the efforts of Doody. Among
the others Alberto Marini, the small, robus-
tious tenor, is again taking loud, melodious
liberties with familiar English ballads. His
rendering of "The Rosary,” for instance,
tears the song’s soulful sentiment to shreds.
'!< •I' H*
Branscombe’s Dandies warble and frivol
as usual at their well-appointed domicile on
St. Kilda Esplanade. Eight .Dandies, in-
cluding the pianist, supply an entertainment
which commands a good deal of warm ap-
proval on the bleakest Melbourne nights, and
seems twice as good when the canvas roof is
furled alid stars blink upon the auditorium
from a balmy sky. Hilda Lane, the soprano-
in-chief, is a neat vocalist with a sweet
voice; and the contralto, Evelyn Ward, sings
quite charmingly. Two men are a note or
two above ordinary open-air concert form,
George Morgan is moderately funny at one
end of him and very nimble at the other,
whilst the comedy sketches by Irene Vere
and Claude Dampier count as legitimate in-
citements to mirth. Irene has an air of
enjoying herself, and Claude is a personified
joke.
* * * *
Spencer’s main offering in Melbourne last
week was “Chelsea, 7750,” a detective drama
of great length and much sensation. “Better
Days” and “Old Coupons” both dealt with
a tramp’s affection for a little cheild, while
“Beauty in the Seashell” was a seashore
idyll crowded with the momentous passion
of sticky-sweet 17. Scenes in the Chevreuse
Valley (France) and the usual “Gazettes”
came as something of a relief to the gener-
ally bathetic tendencies of the prevailing
fiction.
At Melbourne Majestic “The Chorus
Lady” is still rescuing her young sister from
the clutches of the unseemly liichard Craw-
ford. Also her lover, Dan Mallory, con-
tinues to put the worst possible construction
on her movements, and shouts his opinion
of her virtue to her friends and relations.
As for Crawford, he leaves it to relays of
fashionable footwear to convey a hint of
his wickedness. Otherwise one would say
that he was a sedate and solemn city man
who had wandered into the drama by mis-
take.
At Adelaide Royal, “The Man on the Box”
has run a merry week. It is just one of the
good-style sentimental farces that modern
Anierica loves, and Hugh Buckler and his
wife do more tba_n justice to its light humor ;
also they are well supported, especially on
the men’s side. In the open air there is
steadv support for the Dandies —a capable
all-round crowd. At the Tivoli Reynolds
and Donegan still skate, and the Fanton
athletes still turn upside down while blind-
folded, and the Primrose heavyweights and
Lillie Langtry still sing. Lily Eyton is a
pleasing new item. The King’s has a lot of
clever new people, who are reserved for
criticism next week. West’s have a French
drama, gloomy but highly popular; Wonder-
graph a sporting event, racing plus aero-
p’ane, with a beauty competition to follow ;
and the little Pav., handily-situated for
Christmas shoppers, scores with a well-mixed
programme.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“R. 8.”: I have been looking over Sydney’s White
City, and find it the expected Place of Things to
Scare People Deliciously; Mad Railways to Make
Girls Scream and Grip Their Escorts ; and, more
than all, Contrivances Which Hurt Other People
and Make You Laugh. Before I left for America
a youth in The Bulletin office asked me to place
his invention of a loop-the-loop railway at Coney
Island. The showman I submitted it to said: “This
ain’t dangerous-lookin’ enough. Watcher want is
sumpin’ real safe, but lookin’ terr’ble dangerous, so
the girls in the car squeal and hold fast to the
boys. Then you got some success.” Scenic rail-
ways do that; so do the joy-wheels, with the added
attraction of informal leg-shows. But what pleases
most is hurling at something in the form of man-
kind, even though it be but an effigy ; and in throwing
balls at something breakable, preferably crockery.
The show that must become popular is the collec-
tion of solemnly-ridiculous scarecrow effigies, moving
on a circular railway, of 'which but half is seen-
out of a “property” pub, and round- the stage, and
off again. These figures are of wood, so that they
may suffer the impact of wooden balls without seri-
ous damage, and when a figure takes a stunning
blow on the face or head great is the joy thereat.
But the show should do better by giving personal-
ities to the wooden targets. Most of the people who
go to the White City are probably Labor ; and what
could be funnier to the mob than the bombardment
of comic politicians of both sides of politics? Joseph
Cook; Speaker Johnson as he played Little Willie
in “East Lynne” ; Willy Kelly taking himself seri-
ously; Bruce Smith at his most portentous moment;
Irvine burlesquing George Higinbotham; George
Reid and his Primitive Methodist jokes ; Kink O’Mal-
ley at any time; the gloomy Wade; that great
statesman Ryrie, who would thus be served by the
only argument he can understand ? The Libs might
be given Holman and Griffith to shy at, though the
average Lib. can rarely hit anything, if his choice
of a leader is anything to go by.
From Maud Williamson, Surrey Theatre, London:
“Absalom Jones” (B. 18/9/’l3) honors me too much.
I have never written or played any version of
“Joseph of Canaan” other than the admirable one
by the Rev. Geo. Walters, produced by the late Geo.
Rignold at Her Majesty’s Theatre, Sydney, in which
I created the part of Ayesha, Potiphar’s wife. In it
I afterwards toured with Mr. Alfred Woods, who
played Joseph. There is eertainly no such line in
the play as “Absalom” cites. The Rev. Geo. Wal-
ters’s play, which was before its time, is almost
identical in scenario with “Joseph and His Breth-
ren,” now running at His Majesty’s, London. Geo.
Rignold brought the Walters play to London and I
was engaged to play my original part in it; but
the Lord Chamberlain wouldn’t let us. It would
be interesting to compare a few of the things
they do license with this very fine version
of a now successful licensed play. Pictures of the
Australian production and photos of the late Scot
Inglis, Geo. Rignold and myself appeared in
The Sketch, St. Paul’s and other London papers;
but I have not seen one paper refer to the fact that
the first version of the story of Joseph and his
brethren was done in Australia, and by a man as
well known theatrically as Sir Herbert Tree.
“Stargazer”: Everything from America is ragged
nowadays. The leading woman in “The Chorus
Lady,” now being produced in Melbourne by an
exceedingly Yankee co., actually succeeds in ragging
the English language. She drops and lifts her
cadences regardless of anything on earth but the
spirit of plantation melodies, and breaks up her sen-
tences in defiance of all the simple elements of
punctuation. A syncopated speech by this lady sounds
as if it should be accompanied by samples of buck
dancing by a celebrated coon-specialist named Sam
Johnsing.
One of the most joyous and conscientious souls
went on the Last Tour when Barney Levy died
in Brisbane the other day. As an advance agent he
knew Australasia, up hill and down gully, working
for St. Leon’s Circus, Dan Batr5 r , Bilanderson,
George Marlow and others. He was with Barry for
about 15 years, and to that salubrious manager sent
a telegram which is still quoted in the dressing-
rooms. Levy had been sent ahead into the smalls
of Victoria, and arrived at Nhill. There- he found
a newly-built hall—the only play-house. He
enthusiastically measured it up and wired to Barry
as follows: “Magnificent hall here; show week; hall
seats about 1500 ; good prospects; ought to do excep-
tionally well, but can’t get hall.” One more. The
MacMahons were running a show which needed an ox
to draw a light waggon. Levy was ahead and was
ordered to get the beast. When the co. landed at
the railway station Levy was asked if he had obtained
the ox. “No,” said he, “but I got a cow.” “What
the is the good of a cow?” asked Alfred
Woods, who was then stage-managing. “Oh, it’ll
be all right,” replied Levy;’“bring it on half-way!”
“Ginno Fizzo”: George Portus, who blew into
Adelaide a few days ago to manage the New Tivoli,
blew out almost as quickly. He moved on to Wes-
tralia with a bunch of variety artists to open up
vaudeville in Perth for the Rickards circuit. Mclntosh
fixed everything up at the last minute —-even to the
act of the Eight Saxones, who (on a request by wire-
less) got off the outgoing English boat at Fre-
mantle. The departure of Portus gives Adelaide a
new manager in Percy Crawford—the third within
seven days.
“Blue Mask”: It was suggested last week by
several enthusiasts that the supporting public should
have been given a say by ballot as to which operas
should be produced by Quinlan at Sydney in the final
nights of the season. Wonderful what faith innocent
box-payers have in that ballot fraud! Labouchere
used to chuckle over the way he worked it when he
was in the game. He had announced his intention
of producing a Shakspeare play, and began to push
the advertising by giving patrons of his theatre the
right to vote as to which of the immortal Bill’s
pieces should be selected. Meanwhile, he had pre-
pared “Cymbeline.” The public went 1 heavy on
“Hamlet,” “Macbeth” and “The Merchant of Venice,”
in that order. Only two plunged on “Cymbeline.”
They were Labouchere and his manager. They won —
easy. Also, they did good business.
Australia’s erstwhile American friend J. D. Wil-
liams is understood to be bringing a “magic” show
with him on his imminent return from the United
States. It was something of the kind he had last
time.
The obsolete system in the picture-show business
of giving one performance each night and two
matin&es a week will soon be a thing of the past in
Australia. Spencer has been hanging on ,to it; but
he passes over to the continuous-day-and-night idea.
Which means that the Combine has decided on the
non-stop.
“B.B.”’: I nominate as the busiest scene-painters
in Australia Harry Whaite and George Dixon, of Syd-
ney. During the year they have used over 250 bolts
of canvas, and a bolt contains 65 or 70 yards double-
width. The shows and “shops” they have dressed in-
clude the new Adelaide Tivoli, Little Theatre (Sydney),
Sydney Tivoli (new scenes and drop), Hongkong
Theatre, Perth Theatre (West’s), another at Albany,
Corson Clarke’s show at Melbourne, the “Glory Girl”
turn (Tivoli), exterior of a church for “The
Miracle” (West’s), “Paul Jones” .(Newcastle
amateurs), “Mikado” (Goulburn amateurs), “Doro-
thy” (Maitland amateurs), “Country Girl” (Lis-
more amateurs), “Chorus Girl” (for rfew Ameri-
can co.), Stephanson-Linley panto. (M.L.), “Les
Cloches des Corneville” (Warwick Operatic Co.),
and a large stock of scenery for the Queensland
Government. Whaite was for years with Brough and
Boucicault and Herbert Fleming, and painted all
their productions while with them. His partner,
Dixon, was up till recently chief assistant to Joseph
Harker, London’s biggest brush, and did a lot of
Oscar Asche’s beautiful scenery. Whaite promises him-
self a trip to Britain in March or thereabouts.
“Anon”: “Harrison O.” (B. 27/11/’l3) wants the
divorce proceedings in “A Butterfly on the Wheel”
quashed for irregularity. Of course, the anonymous
letter is not evidence per se, and it is in getting over
the difficulty that the legal knowledge of the K.C.
helps the dramatic skill of his collaborator. It is
done this way. Counsel for both respondent and
co-respondent withdraw any objection they had to
the letter being read to the jury, because any damage
the reading of the letter might do had already been
done by the outburst of the respondent after the
letter had been placed in her hands. It is far-
fetched, of course, but ingenious and quite good
law. If it hadn’t been for the letter going in as
evidence, Peggy would probably have lost the case,
the play would have been a poor one, and Beerbohm
would not have made the tender inquiry; “Do you
write with your left hand ?”
“Stargazer”: I have seen the Waller whom London
was unaccountably keen on. He was playing in “The
Butterfly on the Wheel,” and over against him was an
unimportant pro. of the Beerbohm family, whom not
even Lal-Lal has gone crazy about. Yet the com-
paratively young Thespian whom Lal-Lal has studi-
ously ignored, by a genteel effort of easy speech and
natural action made the great artist look a hard,
harsh metallic product. Really Waller was most
unwise in throwing his old, rigid, stage-made methods
into contrast with the manner of a more recent pro.
who acts as he lives, breathes and has his being, so
that the star appeared a dramatic slot-machine by
comparison. I know comparisons are odious, but
Waller is responsible for this one.
“C.A.”: Picture-man Spencer’s ambition has been to
create an Australian picture-film industry, and now he
believes he has done it. When he first tried to start
it he found the Yankee and European combines too
strong for him. To go in for the business on a large
scale meant that his supply of films was likely to
be cut off, and, in any case, he had no chance of
exporting. So he took two trips round the world to
see what could be done by diplomacy. On his last
jaunt he fixed things up, and brought back with him,
as producer, Alexander Butler, a man of experience
in America, England and Italy; and he made an
arrangement with the English and Yankee combines
to take 40 copies of every film produced. So the
industry starts at Rushcutter’s Bay (Sydney) on a
good, sound basis. A stock company is to be engaged
and the work will go on all the time.
Tittell Brune has turned up again in London, this
time as Cigarette in a revival of Ouida’s “Under
Two Flags” at the Lyceum. Like “East Lynne,”
this tearful yarn has a big following, and Tilda has
been scoring as a sprightly vivandiere before crowded
audiences.
MOON MOODS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My soul is filled with sentiment
(The sloppy sort, in penny books),
Because the moon has torn the tent
Of dark-blue sky, and roundly looks
Upon me as I lie among
The grains of , sand ;
Because the summer night is young;
Because the band
(By ocean voices nearly drowned)
Emits a rhythmic, rippling sound.
My eyes send out a pair of tears
To take a gentle evening run,
The while I dream of useless years
And wondrous things I might have done ;
Thus good intentions stir my s#ul,
Beneath the moon,
As though it were a porridge bowl
Stirred by a spoon ;
And with my mood in sympathy
The band croons in a minor key.
But soon again impenitent
(For moods are brief beside the sea),
Light moon-engendered sentiment
And sweet romance steal over me.
A maid with moon-embellished face
Upon the sand
Returns a silvered youth’s embrace,
The while the band
Bleats out a little tune for me—
A plaintive, lovesick melody.
But now the night, like us, -grows old,
And freezes my romantic mood;
Young love upon the beach grows cold,
And hurries off in search of food ;
My nose dislikes the chilly air
The while the sand
Has mingled damply in my hair;
And now the band
Has changed its tune from gay to grave,
And blows its final blast, “God save.”
N.s.w.
F. OLDMAN.

In the calm of evening, when the last sun
reflections were fading from the discolored
Yarra, a lonely river fisherman felt some-
thing heavy on his line, and pulled in. As
the hook neared the surface the Something
rolled off, yet not before the fisherman had
seen what it was. But he pulled himself
together and angled for it again in a rever-
ent sort of way, and, having hooked it firmly,
drew it out and laid it on the shadowy bank.
Then, having caught quite enough for one
evening, he wound up his line in haste and
TAINT AND POWDER.
THE ARTIST : afraid I've got your color a trifle too high.”
THE LADY : “Oh, a little powder will put that right.”
stumbled off to the South Yarra police sta-
tion, where he reported that a cold, silent
man was waiting down by the dark
river. The Yarra fishermen, of whom there
are a fair number, occasionally catch fish;
but mostly they go to the sad brown stream
to think deeply and listen to the wind sigh-
ing in the willows. Thev look mournfully
at the people in passing skiffs and are only
moved to passion by the wash of a motor-
boat disturbing their lines. The submarine
traveller who arrived without making a
ripple was probably- regarded with an
approving eye by his captor. But did the
fisherman go down next evening? Would
anyone—even a Yarra fisherman —want to
go down next evening and put a new hook in
the Brown Waters of Oblivion?
This shows how the wind blows since the
waterside strike commenced in Maoriland.
He had taken out his passage from Auck-
land to Sydney on the last trip of the
Maheno, which steamer was manned (in a
manner of speaking) by a nondescript crowd
of “loyalists.” Being known as a “red-rag”
public speaker, he was welcomed by
“specials” (presumably /special constables)
on the wharf, and by them reviled and as-
saulted. However, he got aboard the ship
and to his cabin; but presently it was
rushed by the strike-breakers who formed
the crew, and he was assaulted some more,
dragged along the deck, bumped down the
gangway, and tossed out into the roadway.
There some mounted police rescued him.
Of course, no arrests were made ; but if that
passenger had been a “free laborer” the
mounted police would probably have batoned
the assaulting crowd good and hearty, and
would certainly have arrested one or two of
them. Likewise, the Tory press, which re-
ported the above facts as a mere matter of
indifferent detail, would have been howling
at the door of the Government for revolvers
to arm the police in the defence of helpless
citizens against the brutal mob.
Mrs. de Willoughby Waye writes
You know the present fashion in hats is a striking
one, a la Hussar and all that, and I really had to
have a new one (as I told George) if I was to help
on polling-day. The last fortnight has been too awful
for anything, what with one thing and another, and I
wish you to understand that I refer particularly to the
shameful way in which people have been libelling our
man. George says it’s actionable, he believes, only that
at election time things are not quite so rigidly adhered
to, or something like that. He growled when I told
him that I had ordered my hat —a perfect duck;
turquoise-blue pile velvet, white ostrich feathers and
pale gold band, and only 20 guineas. But of course,
it was quite impossible to go and vote in anything
common. Do you know, we were worried out of our
lives by the wretched —what do they call the crea-
tures ? ” Yes —canvassers (what a horrid word !) dur-
ing the last two or three weeks. You know how
• hard it is to keep a girl nowadays, with the
minxes running off to get married (how they can
do it and leave ladies to perform quite menial
duties, passes my comprehension) ; and my cook
absolutely refuses to answer the bell, as she tells
me it is not in the schedule of the Cooks’ Union
(not the bell, of course, but the answering).
was the consequence? Not only had I to see most
of the callers myself, but — ugh ! —those horrid can-
vassers ! The first one was Mrs. Dobbs, our fruit -
and-vegetable man’s wife, a person who wished to
know whether I would .vote No-license. Of course,
1 said at once that I would; I really could not afford
to be seen talking with a woman of her social posi-
tion on my own doorstep ! Then a very fat, pimply-
faced man, who smelled dreadfully of drink, and
broke a lot of our flowers as he came up the path,
he was staggering so, rang the bell and would not
g 0 away un tn he had told me the history of pro-
hibition on — I think it was the rolling main, he
said; and he looked like a shocking bad sailor,
Anyhow, I promised to vote for it if Madame sent
my hat m time, and he went away. A woman called
from the Labor League next day> and; of COU rse, :
promised, to get rid of her, as I could see she was
impossible, socially. Several ladies came from the
Liberal League, and implored me not to vote for
anyone but the rejected candidate, otherwise Labor
would win the seat, and we should all be ruined.
I didn’t know anything about him, but I promised to
yote for hjm wh - en M B adame sent my hat. But the
wre tch never sent it, and so I stayed at home all
day and ne ver voted at all.

Toys must now be numbered among the
things that Australians Can’t Make. That
is to say, with their present ineffective Pro-
tection, manufacturers in this country are
unable to turn out an article that can com-
pete with the sweat-shop production from
abroad. For the first 11 months of this
year £70,000 worth of toys were imported
into the Commonwealth. Japan sends us
about £lO,OOO worth, largely for the
Christmas season; and in October of this
year £8436 worth made in Germany
and £6778 worth from the Cold Country
were dumped in this long-suffering country.
However, the good unionist who buys a doll
for his infant need not worry that he is
purchasing the product of sweated white
slaves. The chances are that the thing was
made by a Jap, a Hindoo or a Cingalee—-
the two last being also contributors to this
country’s toy supply.
“Gazer”; “F’s” faithful account of the
dull horror of Horsham (B. 4/12/’l3) re-
minds me that a couple of years back a
cocky, domiciled some miles out of that
Stygian country village, sued his wife for
divorce. The climax of his list of reasons
why the marital halter should be unhitched
was that his wife, instead of working con-
tentedly in the domestic abode, was always
dissatisfied, and “yearned for life in Hor-
sham”!
* * * *
A Melbourne sensation of last week was
the hasty suicide of Mrs. Isabella Newman,
who first pushed herself into prominence
when she issued a challenge to run any
woman in the Commonwealth for £5O and
the alleged championship. Her gage was
accepted by Miss Ivy Evans, of Bendigo,
and the contest happened on the South Mel-
bourne ground in March, 1908, in the pre-
sence of a numerous assemblage and the
late T. Bent. Mrs. Newman, or Madam
Isa Bell, as she called herself, was then
about 40 and possessed a husband and sev-
eral children, wherefore no one was sur-
prised when Miss Evans, much younger and
more sprightly, easily defeated her. Six
months later publicity of another sort came
to her, over a matter of a very new baby,
which had been taken from its mother at
Geelong and handed to a nurse at Coburg.
From her it was adopted by Mrs. Newman ;
but when the police wanted to know why it
hadn’t been vaccinated it could not be found
-—and it has never been found to this day.
The athletic lady said she had transferred
it, in the street, to a Mrs. Milburn, but no
Mrs. Milburn with a newly-adopted infant
could be discovered. There was practically
no evidence to convict her of having a hand
in the disappearance of the infant, but the
law tried her on a charge of perjury com-
mitted when “up” for the non-registration
of the adoption—and gave her 12 months.
Since then the police have naturally been
interested in her, and finding that, under
various names and in different suburbs, she
was advertising her willingness to adopt in-
fants, they became more than suspicious.
Finally a Fitzroy woman, being unable to
get a view of a baby Mrs. Newman had
adopted, complained to the police, who pro-
ceeded to Mordialloc and arrested her. She
took the matter coolly, and desired a tew
minutes’ seclusion to change her raiment
which few minutes she spent in writing a
note declaring her innocence and in taking
a dose of strychnine. She was dead in hai
an hour.
FROM A SELF-MADE MORALIST TO A LADY
FRIEND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Pray, do not worry, Mabel, though
You have, at times, been called a “sport.”
Ignore dull critics of the quaint,
Wayback Elizabethan sort.
Be careful of your looks, my dear,
And you may smile at Chadband’s blame.
Though through your dress your curves appear,
From me no censure need you fear ;
For skirts that cause a cry of “shame”
The perfect figure oft proclaim.
Whom the “boys” love are always young
And fair, this being as it should ;
So be you beautiful, dear girl,
And let whoever will be good !
Ma Grundy’s pain is Percy’s bliss;
She wins the gay who shocks the staid.
The “sport” we ; un’t afford to miss;
So stay. And please ■'emember this,
That Modesty, my gentle maid,
Is but Immodesty afraid !
VICTORIA.
HARRISON O.
THE DERELICTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Melbourne Argus points out many shocking possi-
bilities of a system of appointing Australian Gover-
nors. Ruined by the luxury and indolence of the
vice-regal office, the men would decline to return to
any menial task. They would become bankrupts,
perhaps “guinea-pigs”; but most assuredly the dis-
carded Governor would be a wretched creature for
whom the couritry could have no conceivable use.
’Tis well this possibility
Is brought right home to all of us.
Oh, what a shocking thing to see
One who has stood for good King G.
Reduced to be a common cuss,
With broken clothes and battered hat,
Who begs a copper on the mat,
Or follows some mere butcher fhr
To cadge the butt of his cigar!
Victoria’s democratic son
If set in Tommy Fuller’s seat,
When his vice-regal course is run
Will be no use to anyone,
And may become a wretched “beat.”
No kind of work would suit his whim;
Whatever could we do with him —
Unless we sent him, Crusoe style,
To finish on a desert isle?
The country will be much annoyed,
If we this foolish scheme commence,
With one new class of unemployed
In whom incentive is destroyed
And nothing’s left but decadence!
Poor refugees from Gov’ment House
Turned off sans energy and nous
To start as nobodies anew—
As oft Imperial Governors do !
VICTORIA.

“Bloore”: It seems to me that “the Ser-
vice” must be going to the dogs, for I under-
stand that another British naval officah is
to do the necessary pen-pushing for the South
Sydney Hospital; and I ask, Whaffor? Why
this indecent haste to retire from “the finest
service in the world, sir!” with its super-
Dreadnoughts and its entree to Government
House, to take on the recording of minutes
and the supervision of hot-water services in
suburban hospitals? If it is the screw, then
what, in all conscience, can Captain Fitz-
herbert be receiving as chief of 11.M.5.
Horrible, and what must his understrappers!
the navigating lootenant and the torpedo
expert, be getting that they hanker after
these tadpole shore-billets! But the queer
part of the arrangement is that, whilst any
old person is considered capable of taking
charge of hospital finances and general ad-
ministration, a matron must graft for half
a lifetime and pass all sort of exams, in
midwifery, obstetrics, sanitation, etc., and
then achieve a possible (but very improb-
able) £l5O a year!

“Petrol” : The news that the Dutch packet
steamers plying Between Australia and
Java have, for commercial reasons, changed
back from oil fuel to coal makes pleasant
reading for mariner folk. Sailor men have
never taken kindly to oil, for various reasons,
one being that perils were thick enough
already. Mineral oil is dangerous stuff,
with a bad habit of giving off a colorless
vapor of a highly inflammable nature. Hence
its prohibition as cargo in passenger ships.
As to the real nature 6f this danger, witness
the fate of the British s.s Twingone while
unloading case oil in an Asiatic port last
September. She had discharged a large
quantity of oil in cases and drums into a
lighter alongside, when a sling broke and
a case fell on top of the cargo in the ligjiter.
Everything blazed up at once, and every-
body on both lighter and steamer did the
one thing possible—took a header into the
shark-infested harbor. All hands were picked
up with the exception of the first and second
engineers, the captain’s three-year-old
daughter and nurse, the port-officer’s nine-
year-old son, a fireman in the stokehold and
a few natives. Eyewitnesses reckon it was
only four seconds from the time the sling
broke until the lighter was in flames, and
no more than four minutes before the
Twingone was enveloped. The terrible
rapidity with which the flames spread was
due to the way the vapor from the oil had
permeated the ship.

“Royal Blue’s” pained remarks: —
Lister Lister has been awarded the Wynne prize
(1913) for the best painting of an Australian subject
by an Australian artist. The value of the prize this
year is £33 ss. (the interest on a bequest of
£1000), and the winner lifts the pool with his
picture of Canberra. This is the fifth time Lister
has collected the money, and the consequent dissatis-
faction has something to commend it. The truth is
the allocation of the Wynne prize is in the wrong
hands to inspire confidence. The trustees of the
N. S. Wales Gallery are estimable citizens —good
traders, politicians, and so on. Also, there
are two artists on the Board, the principal
one being Lister Lister, the other being G.
V. F. Mann, the secretary. Now, what a
lot of people want to know is, did the traders,
etc., convince Lister that his picture of
Canberra was the best Australian subject produced
this year, or did Lister convince the traders? I say
unhesitatingly that I do not believe Lister had any-
thing to do with it. That leaves the responsibility
to be shared by the secretary and the traders. And
it s a responsibility that calls for, at least, a more
critical foundation.

There was a touch of grim humor —or was
it womanly sentiment? —about Melbourne’s
deceased baby-farmer, Isabella Newman.
Hanging on the wall of the dilapidated fowl-
house, just over the spot where the police
dug up two baby bodies, was a withered
bunch of flowers —pansies and forget-me-
nots !
“Gerald Baker”: A
heap of money is being
spent on naval bases, and
I want to know why they
are all situated in the
southern half, of Austra-
lia. Trouble must come
by-and-bye, and it is a
hundred to one on the
Brown People starting it
in the North; yet the
fleet seems to be for ever
having a good time in the
South. When trouble
comes, are the ships sup-
posed to scorch north-
wards and thread their
way at breakneck speed
through the disgracefully-lighted
Barrier Reef? There’s going to
be some fighting, anyhow, and in
case of partial disablement, neces-
sitating dry-docking, have they to
come back to Sydney or Jervis
Bay (if it is ready), or go right
round and down to Cockburn
Sound? Surely a naval base
should be constructed on the north
coast and made as impregnable as
human ingenuity can do it. What’s
wrong with Port Darwin, where
there’s a fine harbor, which will
be situated at the northern ter-
minus of the north-to-south trans-
continental railway—-when it is
built? Brown Brother might at-
tack one of the big cities in the
South, but he would find it a
million times harder to hold on to
anything there than he would to
occupy and keep the at present
empty but fertile void that lies
practically unprotected in the North.
“Clay Face” : The painting of Australian
pictures and portraits of representative
Australians for the Commonwealth is out of
the hands of Australians and in the hands
of the wee Scotch Andrew Fisher. Hugh
Patterson (who is also Scotch and a friend
of Ahndrew’s) and Meldrum practically have
the disposal of the jobs, and the result is
very Glaswegian indeed. Hopetoun and
Tennyson were not Australians, but pil-
grims and strangers; and. in any case, one
Henry Parkes or John Forrest or William
Lyne or J. C. Watson is of more importance
to Australian history than a ton of Gover-
nor-Generals. The selection of artists
amounts, too, to gross favoritism. Any Aus-
tralian artist who has made good should
have a chance, and the wider the experience
of portrait art so gained the better. Mel-
drum has already had three commissions,
and is understood to express his belief that
some more will find their way to him. What
justification is there for sending back to
Phillips Fox his portrait of Fisher, or for
turning-down Ambrose Patterson, who is
good enough for Brussels gallery, anyhow?
Last May, when the “Liberals” were doing
their best to decry Labor throughout the
country, a loud song was made about the
alleged increase of strikes in the Common-
wealth under Labor rule. Now Statistician
Knibbs, to whom it doesn’t matter whether
a Labor or a Liberal Government is in
office, hands in a few brief but suggestive
figures concerning industrial disputes during
the first three quarters of this year. The
first quarter of 1913 saw 39 disputes in the
Commonwealth ; the second 49 and the third
60. And the second quarter was passed
partly under a Liberal Government, the
third wholly. These statisticians are a
nuisance.
“Front Garden”: Whisky is not my
hobby, but I live in N. S. Wales, and voted
in the top square and gave the Demon
Drink a leg up. It was the way the local
publicans worked the campaign that got
me. One night, on coming home, I found
that the Demon or his understudy had paid
a call and left a neat little booklet filled
with interesting historical details of the
few pubs in my electorate. The publicans
themselves, splendid fellows without excep-
tion, were so highly spoken of that I felt
sorry I had not more time to drink for them.
Whoever wrote that booklet wrote it with
tears streaming down his pen while his
bosom swelled with emotion. He could
hardly bear to say what would happen to
the district if these noble characters were
driven out, and he sobbed for a whole page
when he spoke of the possible drying-up of
the local bowling green. It did me good to
read it, and I stowed it away in my pocket.
Next evening I found the No-license der-
vishes had thrown a handful of unpleasant
leaflets amongst my struggling plants and
converted the place into a sort of intellec-
tual rubbish tip. The untidy leaflets, as
well as being unpleasant, were full of insult-
ing suggestions. I was given credit 'for
about 3d. worth of will power and 2d. worth
of decency. It appeared that I was running
a deadly risk. The fact that beer was avail-
able on six days per week four miles off was
threatening my whole life. A dreadful
drawing of a hopeless drunk lying in a
gutter was appended* just to show me what
would become of me' if I didn’t hurry the
curse out of the district. I burnt the in-
human, joyless stuff in- the back yard. The
militant teetotaller is what the boxing re-
porter would call “an unattractive fighter.”

Swimming further out into the placid sea
of Socialism, the Prahran and Malvern
Tramways Trust (Vic.) is engaged in the
construction of yet another ele’ctric bar-line,
this time from Malvern to Glen Iris. The
latest enterprise provides "Sufficient proof
that the Trust’s previous tramway-experi-
ments have been a success. Several Glen
Iris and Malvern East landholders have
particular reason to rise up and call the
Trust blessed, and to hail Socialism as a
pal. One valuable lot of land at the ter-
minus of the new line belonged to the late
Bill Knox, and was sold the for
big money. Another nice area is owned by
Sydney Stott, the shorthand expert, who got
it at a bargain ; while the recent sale of cer-
tain tramway-blest landscape has greatly
benefited a Stott neighbor, Miss Robinson, a
lady who is known throughout the dairy
sections of the State .as a breeder of the
beast which keeps Little Willie Backblocks
from oversleeping himself. The unearned
increment on these three areas would be
enough to keep the Knoxes, Stotts and Miss
Robinson in motor-cars for some time;
wherefore it seems strange that, in all its
Socialistic tramway-construction projects,
the Trust has never sought powey (if it
hasn’t got it already) to buy up land along
the proposed tramway-routes prior to start-
ing work. This would spread the financial
blessings around among all the ratepayers
who meet the bill for the car-lines, instead
of giving the advantage to a mere handful
of them.

BOATING ON THE UNLIT YARRA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Coroner: I went up the river on Thursday
night. There are sharp turns and shoals and other
dangers. To a person who does not know the river
it is a series of pitfalls. —lnquest report.
The Yarra winds among the hills,
And when night closes down
The flannelled Totties, Kates and Bills
Row, singing, back to town.
There is no light to show the way
That darkly twists and twirls;
It’s tangled badly in the day ;
At night it’s like Jane’s curls.
The willows comb the inky stream
That flows without a sound ;
And, merged into the river’s dream,
The black hills wait around.
No one aboard knows where to turn—
Unless it is the chap
Who sits enraptured in the stern
With girls upon his lap.
Impatient rower, ask him not
Which way the river goes,
For he has other cares, God wot —
Plump curves and silken hose.
Oh, there are sudden shrieks of fear
When something scrapes the planks;
But when the rattling skiff glides clear,
There’s just a sigh of thanks.
It’s all right when the boatshed light
Shines clear for all to see,
And with a straight and silent flight
The boat glides home. “Coo-ee!”
White dresses on the landing ramp;
Young laughter, clear and sweet;
A hired boat rolling by the lamp
When spurned by shoreward feet.
It’s fine when all who rowed away
Elude the shoals and snags,
But dismal if, at dawn next day,
The police are out with drags.
N.S.W.
CHARON.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
With Harry Levien out of the N.S.W.
Assembly, Solemn Lee, of Tenterfield, will
be the father of the House. He was elected
in October, 18S4, and hasn’t been disturbed
since.
Died at Geraldton (W.A.), aged 60, J. M.
Finnerty, first goldfields warden of Westralia
—a man whose hard common-sense overcame
many difficulties in the early days of Cool-
gardie. His father was a brevet-major and
staff-officer of Imperial pensioners at Fre-
mantle.
OPPOSITION DEPUTY-
LEADER W. H. WOOD
(N.S.W.), defeated on
Saturday.
Passed out in London Lewis H. Berens,
long-time of South Aus., an ardent single-
taxer and advocate of land reform. He wrote
“The Story of My Dictatorship,” a single-
tax argument in dialogue form that has been
translated into many foreign tongues, be-
sides much lighter stuff.
Died, at Hotspur, on the S.A.-Vic. border,
Duncan McEachern, 78, a man who loved a
fight when he was young. He lost several
toes from frostbite in the Crimea, but this
did not keep him from taking a share ill the
American Civil War a little later, and he
drew Uncle Sam’s pension till his death.
Samuel Toombs, who dug Varney Parkes’s
political grave at Hurstville, is a master
mariner, and has for some years been dodg-
ing up and down the coast in various small
packets. If Sam Toombs speaks up for the
sailormen as effectively as did Sam Smith,
whose memory is hallowed in every fo’c’stle
on the coast, he will earn his halo.
J. G. Aikman, M.L.C., the Melbourne
business man who is alleged to have been
systematically robbed by an employee of
£15,000, made a famous spec, when he pur-
chased the R.M.S. Australia, wrecked 20
years ago at Port Phillip Heads. He bought
the marine ruins at auction for a mere
trifle, and aided by smart workmen., fine
weather and smooth seas, he was able to
extract the innards of the stranded liner be-
fore he knocked off work. Estimates as to
his winnings on the deal ranged from £20,000
to £40,000.
Jones, of Paddington, again achieved dis-
tinction at the recent N. S. Wales elections.
With 25 votes, he was, with the exception of
a Belmore candidate who retired at 11.30,
and therefore wasn’t fairly in the competi-
tion, the candidate with the fewest votes
recorded in his favor. The only Jones has
achieved the same distinction several times,
once with three votes ; and the fact that he
has, since then, increased his following by
833 per cent, indicates that if he continues
long enough he may secure a winning num-
ber yet.
If bakers ever appear at the Arbitration
Court and assert, in the fashionable way,
that their occupation is unhealthy, they will
probably be confronted with the record of
James Wardley, presently 92 years young,
and just appointed president of the Victorian
Operative Bakers’ Society. Wardley baked
the daily bread of many Australians for 58
years, and prior to that performed the same
useful work for fellow-countrymen in Eng-
land for 20 years. He is not a teetotaller,
nor is he a non-smoker, and can offer no
explanation for the fact that he is a sur-
vivor from the first days of George IV.
Of course, the Australian can never be a
seaman. It’s not his line, as everybody
knows. Take George A. Whitton, jun. Hav-
ing arrived at the age of 21 the other day,
he wandered up wearily to the examiners
(he had been compelled by the regulations
to wait a considerable time until he reached
his majority) and obtained an extra
master’s certificate. This is the highest dis-
tinction a sailor-man can attain by examina-
tion ; and in honor of the occasion the
owners of the barque Daniel, commanded by
Whitton, sen., brought the “old man”
ashore for a trip and handed the sailing
orders to the youngest skipper trading out
of Port Jackson. It’s all very discouraging.
From the Oldest Inhabitant: —
Apropos “Mad Tom” Davey, Lieutenant-Governor
of Van Diemen’s Land (B. 11/12/’l3). “Mad Tom”
had been a “First Fleeter” at Botany Bay. He was
then a lieutenant of marines under the wrangling
Major Ross, the man who said it would be cheaper
to feed convicts on turtle soup and venison at the
London Tavern than keep them in Botany Bay. Tom
had risen to be a colonel when he was nominated
Lieutenant-Governor. He had seen a good deal oi
service, and had fought at the Battle of Trafalgar.
When his appointment reached him he was a
prisoner for debt in London. The house in which
he was detained, called by some “a sponging house,”
was kept by a Mr. Beaumont, who paid his debts
in order that he might accept the offer. In return
Davey took Beaumont’s son with him, promising to
make a “squire” of him; and he kept his promise.
His favorite Hobart pub, by the way, was the Union,
on the corner of Liverpool and Campbell streets.
Holman’s energy is magnificent. On
Friday night, December 13, having raced
round the suburban constituencies, without
waiting to hear the result of the poll he
started out for Bega. having mapped out a
seven-day tour, talking in Bega, Albury,
Bingara, Tamworth and Goff’s Harbor —it-
self a huge contract. It usually takes two
days to get to Bega; but Holman, with a
strenuous drive in the State motor-car,
reached there on Saturday evening. He
got his reward in being able to announce to
his audience the defeat of W. 11. Wood. The
same night, when the reporters went to look
for Wade, they found that strenuous
politician in the front row of the stalls at a
city theatre.
Of the new men in the N.S.W. Parlia-
mentary Labor party .Tabez Wright, of
Willy ama, is the most experienced by a
long chalk. Wright would probably shock
Willy Holman if ever he got to Government
House and Willy happened to be there too;
but, as a set-off against that, he has had
over 25 years of Labor organising, and was
the first Labor mayor, appointed by a
straight-out party of Labor aldermen, in
N. S. Wales, if not in Australia. That was
at Broken Hill. Jabez, who, though he has
been burying people for quarter-century,
couldn’t spell “cemetery” to save his life,
is, nevertheless, an effective debater; and
the Labor Government which cuts from the
Labor platform is liable to have more
trouble from its new “supporter” than ever
Wade knew how to give it.
PROTECTING THE MALES.
At the meeting of the Mirboo North branch of the A.N.A. several members protested against the
onesidedness of the Bill introduced by Mr. McLachlan in the Assembly, to raise the age of consent to
18 years. It was contended that males should have had some protection. A motion to that effect was
carried. — Argus.
Had Mirboo North been in a position to have a say in the matter , poor Adam would
have had an opportunity of reaching the age of consent before the Fall , and all this trouble
might have been averted.
Mathew Leighton Hutchinson, an old Mel-
bourne bookseller, passed out a few days
back in his 84th year and the 60th year of
his residence in Australia. He started book-
selling in Elizabeth-street, and then bought
out Robert Mackay in Collins-street West,
where he remained some 21 years. After-
wards be moved into Little Collins-street,
where his sons still carry on the business.

A Maoriland

sion of the Red
Fed. organiser.
The recent political try-out in N. S. Wales
promises one good result: the return of
Percy Colquhoun for Mosman will dim the
spotlight hitherto thrown upon the athletic
pre-eminence of C. G. Wade. Apart from
his Mount Iveinbla performance, Wade’s
claim to athletic distinction rests mainly on
the fact that he was about the first Aus-
tralian to represent Oxford and England in
Rugby union football. Harry Vassall, sen-
ior, whose name is a household word
in English football, has left it on record in
his book on the game that Wade relied
chiefly on his “bumping” abilities for suc-
cess. Colquhoun, on the other hand, was
one of the most skilful and resourceful
“centres” that Australia has produced, with
a knack of saving the situation for his side.
Moreover, he has been a front-ranker in
cricket and lawn tennis, and is one in golf.
Among the burly men in the Senate,
Barnes, of Victoria, is a prominent figure.
Barnes left one good billet, where he had
to work hard, for a better one, where so
far the toil has not been of a nature to
give him corns on the brain. Organiser
first for the A.W.U. in the West Darling
country, and then secretary of the Vdc.-
Riverina branch, he reached the Senate in
spite of a severe handicap. This was the
circulation by the Vic. P.L.C., in tens of
thousands, of a leaflet containing an alleged
portrait of Barnes as one of the “Senate
three.” It represented him as a slim, neat,
small-faced youth of about 20, with a
faint moustache. In the flesh, Barnes is a
broad and upstanding Australian
with a hard and cheerful dial, a
quiet manner and a square jaw—
a fine type of the bushman who is
the salt of the Labor movement in
Australia. Barnes has a large
personal acquaintance with his
own country and his own people,
and is well able to barrack on the public
plutform for both.
Tom Tunnecliffe, who somewhat unexpect-
edly failed to get into the first Vic. Labor
Ministry, was passed over chiefly on account
of the fact that, although his services to
Labor date back into the cobwebbed past,
his actual Parliamentary career has been a
short one. Heaven tempers the wind, how-
ever. Latterly Tunnecliffe has become some-
thing of a bloated landlord, having built
a brace of villas at Gardiner, a semi-ruraj
suburb on Ivooyongkoot Creek, 20 minutes’
rail from the city. This, of course, is much
better than spending his £3OO, like some
Parliamentary Liberals have done in their
time, on various brands of joy; but it gives
Liberalism a chance to ask him, sourly, why
he doesn’t live on £2 a week and give the
balance to a hospital. The Liberal landlord
never did like to see anybody else collecting
rents—any more than the hog likes to share
rations wflth his brother. .
CHRISTMAS RUBAIYAT OF MARY ANN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wake! for the maid has ta’en her lonely flight
And laid upon this dusty house a blight.
Housekeeping is a curse when Christmas comes
And calls her home with relatives to fight.
Before the shadow of the Christmas goose
She banged about morosely. Said, “Th’ jooce!”
When asked to do the things we paid her for,
And seemed to find her broom of little use. -
The stairs whereon she, working, used to sing
And many a gibe at passing children fling,
Are silent now. For, with her faded box
She scuttled out—a tyrant on the wing.
Lord only knows why at the glad year’s end
She should so want her homeward way to wend,
And part forever from the housewife who
Has only tried to be a paying friend.
Some say she can’t the homely suburbs bear,
And longs to be where boarders beat the air,
And gossip rages through the fainting hours,
And young men lounge with tightly-plastered hair.
Oh! wash the cup and by the fire of gas
Tell not her sins, nor call her fool and ass,
But give her time to spend her little hoard ;
She may return, more treasure to amass.
N.S.W.
J. DISHRAGGE.

This office has been making rapid progress during
the last few years, and the movement is all the
more remarkable because it had made hardly any
at all for very many years previously. The manage-
ment in the old days was tremendously respectable,
and still more tremendously stodgy. In these days
it adds hustle to its respectability, and goes in for
making the best display of its stock. Take the
rapid growth of income, for instance, in both the
life departments. Insurance policies may be roughly
divided into two classes —(1) those that don’t
mature till the death of the policy-holder (life
policies) ; (2) those that are payable at a given age
or earlier death (endowment policies). The life
policy is the truer insurance instrument; it is, in
fact, the only one with which the young married
man or the young man who hopes soon to be married
should have any truck, presuming that he is a person
of small means. But, though the life policy is the
truer insurance instrument, an endowment policy of
the same size brings in more money to the insurance
office. The endowment premiums are necessarily
larger, because there are fewer of them; moreover,
the canvasser can make an appeal to the selfishness
of the man he is angling for; and, finally, it is
quite legitimate to take for a short-term endowment
a life that is much too poor for a whole-life policy.
One way and another, therefore, though endowment
business is poorer insurance than the other, it is easier
to get; and, consequently, it is easier to pile up an
income by specialising in that than in the other
sort. And the Temperance and General has special-
ised in that sort. These figures show the various
kinds of policies existing in 1910 and 1913 in the
Ordinary department:—
1910. 1913. Increase.
Whole life .. . . 2,943 3,018 75
Endowment insurance . . 15,087 17,883 2796
Endowments . . . . 16,172 19,346 3174
.Thus while the T. and G. has only 75 more whole-
life policies than it had three years ago, it has 5970
more endowment and endowment insurance policies.
Endowments considerably exceed the other sort in
the Industrial department also.
But while it is possible to make a better show by
specialising in endowment business, it is exceedingly
. improbable that a society which does this will get
- the best advantage from the fine mortality experi-
ence of Australian offices. In other words, the
. smaller the proportion of whole-life business an Aus-
tralian office does, the poorer will its bonuses be,
, other things being equal. Bonuses come from three
.sources: (1) better interest earnings than those
. assumed when the premium tables were drafted; (2)
■ lower expenses than were assumed when the tables
. were drafted; (3) a longer average life than it was
. assumed that the policy-holders would live. But
obviously the Australian’s longevity is of no advan-
tage to the T. and G. if it has paid him out at 45
. or 55 or 60 under an endowment policy. The office
which has issued an endowment payable at 60 to a
man of 30 can’t get more than 30 premiums, whereas
the Australian office which issues a whole-life policy
at 30, expecting a man to live then years, may
easily go on getting extra premiums from him for
10 or 15 years ; and those extra premiums all mean
, extra bonuses for the whole body of shareholders.
There were doubts once about some of the T. and
■ Ul.’s tables of premiums. It was believed that they
were less than they ought to be; in plain language,
it was doubted whether the society could pay what
it had undertaken to pay. There probably are actu-
aries who wouldn’t feel comfortable if they had to
work on those tables even now; still there is no
doubt that the position has improved. For one
thing, the expense rate is much better than it used
to be, especially in the Industrial department; and
it was the Industrial tables especially that were
under suspicion. Here are some figures: —
Expenses Compared with Total Income.
Sept., Ordinary. Industrial. Accident.

Barring the Accident branch, the figures are really
very good, because the new business is very solid;
and it is new business that runs away with the
money. On any accepted basis of calculation, the
Ordinary new business is now costing well under 75
p.c. of the first-year’s premiums, which is a very
excellent performance indeed. The one qualification
that must be made is that this co. does not appar-
ently include all its rates and taxes among its ex-
penses, but deducts some of them from its income
from property ; and this, while reducing the rate of
interest earnings, cuts a bit also off the expense rate.
Still, making allowance for that, the expense rates
have been improved almost out of recognition. They
are now almost as respectable as the old officials
used to be. Probably, if it came to choosing, the
policy-holders would rather have respectable accounts
than respectable accountants. The figures in the
Accident department are still, however, ghastly.
And things have been helped also by the improve-
ment in the interest earnings:—

The insurance funds now amount to £1,524,425, and
every shilling per cent, more earned on that lot is
another £750. Of the total assets, public (including
municipal) securities represent a moderate 36 p.c.,
mortgage loans 36 p.c., and freeholds 12* p.c.
Apparently a pretty fair lot, though there is too
much money in property.
The society has_ had its annual actuarial valuation.
In the Ordinary 'department there is a surplus of
£35,256, of which £21,280 is distributable = 11.8
p.c. of the year’s premium income. In the Industrial
department a small amount is available also for
reversionary addition to policies in force five years
or over. The mortality experience is reported to be
highly favorable, especially in the Temperance sec-
tion. The figures are certainly striking:—

But that isn’t conclusive, of course; what one also

The Australian Provincial Assurance
Association Ltd. (Sydney).— This co of which at least
three prospectuses have been issued, began business
on March 23, 1912. None of the prospectuses are
Hatod • but according to what seems to be the sec-
ond the authorised capital is £lOO,OOO in 200,000
shares of 10s. each. Originally 100 000 shares were
offered for subscription, 10,000 fully paid and
£lOOO cash went to the promoter, and 90,000 were
held in reserve. According to a subsequent pro-
spectus, issued some time this year 40,000 were
then offered in Queensland and 40,000 we F e ,, held in
reserve; it isn’t plain what is to become of the other
10 000, unless the promoter takes them too. Accord-
ing to the accounts now published, covering the
period from March 23, 1912 to October 31, 1913
8253 fully paid shares have been issued and 93,69,4
contributing shares; but the allotment money has
not been paid on all the shares. The paid capital on
October 31 was £25,218. Of this money the co
still held as at that date £8876. The other £16,342
is accounted for as follows: —
Preliminary expenses .. •• £ 6.448
Excess outgoings over revenue . . 9,894
Total . . • • £16,342
The position is even then not made clear. Pre-
liminary exes, are set down at £8059, less £l6ll
written off =£6448. But it is not plain whether
that £8059 includes the whole amount payable to
the promoter. It is also not clear whether the pre-
liminary exes, are the expenses of getting business
or of selling shares, or both. The prospectus —one
of the numerous prospectuses —said that an agree-
ment had been made whereby Benjamin Fowler Smith
“agrees to pay all preliminary costs, including adver-
tising, legal expenses, printing, stationery and post-
age down to the date on which the Association com-
menced business, except brokerage and underwriting
commission,” in consideration of the 10,000
shares and £lOOO cash before mentioned. The direc-
tors, according to prospectus No. 1, could
proceed to allotment on receiving applications for
2000 shares, and “commence business.” Thereafter
the co. would bear all the expense of forming the
co. The promoter, in return for his 10,000 shares
and £lOOO cash, was not therefore under any obliga-
tion to do very much. The Bulletin _is
unable to find any services he performed which
should entitle him to anything.
Anyhow, this is the position at present. The
paid capital is £25,218, and at the end of the first
period—the “annual report” covers 19 months —■
there is £8876 left; and part of that is in pre-
miums outstanding (which the co. may or may not
collect) and “bills receivable” (what is the co. doing
with bills at this time of its life?) and stationery
(which probably has no value apart from the busi-
ness). But let it be understood that the co. hasn’t
got £8876 net. Against that it has numerous policy
contracts. It says it has issued policies assuring
£251,300, and is very proud of the performance.
Big policy figures are pleasant, no doubt; but big
r-olicv figures mean big policy liabilities. So the
position is that this co. has issued policies on
which, on maturity, it has undertaken to pay
£251,300, and it has £8876 towards meeting its
obligations. This paper isn’t, however, sure whether
the co. knows much about such things as the valua-
tion of liabilities. It has directors and local direc-
tors and provisional 'directors and managers and other
officers galore, but neither the prospectuses nor
the present accounts mention such a person as an
actuary. Who produced the tables under which this
co. is working? Who certifies that the premiums
charged will meet the policy obligations? Do the
directors know what they ought to have in hand at
the end of any particular term? What is their
assumed rate of interest?
The Bulletit doesn’t expect an insurance office to
show good figurtes at the end of a year, or even two
years. The cost of getting the thing established is
always heavy, a,nd this paper doesn’t expect the first
year’s premium). to pay the first year’s outgoings.
But it likes to see some method about a new con-
cern ; it likes to have some sort of assurance that
the new co. knows just where it is. And it doesn’t
get that assurance in' this case. Everything in con-
nection with the concern is sloppy, and almost every-
thing is unsatisfactory. The absence of any men-
tion of an actuary is one instance. If, as seems
highly probable, this co. has started without an
actuary to guide it, then the sooner the law prevents
a recurrence of it the better. The law will not
allow a ship to go to sea without a man who knows
something about navigation, and it should not allow
an insurance concern to put to sea either. True,
one takes life with it, and the other doesn’t; but it
takes the money which neople are putting by to see
them through their old age, or to feed their widows
and children when they are dead. This co. may, of
course, have an actuary, and the tables on which it
is working may be certified to by him, and his eye
may be upon the whole proceedings; but when he
isn’t mentioned, and such persons as business super-
visors and organising managers and district man-
agers are mentioned, it seems fair to assume that
he doesn’t exist. An'd until he does exist, and his
existence is made known, the insuring public ought
to know what to do with the Australian Provincial
Assurance Association, Ltd.

This little bank’s figures haven’t been affected much
by the stringency in the money market and the other
financial troubles that the world has had lately. In
the past 12 months its deposits, both free and fixed,
have moved up a trifle; and it has let out a little
more money ; and it has a few more pounds in liquid
assets in its till. It doesn’t ask for deposits outside
Tasmania, and it has no branches away from its own
small island; and the Balkan situation isn’t of
nearly so much importance to it as the price of
potatoes.
As mentioned just now, there has been a small
increase in almost every line during the past 12
months:—-
N0v.,’12. N0v.,’13.
Notes in circulation £6,012 £4,998 Dec. £1,014
Bills, etc 14,833 21,501 Inc. 6,668
Deposits at interest . . 316,427 328,004 Inc. 11,577
Free deposits.. .. 324,947 333,765 Inc. 8,818
Liquid assets.. .. 190,403 199,007 Inc. 8,604
Advances . . . . 665,609 689,371 Inc. 23,762
Premises, etc. . . 28,272 28,199 Dec. 73
Add up the liabilities of all sorts and you will find
that the bank owes £688,268, while its own funds
(capital and reserves) amount to £221,222. So of
other people’s money in the business it has very little
more than £3 for every £1 of its own. This is a
very small proportion of liabilities; and that is
another way of saying that the public’s money is
unusually well protected. There are many banks
in which £1 of shareholders’ money has to protect
anything from £6 to £8 of the public’s. The weak
point lies in the comparatively small amount of
assets held liquid. On current account alone the
bank has £333,765; yet though this money may
presumably be called for at any time, the liquid
assets (coin, cash balances and Australian notes)
amount to only £199,007. Under 30 p.c. of the
total liabilities could be met without realising on
something or calling up overdrafts, etc. ; and it isn’t
enough, especially in such times as these, when the
most serious things are liable to happen the most
suddenly.
The interest earnings calculated on capital and
reserves combined are very fair: —
May, 1909.. £8 7 2p.c.iNov., 1911. . £6 15 2p.c.
Nov., 1909.. 7 12 2 „ May, 1912.. 8 6 7 „
May., 1910.. 7 19 11 „ Nov., 1912.. 7 7 8 „
Nov., 1910.. 7 16 10 „ May, 1913.. 7 19 11 „
May, 1911.. ,7 16 4 ~ Nov, 1913.. 7 19 8 „
Doubtless the fact that the bank has so small a hold-
ing of bullion, etc., helps the profits; all the same,
the bank which keeps a larger sum liquid, and thus
holds itself in readiness to assist customers in a
crisis, is likely to do better in the long run. Whether
the Tasmanian thinks much of the matter yet Thf.
Bulletin doesn’t know; but in other places, any-
how, the customer begins to suspect the bank which
trades to the last possible sixpence in good. times,
and then has to call in overdrafts in dull times in
order to keep its own position sound.
The Marshall Shoe Co. (Melb.) took
over a boot-manufacturing business established four
years previously. The deficit to start with was made
up this way: Goodwill, £7500; preliminary exes.,
£892; total, £8392. Less renewal and contin-
gencies reserves, £5320. Net deficit, £3072. Sub-
sequent history follows: —
Towards De-
Profits. Dividends. Reserves, ficit.
July, 1911.. £3,072
Reserves.
June, 1912. . £15,029 8 p.c. = £*6367 £8662 5,590
June, 1913.. 14,475 8 „ „ 9416 5059 10,649
The capital is £121,500, in 24,000 8 p.c. cum. pref.
£1 shares and 97,500 Ordinary £1 shares, all fully
paid. For the goodwill of the business, as shown
above, £7500 was paid, in shares. -The goodwill—a
paper asset, of course —is now being written off. In
the foregoing table it has not been regarded as an
asset, the reserves as shown above being made up as
follows: —
Plant renewal account ..£13,000
Reserve fund . . . . • . 1,500
Unappropriated profit. . , . 1,149
Total reserves . . . . £15,649
Less intangible assets.. •• 5,000
Net reserves . . . . £10,649
If anything, that rather paints the lily. For this
reason: Instead of writing down the value of the
plant, the directors are building up a renewal fund,
and that fund is included with reserves. Hence part
at any rate of the reserves represents actual deprecia-
tion. For instance, if 7+ p.c. should be written off
plant, fittings and rolling stock to represent the
year’s wear and tear, then the actual profit in the
last 12 months was £11,398 instead of £14,475,
and the reserves are less than £10,649 by the amount
by which the plant, etc., has depreciated since the co.
took it over. The Bulletin doesn’t know for certain
whether 7 h p.c. is a fair thing to write
off for wear and tear; for no two plants
wear out or become obsolete at exactly the same
rate. Twenty per cent, isn’t too big an annual
charge in some cases. In this instance, how-
ever, 7$ p.c. all round is probably quite enough.
This would still leave the co. with a profit for the
past year of £1982 in excess of the 8 p.c. dividend,
which is a fair margin, though nothing at all out of
the way considering the country’s prosperity, and the
fact that it is very much easier to buy boots when you
are borrowing money than it is when you have
“blewed” all the borrowed money and are struggling
to pay the wretched interest.
When the dull time comes this co. will, at any
rate, have the advantage of having precious little
debt of its own to pay interest on —-assuming, of
course, that the present policy is maintained. On
all accounts its liabilities at the end of its year were
onlv £10,090, and most of that was represented by
ordinary trade debts. Better still, close on £lO,OOO
has been added to the plant and about £6OOO to
the freehold premises, etc., during the year without
going to the bank, the trick being done partly by
getting in some unpaid balances on shares and partly
by reducing stocks. The latter, however, still stand
at £48,041. Further, there isn’t, as far as this paper
is aware, any contingent liability represented by roy-
alty payable on plant. Marshall’s is one of the very
few factories in Australia which are free from the
Shoe Trust —the American concern which charges the
manufacturer for installing plant and then collects
a thumping royalty on its use: in some cases it is
alleged that the installation fee plus the first year’s
royalty is actually greater than the original cost of
the machine. A while ago Marshall’s was completely
free of the Trust, and presumably it is so still; and
that ought to be a fact of much consequence when
dull times come and competition is keener than it has
been in these latter days when the trouble has been
not to find buyers but to get operatives to turn out
the goods fast enough.
The Dubbo Co-operative Flour Milling
