Milling—country milling particularly—is partly a
business, and largely a gamble. The miller to make
sure of his supplies mostly has to buy early; and
sometimes that means profit, but often it means loss.
If the wheat market improves after the harvest, then
flour moves up to more or less correspond, and the
miller is in clover ; if the other thing happens he is
in the soup. For the past few years he has been
oftener in the soup than in the other place. How-
ever, unless he is very green at the game, he knows
what a tricky sort,of business he is in; so when he
makes his punch he puts by most of it, and simply
draws on his funds when the market goes against
him, and prays that the funds will hold out and see
him through. This co.’s paid capital is £11,389,
and in spite of numerous poor years there are still
reserves of £8842, so shareholders can stand a
bump or two yet. And quite likely they won’t have
to stand a bump this year; anyhow, the market for
new wheat has opened low enough, and though of
course it may go lower there is plenty of room for
it to move upwards. As at September 30 the co.
owed the bank £4782 ; but probably what it owes in
September isn’t much of a guide to what it owes
in February' or March, after it has done most of its
year’s buying. For an elderly lady with a weak
heart and no experience in the vagaries of wheat-
growing and wheat-dealing, The Bulletin wouldn't
advise investment in a milling co. But for a man
who likes a little excitement with his money-making,
there are worse things to consider, though it is cer-
tainly probable that he will find better profits await-
ing equal risks elsewhere.

For the past half-year £lBOl of profit has been made

bonuses are appropriated after the annual balance in
March. Profits have shrunk a trifle, notwithstanding
a heavier turnover; lower prices (and therefore
smaller commission earnings) are no doubt responsi-
ble. The directors report that, under instructions
from a conference instigated by North Coast fac-
tories, a committee is drafting a scheme for the
amalgamation of the two butter-distributing cos. in
N. S. Wales. This is a good move, even if it isn’t
a very new one—for it isn’t the first time amalga-
mation between the Coastal Farmers and the Berrima
cos. has been proposed. Of the two cos. one is
wholly a co-operative distributor ; the other is mainly
a distributor, though it does a bit of manufacturing
as a side-line. So far as the distributing businesses
are concerned, they ought to be amalgamated, and
the factories are the right people to insist on it, for
it is either they or the public—their who
must pav for the quite unnecessary duplication of
offices and staffs. The Berrima’s factories may be a
small obstacle to amalgamation of the two businesses
as they stand ; but it should be quite a small matter
to transfer them to a separate manufacturing co. A
way will be found all right if the co-operative fac-
tories insist.

A great mistake, when erecting new works or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

Hoover Rapid Laboratory Sizer is unsurpassed. An

takes about 10 hours to complete, the Hoover Sizer

the use of one of these Sizers the saving in time
would, in a very short while, cover the small initial
cost of the machine. The machine can be dressed

Water-Supply engineers will be interested to learn
that further evidence proving the durability of W. I.
and Steel Pipes when coated by the Asphaltum pro-
cess, has just been obtained by the engineers of the
Melbourne and Metropolitan Board of Works. Certain
alterations were made to the main which supplies the
City of South Melbourne with water, and consequently
some pipes were taken up. These pipes were made in the
year 1889, and were coated with Asphaltum composi-
tion in the usual way, and they were laid in ground
that was particularly unfavorable to the preservation
of any unprotected metal, being originally swampy
land, containing much vegetable matter, and filled
with tidal dredgings. On examination, these 24-year-
old pipes were found to be in an excellent state of
preservation, and the Board's engineers were so satis-
fied with their condition that they decided to use
them again. They were, therefore, removed to tht?
works of Messrs. Mephan Ferguson Proprietary Lim-
ited, at Footscray, Victoria, where these 24-year-old
pipes were cleaned, retested, and recoated with
Asphaltum composition in a new and improved pro-
cess. They are now being relaid in a position where
they will have to withstand a working pressure of
1351 b. to the square inch. That the Asphaltum pro-
cess for coating pipes is rust-proof and thoroughly
efficacious is proved by the foregoing.*

and with this the co. hopes to make a steady saving in
running costs, as well as an improvement in the
output. But, of course, it won’t lessen the gamble
connected with wheat-buying. Liquid assets (mostly
stock) stand in the books at £23,545, and plant,
buildings, etc., have been steadily written down to
£17,110; against that there are liabilities of £795.
So the position in that respect is excellent, though,
doubtless, when the season’s buying is done, the
bank account won’t be in credit, as it was for a
small amount at the end of November.
It is admitted now that the last lot of
£2,000,000 borrowed by the N. S. Wales Govern-
ment cost no less than £4 19s. sd. p.c.—or some-
thing well over 5 p.c. when etceteras never charged
against an issue are taken into account. The rail-
ways and tramways are the most profitable large
business undertakings of the State; yet even these
can’t show 5 p.c. Last year the railways gave
£3 15s. 2d. p.c., and the tramways £2 18s. lOd.
p.c.; and that, of course, included the returns from
the most profitable lines, the revenue of which was
boomed by the exceptional traffic resulting from the
Government’s big borrowing. It is quite safe, there-
fore, to say that there is not in view a single new
undertaking which can earn as much interest as the
Government has to pay for money at present; and
there are only two ways to make up the deficit —by
borrowing (which is dishonest, seeing that we should
be borrowing to pay interest) and by increasing the
people’s taxes. The Treasurer, making the best
defence he can, says that others are paying as much
as we are. But that is no answer at all. The ques-
tion is, even if borrowing is sometimes justified,
what sort of a fool is he who borrows at a time
when money is exceptionally dear? And the answer:
Exactly the same sort., as the indigent person
who chooses the time when strawberries are half-a-
sovereign a pound to buy those vegetables.
8., M. & Co. have taken on the tough con-
tract of general-managing Great Cobar, and George
Klug leaves for the mine this week.
Pellew Harvey, the engineer on the Great
Cobar Board who visited the mine lately, re-
ported to his co-directors that a change in manage-
ment was necessary. Bellenger had decided to quit in
any case. He made a fatal mistake at the outset:
probably not to give the shareholders too great a
shock, he didn’t write Kendall’s extravagant state-
ments down low enough, and held out hopes which
it has been utterly impossible to realise. And when
at the last meeting in London he was present and
didn’t make good, his cake was dough. London
directors like a manager who can talk prettily to
the shareholders now and then. Bellenger quite
unexpectedly was on that occasion like a sheep led
to the slaughter.
The overloaded Adelong Gold Estates
(N.S.W.), in which Melbourne heavyweights have
put more money than they care to think about, made
a loss of £2256 last half-year. Practically every
ounce of gold won cost £2 more than it was worth.
The co. is making heavy weather under a debt
(mostly debentures) of over £20,000, and there is
considerable outlay ahead in alterations to the dredg-
ing plant. The ground is poor—l.l2gr. per yard—-
and it will take mighty fine work to get down to 3d.
a yard, which must be done to show any profit at
all, unless the recovery is improved.
» * * *
Surprising items from a White Crystal,
Ardlethan, report, as published in the S. M. Herald
of 10th inst.: “Started to crosscut east Crossby’s sec-
tion, underlay still in hand mineralised country....A
most up-to-date and complete place for this mine
has been purchased at Macdon, Victoria. Share-
holders in the Carpathia and Wild Cherry mines will
be pleased.” Most people have heard, now and
again, of a gold reef being “hand mineralised” for
the benefit of prospective buyers, but to secure this
state of affairs with regard to “country” in a tin
show seems sheer waste of time. Then, again, surely
it is an error of judgment to purchase an “up-to-
date and complete place” for the mine at a spot like
“Macdon, Victoria.” Why go to the expense of shift-
ing the whole show all that distance when there are
plenty of splendid sites available nearer home ? Still,
it is reassuring to learn that “the shareholders in
the Carpathia and Wild Cherry mines will be
pleased.” Evidently they are supposed to know that
these shows want a change of air badly.
Thanks to high meal values and a brisk
coal trade, N. S. Wales mineral exports for the first
nine months of this year were worth £7,837,166. Lead
ore, concentrates, etc., stood for £2,631,929, coal for
£2,422,501, and copper ingots, ore, etc. (16,070
tons), £1,084,529.
Lloyd Copper, Burraga (N.S.W.). sus-
tained a loss of a few hundreds by a recent fire; in-
surance covered the rest. But the fortnightly reports
don’t give one the idea that copper is being won at
a profit. Concentrates produced at a ratio of 5 to 1,
and assaying 13.25 p.c., mean an average from crudes
of only 2,00 p.c. The concentrates lose copper in
the furnace slag, and the final yield is only a shade
over 11 p.c. At £6O per ton the metal recovered
per ton of ore is worth from 245. to 30s. There
must have been serious losses at the mill. Much of
the ore treated assayed 4 p.c. in the crude state, and
4 p.c. at £6O = 485., compared with 2.65 p.c., worth
325., recovered from the mill. After the holidays a
regrinding plant will be installed, and practicallv
all jig tailings and slimes with copper in them will
be run through the Minerals Separation plant.
Although it is certain that costs have been too high,
there may be a profit from old mill products, of
which there is a large quantity in stock.
Star Exploration, Ltd., has decided to
give up business in Westralia. At present its in-
terests there comprise an out-of-work residues plant
and big wads of shares in the Lake View and Star
and Mountain Queen. The co. finds that if it were
to declare a dividend, the bulk of which was earned
in other lands, it would have to pay the AVestralian
Government dividend dutv on the lot. On ascertain-
ing this, the Board decided to close up in Westralia
and open elsewhere. Apart from its Westralian in-
terests the co. has foreign tin investments, out of
which it netted a profit of nearly £5OOO last year.
It also had at the date of the last balance-sheet about
£20,000 in cash, and it valued its entire assets at
approximately £40,000.
The hauling of ore from No. IS level
through the internal shaft sunk from No. 13 level
will be started at the Great Fingall (W.A.) early m
1914. The lengths and value of the shoot m lie
deep levels of the mine are: No, 15 level, 140 ft..
average 435. 7d. : No. 16 level, 350 ft., average
395. 6d. ; No. 17 level, 300 ft., average 40s. ; No. 18
level, 400 ft., 465. 6d. These levels have all been
connected by winzes and rises. Altogether about
800 ft. of backs are available, and as bores and cross-
cuts have proved the reef in places to be wider than
the drives, the reserves amount to a substantial
figure.
Yuanmi’s two Westralian shows together
produced a revenue of £217,152 for the year ended
June 30. After allowing for all costs and deducting
over £48,000 for depreciation and development, a
surplus of £57,080 remained. In dividends
£43,750 (2s. 6d. per share) was disbursed, leaving
with income tax taken off a carry-forward balance of
£7495. At both mines a substantial reduction
was effected in working costs. The general man-
agers in their report state that at June 30 the ore
estimated to be in sight was 116,768 tons, of a
gross value of £214,070, and that there were some
13,000-odd tons of partly-visible sulphide ore, the
average value of which was estimated at between 235.
and 28s. per ton. The management claims that the
work so far done by the sulphide plant on the
Yuanmi lease indicates that there are no treatment
troubles to contend with.
Great Boulder (W.A.) is now thinking
over Dr. Maclaren’s suggestion that it should carry
the 2720 ft. level of the Ivanhoe further east to see
if there is anything worth having in its own
ground at that depth. There wasn’t much found in
that part of the Boulder up above, but as a sort of
last resource Maclaren’s advice is worth taking.
Mountain Queen (W.A.), which recently
applied for and obtained exemption on some of its
leases, backed up its request with some figures. Re-
venue to date, £83,901. Expended in wages, stores
and sundries, £57,172; on mine development,
£19,000; on plant, £25,000. A big amount was
also absorbed in experimenting and in working
options. It now has the Great Victoria on approval,
and on the 20th inst. it will either have to pay a
preliminary deposit of £lO,OOO or get out. The
full purchase consideration is said to be £lOO,OOO,
but the bulk of this is payable out of profits. Should
a deal be made a 10,000-tons-a-month plant has to
be working on the property within six months. The
probable cost of mining and treatment is estimated
at between Bs. and 95., and as no development ex-
penditure to speak of will be needed for years,
monthly profits are estimated at several thousands.
Sample of the inane stuff that is getting
into print anent Edna May (W.A.): “An intrusive
tongue of granite is showing, but does not appear to
have influenced the reef in any disastrous fashion....
What the future of the mine will be at depth is
hazardous to prophesy, but if the shoot is as long at
150 ft. as it is at 75ft. share values will be main-
tained. .. .The lode is not in settled country, and
anything may occur.... The mine is on trial
Like the Kalgurli it presents insuperable difficulties
to the valuator. .. .The reef is looking well at the
150 ft. level, and if it continues to open up [etc.,
etc.], then there can be no doubt as to the value of
the_ ground above that level.” In a nutshell, the
position at the Edna May is that some granite has
entered an appearance, and when granite shows itself
in gold mines it is generally a case of good-bye to
regularity of values. The tale about the show offer-
ing insuperable difficulties to the valuator is non-
sense. To make shares in a mine like the Edna May
worth 555. there ought to be at least 275. or 28s.
profit in sight, and not a flaw visible in the bottom.
If these conditions do not obtain (and any qualified
mining engineer could settle the point in one act)
then the serin should be passed on by present holders
to less critical speculators.
Oroya Leonesa has been obliged to recon-
struct. The already heavily-interested Lake View
and Oroya has agreed to underwrite the whole of the
new issue of 450,000 shares without remuneration,
and by doing so guarantees to fork out 2s. assess-
ment for every share of that number not taken up.
The reconstruction will provide £45,000, about half
of which is required to pay the co.’s ordinary debts
and provide supplies. There is a debenture issue of
£35,000 to be added to the col’s load of indebted-
ness, but this is to be exchanged for similar deben-
tures in the new concern. Revolutions and conse-
quent shortage of labor through death, wounds and
disappearances have hampered operations to date;
but as peace and quietness now prevails in the Cen-
tral American Republic in which the co. is domi-
ciled, better times are predicted. Ore reserves are
computed at 85,744 tons, averaging 40s. lOd. ; and
two adjoining claims are also alleged to have some
wealth in sight.
* * * *
Ivanhoe (W.A.) is singularly unfortunate
in the matter of drill holes. Some years ago a bore
through the property from Boulder ground put a
damper for a while on the hopes of the co. ; and
now another one, put down from its own workings
at 2870 ft. to cut the main lode at 3067 ft. from the
surface, has also proved disappointing. In the
former instance crosscutting disproved drill results,
and history may repeat itself in the present case;
but as the core shows the ore to be in porphyry, the
chances are against this happening. Deeper down,
however, values have, according to Dr. Maclaren, a
chance of improving.
Queensland’s gold will show a ghastly fall
this year. To the end of November, 19i2, the output
was £1,356,537 ; for the corresponding 11 months of
1913 only £1,023,477, or £333,060 less. High-
water mark was.reached in 1900, when £2,871,578
was produced. For five years after that, things
kept pretty good ; but since then the petering-out has
been quick enough to take the breath away. And
there is not a single field that holds out hopes of
better things.
Mount Morgan comes along with its quar-
terly Is. ( £50,000), but the market has lost heart
because electrolytic copper is down from £l2 to
£l4 compared with the quotation 12 months ago.
The official account of things places the revenue for
12 weeks at £219,374, of which £103,394 is for
gold and £115,980 for copper at £6O per ton. The
gold return for three months is worth twice as much
as the dividend, so if Morgan could make the red
metal pay all the costs, things would be comfortable.
But it isn’t easy to do that, for 3 p.c. copper at £6O
is worth only 365., and costs are still very high.
Brilliant Extended, Charters Towers, re-
ports a clean-up valued at £5225, which gives a
profit of £919 for the month. During the run 1820
tons were milled and 1600 tons cyanided, so the
profit is at. the rate of slightly over 10s. per ton.
The half-year's profit was £11,500, from 14,100 tons
of stone and 12,200 tons of tailings. This flash of
prosperity caused the directors to shell out two divi-
dends totalling £12,500 (Is. on 250,000 shares).
The higher interest offered induced the directors to
increase the fixed deposit to £13,000 —over Is. per
share —a substantial amount for a concern with a
share market value of £37,500 (3s. per share).
Costs for mining, milling and cyaniding were
£1 17s. 7.7 d. per ton (mining, £ 1 Bs. 4.7 d. ; milling,
ss. l.ld. ; cyaniding, 4s. 1.9 d.). The moderate charge
of 9s. 3d. per ton for milling and cyaniding gives
the Extended a decided advantage over those mines
which pay dearly for carting and crushing, and sell
their tailings much below their gold value. The half-
year closed with a total credit balance of £16,891.
* * * *
Papuan Brilliant and Victoria (Charters
Towers) has, to some extent, demonstrated the value
of the long-neglected Moonstone reef, four dividends
having been paid this year, totalling Is. per share
( £4800). Shares could on many occasions have been
bought for Is., so the Towers must have had mighty
little confidence in the Moonstone. And that indiffer-
ence continues, in spite of the fact that the Papuan
(96,000 shares) has done better than some of the
heavily-loaded old favorites.
The half-yearly balance-sheet of the New
Specimen Hill Co., Daylesford (Vic.), shows glaringly
how the directors made unreasonable haste to pay a
div. and boost the share values. The mine produced
only £25 more than it absorbed in working exes.—
£6292 against £6267 —during the six months. The
early div. amounted to £lOOO, and necessitated a
subsequent call.
Malmsbury Alluvial, pioneering at Malms-
bury (Vic.), isn’t sure that it is under the main
gutter. Nothing wonderful has been got from the
drives as far as they have been pushed out north
and south, the best dirt showing in the face of the
latter. Farther driving east has proved a second
run of wash, so Malmsbury has an idea that the
wash entered is only tributary dirt, and fancies that
the main lead may have to be punched into further
ahead.
George Buchanan and some of his pals
have taken up the old Victorian Cornish, Daylesford,
and intend to train it to gallop. The Cornish started
the late Daylesford flutter, and led to the taking
up of the Ajax and the other mines to the south.
Under English management, with Sam Davey in con-
trol, the flat makes at the Cornish gave good profits,
but they were not big enough to keep a fat London
Board going or pay many divs. on the tall share
register. The bottom levels, too, ran into low-grade
dirt. The present float has support because the new
crowd not only are past masters in the market, but
they don’t waste time over a mine without a chance.
The dredging boomlet has brought out a
proposal to work the Guildford deep lead, near
Castlemaine (Vic.). J. Bull started there in 1905,
picked up the gutter below Mein’s Freehold, but
passed out when he had sunk the shaft. Since he
swung out of the State about 12 miles of the lead
system have been carefully pegged out, and the
public is asked to put up £3OOO to deepen Bull’s
old shaft and then prick the wash. No one can
doubt the existence of the lead, for the old Mein’s
Freehold did well, and extensive boring has proved the
continuation of the system. The good points about
the project are that the wash is at a shallow depth,
water is likely to be light, and the shaft is pretty
well down to the required depth. Still, £3OOO work-
ing capital is a flea-bite of cash for even the most
modest deep-lead show.
The Langi Logan Central (Vic.) is in the
Ararat land of Nowhere, but that does not worry
the deep-lead optimist. He is boring there now, and
he testifies that the gutter is running east and west,
thus showing that, after having gone north and
south for about 15 miles, it swings round towards
the setting sun. Most leads with an east, and west
course fail to commend themselves to miners; the
theory is that it needs a reef to feed a gutter, and
the best reefs run north and south. At the Duke
United trouble began when the gutter cut across the
country at right angles to the combs of the slates.
Central Langi people don’t worry over the theories
of these hard-heads, but dogmatise that the gold
obtained and the character of the wash from four
bores furnish tangible evidence that “the Central
Langi mine is one of undoubted merit, contains a
long length of lead, and will become a highly valu-
able property.” Perhaps ; but to count up what an
alluvial claim will be from a few surface bores is—
well, Christmas is at hand; so peace and goodwill.
The Port Davey (Tas.) hasn’t taken long
to find its level. The CD. was formed to work tin
areas selected and named the Eureka by a crowd of
innocents. Langford took the float in hand; and as
the areas were reported on by an old friend of theirs,
Bewick, Moreing and Co. accepted the management.
The co. was a bold float—looo £5 shares to the
vendors, and 1000 subscribed for at £5 each by the
best bloods in Melbourne. The subscribers got their
shares; the vendors didn’t. The latter, while hunt-
ing for the fly in the ointment, saw 8., M.’s staff
sampling the ground. Then it came out that there
was doubt about the title of two of the leases, and
the market game stopped suddenly. The first tap of
the drum afterwards brought angry contributors to
the ranks, where they had to listen to a tale that
over a thousand had gone to prove the ground of no
value; also, that £2OO had been handed to the
Eureka crowd to cancel contracts with the Co. There
was some talk of fight over this, but “Kodax” Baker
had no difficulty in showing that the composition
was a cheap let-off. The Eureka slcin-de-cat doesn’t
intend to accept the 8., M. sampling as conclusive, and
will put the £2OO back into the ground. Hadn’t
they first better see that their titles are clear?
Liquidation of the Port Davey Co. meins a refund
of £3 10s. for each fiver put up by the bloods of
that concern, so they will have a bit of salvage for
Christmas cheer.
As a result of the discovery of wash by the
Pinafore Co., near the head of the Lefroy deep alluvial
lead (Tas.), Launceston has gone on one of its rare
speculative jags. On Thursday, Nov. 27th, a prospectus
of the Lefroy Leads (155 acres adjoining the Pinafore
property) was prepared one morning. That after-
noon saw a few sporadic applications for shares ; but
on the following morning normally sober citizens were
seized by devils who rushed them violently to the
office of the amazed broker, where they waved cheques
like flags on Mafeking day. In a couple of hours
the shares were over-applied for by some 12,000, and
those who had not secured any were putting it to
the more successful that it would be a sportsmanlike
action to divide.
From • i E. Locker, secretary of the
Umeari (Philippines) dredging proposition: Referring
to The Bulletin’s queries (11/12/T3) re Umeari,
both Messrs. Herbert Park, C.E., and A. 11. Poole
(of Poole and Steele, of Bahnain) declare that the
river from its mouth to the highest point touched
bv the eo.’s claims presents no dredging difficulties,
a i,d the dredge can be floated to the lower claim in
six weeks. Both of these gentlemen have traversed
the Umeari River, and their ripe and intimate know-
ledge and experience of river-dredging are unques-
tionable. The co. has a copy of a survey plan of
the river, and holds an unqualified assurance from its
legal advisers and attorneys in Manila that its rights
to dredge up the river are fully protected. I would
just like to give some of Mr. Poole’s own words
on his return from the Umeari River some seven
weeks ago: “Firewood is very plentiful, and there is
a constant stream of water, with practically no fear
of serious floods. The dredging conditions are equal
to anything I have ever seen. There is no clay or
cement, very little overburden and no hidden river
snags. There are no bars —only a few shallows —and
all the leases are dredgable. The gravel is of a good
class; that is, the stones are fairly round, and will
run freely through a sluice-box or screen, and there
appear to be very few boulders in the river. I saw
a good deal of prospecting done, and always with
good results.”
According to London Daily Telegraph
a Cousin Jack expert at Jewburg has in-
vented a treatment process which is “destined to
revolutionise those parts of the world where re-
fractory gold ores have hitherto baffled science.”
Details were not available at the time the above
assertion was made, but the inventor was busy
assuring newspaper correspondents and others that
ores containing arsenic, antimony, graphite and other
troublesome ingredients will be made to give up 98
per cent, of their gold contents. The inventor fur-
ther states that he will dissolve base metals as
easily as cyanide dissolves gold. Well, why is he
wasting valuable time in talking?

WOMAN'S
LETTER
kydney, December 15, 1913.
MY -Dear Arini, —
Governor Strickland’s departing Help
Talbot kept his intention of looking for
another billet in the “Colonies” —poor old
Colonies! —so hidden from the vulgar eye,
that we packed him off to Aldershot and cold
drill a week ago. Now the news is broken
that he joins the Macgregor’s staff in Queens-
land early in the new year. Society in
Bananaland will therefore be half-soled and
heeled by an attractive youngster who has
been spoilt for a while by over-doses of the
sugar-plums of life.
The Reid cavalcade will wedge a week at
Queenscliff (Vic.) into their hurried flight
along the Australian coastline. From Jan.
26 to Feb. 1, obr Pie Commissioner cools
off in the Apple Island, after his panting
tour of the North.
Barrister Milner Stephen, who seemed a
sure tip for the city ermine, missed the
Mayorship by a head. The first to pass the
judge’s stand was Alderman Richards; ex-
city surveyor. Milner Stephen-, Whbsb
ding this week will be attended by shOwets
of confetti and myriads of relations —the
Stephen family is father less numerous than
the sands of the sea —expected to hand his
bride (she is Miss Molly Graham till the
knot is tied) a chair as Lady Mayoress on
New Year’s Day. That dream is O’er —for
another year-, anyhow.
Our Ue\v Municipal Mother-elect, Mrs. R.
W, Richards* is a sister of Mrs. Sam Hor-
dern, senior, who used to be one of this
city’s best-looking matrons. The sisters were
born in the municipal fold long before the
Mayor of Sydney became a “Lord.” They are
the daughters of a former city alderman,
John Booth. Mrs. Richards has been turned
inside out, without an anaesthetic, by several
interviewers. The revelations are so like
those of a long line of predecessors that I
suppose a Mayoress is predestined to love
her home, and her husband, and needlework
(plain and fancy) and to have no domestic
troubles. This leaves her free to cultivate
Good Works, while ordinary women are
burning saucepans and cultivating a feroci-
ous aversion to darning socks.
The announcement of Debussy’s “Prodigal
Son,” at Her Majesty’s, didn’t kindle any
wild desire for seats, and a moderately slim
audience turned out for the second-last even-
ing of the opera season. Even the bait of a
symphony concert to follow, with the remark-
able Eckhold, the agitato Voghera, and
Joseph Bradley as an extra, failed to keep
the stall-holders in their seats till the clocks
chimed 11. The latter half of a fine
orchestral wail of mixed music was anno-
tated by the stealthy departure of the stalled
ox and the clattering exits and entrances
of the long-haired tribe who thronged the
d.c. during the now-defunct opera orgy.
These disturbers of the peace of plain citi-
zens ought to be herded together in some
sound-proof compound during the visits
of strange song-birds. The average profes-
sional musician can be depended on to add
the creak of shoe-leather to every vocal and
instrumental performance in this city, and
the recent opera co. was, for nine long weeks,
more or less stamped on by these irritating
followers of the Art.
Perceval Allen’s garb as Lia, the mother
of the Prodigal, was mostly veil, about the
size of a tablecloth, and battened down on
her head with a metal circlet. Papa Simeon
(Graham Marr) blessed the returned spend-
thrift in the usual multi-colored dressing-
gown of Asia, surmounted by a hat no larger
than a well-srrown parsnip. This millinery
took root in the centre of a fluffy grey
coiffure. The scene was laid on the classic
shores of Lake Genesareth. If parsnip
modes be the local fashion, the leading gentry
must wear very wet towels over the
back of their heads, for the sun is fairly
scorching in summer thereabouts. De-
bussy wrote no music for the fatted calf.
Indeed, judging by the rotund waistline of
the Prodigal Son (Maurice d’Oisly), the
wanderer slew and ate the beast on his way
home. Edna Thornton, in her best voice,
warbled a song by Ernest Wunderlich, lab-
elled “Good Night,” to loud applause. The
amateur composer, who has been camping
with his wife in the d.c. the last nine weeks,
looked slightly self-conscious during the pro-
gress of this melodious effort, in consequence
of his large circle of acquaintances
staring him straight in the face to see how
he was taking it.
The Quinlan Opera Co. is said to be
more free from internecine warfare than most
travelling aviaries. It is a kind of co-opera-
tive-Commonwealth arrangement, with no
stars at enormous salaries and proportionate
opinions of their worth to play up when the
management gets into a tight corner. The
longer Thomas Quinlan can keep going
the better it is for principals and under-
studies. A steady wage being better than
a tussle for engagements, even one tenor
has learnt to be at peace with another, and
no soprano in the co. has been caught
smacking the face of any other lady who got
too much apnlause. Maurice d’Oisly cer-
tainly borrowed the silver-plated trousers of
Lohengrin Thomas the other night. But it
was all in a friendly way of business, and
Lohengrin got them back with his week’s
washing, in time for the performance on
Saturday night.
Lohengrin, having had his silver trousers
returned by Manrico, strutted round the
shores of the Scheldt, after walking out with
Elsa, for the last time on December 13. Man-
ager Quinlan’s work during the season was
recognised on this occasion by first-nighters,
who gave him an illuminated address and a
gold matchbox. Mrs. Quinlan’s share was a
diamond-spangled bangle. Chief Justice
Cullen, whose white thatch has. been a pto L
minent d.c. landmark of iate, handed over
the goods.
Confirmed opera-goers will now remove
their air-cushions and other baggage after a
nine weeks’ camp in Her Majesty’s, and
turn lightly to thoughts of hamper baskets
and surf costumes.
The N.S.W. . Institute of ArcbitectS
out edrds to hihhi its fifth biehnidl exhibi-
tion; The.sb passes Webe so vaguely worded
that t\yo-thirds of thq invited pet-sobs
believed that last Friday would see the open-
ing ceremony, and were stunned to bead of
Wednesday’s party two days before their
own presence wds asked for by the amuse-
ment committee. After recovering from this
indignity, the Friday assortment put on only
its second-best hat, and sauntered round the
exhibits with an air of detachment till it
fell upon the food supplies with heavy
slaughter.
The exhibition itself pointed severely to
the attempts of the modern builder to scale
the clouds. In the suburban villa department
the Indian bungalow is evidently fighting
for a place beside the fashionable pigeon
cotes with small lattice windows and no
verandahs worth mentioning. The old
“Colonial” house, with its beneficent breadth
of airy balconies and spacious halls, is
crumbling into a memory before pepper-pot
towers and horrible attempts to imitate
mediaeval fortresses on a 40-foot frontage.
The cottage in most of the designs at this
exhibition showed gleams of architectural
intelligence in the matter of kitchen and
pantry. Still, the woman architect in Aus-
tralia ought to have a future, for the male
person has a past strewed with labor-making
devices.
Musical items provided by pianist Rich-
ards and singer Appleby were supplemented
by the deep growl of Bryce Carter’s ’cello.
Mrs. Alfred Lee and a daughter came along
to the party. Artists Ethel Phillips Fox
and Thea Proctor tore themselves away from
Manly for the occasion. Handsome Thea,
swathed in sapphire blue, looked so much
like one of her own French fan fantasies
that she ought to have been mentioned in
the catalogue. The Binnies, Dr. Grace
Boelke, and other familiar figures at these
shows succeeded in peering into the exhibits
over the bodies of the Always-Hungry, who
were cramming themselves with food. Mrs.
Budden ana Mrs. Ernest Scott were lying
in wait for visitors at the entrance to the
R.A. Society’s hot-air compartments.
John McCormack’s farewell to his admirers
the Town Hall ended up with a football
m§lee, in wffiich the rabid encore fiends were
forwards and half-backs. In the excite-
ment of the final scrimmage the people in
the choir seats threatened personal violence
if the lights went out before Kathleen
Mavourneen and other well-known Irish
ladies were introduced. McCormack’s great-
est effort, “The Lord is My Light,” rang out
like a trumpet call till the municipal welkin
trembled, for a few days’ rest had restored
resonance to the silvery tenor. May Hux-
ley, loaded with Christmas bells and caught
in the swirl of the encore whirlpool, sang
the opening bars of German’s “Waltz Song”
with a nasal intonation which she should
throw overboard on the voyage to Maoriland.
Donald Mcßeath fiddled his best, and Vin-
cent O’Brien, as usual, spent the evening at
the piano. Surgeon McCormick (the con-
quering Alexander), legions of W. T. Bren-
nans, the tall Layton duet, John’s pet
colleen twinkling with diamonds, autograph
fiends charging the den behind the growling
organ, and a generally-joyous feeling of
Ballyhooley Fair, mixed up with the Rugby
football before mentioned, finished the Irish
tenor’s 1913 concert tour in Australia.
The air throbs with breaking-up entertain-
ments and the hair bows of the Young
Person have waggled in front of innumer-
able “speech days.” Sydney Grammar
School puts away its slates this week, after
a few remarks from Governor Strickland.
The Girls’ High School, in the crazy old
building in Elizabeth-street, has just pro-
duced a prize-winner who has beaten every-
thing her own weight and age on sight.
This promising youngster, who is a tall, spec-
tacled 17-year-old named Lorna Beveridge,
piled up such a formidable list of wins at
the recent exams, that mere boys will have
to rearrange their ideas about the woman’s
vote. As hard fate would have it, two
ferocious stacks of exam, papers fell due
about the same time. The State school’s
inquisition was racking the Young Idea while
the Uni. senior exam, was tearing informa-
tion from its perspiring devotees, and Lorna
was out to tackle both. For three successive
weeks this learned young person poured forth
all the knowledge she’d corked up for the occa-
sion. Here are a few results : The Horner
Exhibition for mathematics; the Baker
Scholarship, a valuable trophy usually won
by young men; various medals; “almost
equal” for the Fairfax prize (she missed this
by an eyelash or so) ; and a few certificates
and ordinary prizes. Anything not men-
tioned here can be collected later with a
lorry. The North Sydney girl, who is going
to study medicine, starts with a cargo of
mathematics usually found only in the hold
of a senior wrangler.
The Arts and Crafts Society; which hangs
tip its sign id Norwich Chambers, h4§ been
showing its wares with successful assaults on
the public purse. The Christmas-gift habit
is the Society’s sole excuse for this its third
exhibition since Junfe.

A yqiing Scot named Wright wandered
ftom Aberdeen to Sydney, via London and
Paris studios, a while ago, with the wild am-
bition to set up as a sculptor in Australia.
But sculpture, except on tombstones, is not
so necessary as bricklaying in a young coun-
try, and the Wright chisel is doing strong
work at half-speed. Some models of the
Aberdeen native’s work were on view at the
recent Architects’ Exhibition, notably a
group of figures, boldly planned, which are
to stand amongst the smuts on the skyscrap-
ing tower of the new Daily Telegraph
buildings.
After 21 years’ service, Dominie Aspinall
is getting down off his high seat as head of
the Scots’ College for boys. This academy,
for all sizes in knickerbockers, farewelled
its old Principal with a rush of presents,
which included a ticket to the other end of
the earth—marked “Return.” Mrs. Aspin-
all had a similar little token of right-feeling
from ex-students. So the popular pair can gad
round the globe, and keep their own money
stowed away in an old stocking for tourist
hotels and other legalised forms of robbery.
The Todmans’ palatial cot at Homebush is
acquiring an extensive acreage of 32 decora-
tive panels from the brush of Norman
Carter. The same artist, who is wearing as
lean as a greyhound in training, is working
overtime at some more decorative work for
the Sydney ’Varsity Union Library.
A Hobart girl named Mabel Sprent, who
is holidaying here, has just had her first
novel, “Love’s Apprenticeship,” published by
Methuen, of London. The title has a delicious
flavor of youth about it, and the goods inside
are in keeping.
Bernice Edwell, a former Sydney Arts and
Crafts worker, is so bustled with commis-
sions in Adelaide that she is likely to stay
over there as a permanency.
Lawrence Campbell is handing over the
profits of a six nights’ season at the Palace
—in his own playlet, “Jasmine” —to the
Alexandra Hospital for children. This
institution is yelling with all its might—a
good, old ear-splitting Christmas yell—for
cash or toys for its sick and maimed babies.
“Jasmine” was given a good start off the
mark by a packed audience on Saturday
night.
Frank Tait, one of the six brothers who
control the coming and going of many musi-
cal stars, is over here from the Cabbage
Patch with his new bride. They are hover-
ing on the rim of John McCormack’s pro-
jected departure to Maoriland 'this week-
strike permitting. Mrs. Frank is an attrac-
tive slip of a girl, with a handsome pair of
Irish eyes.
The Ernest Wunderlichs are off to Europe
again. They’ll drink deep draughts
of their favorite vintage, Wagnerian chords,
at Bayreuth and other well-known musical
hostels in 1914.
Surfing has to be approached with care
by innocent strangers from the other side.
Vincent O’Brien, the Dublin musician who
accompanies the McCormack voice, found
out one of its perils after he had had an
ear peppered with sand. Since then he has
been haying his drums attended to by a
local aurist. It’s a fairly common complaint
in the surfing season, but mighty awkward
for a pianist who is trying to catch a tenor’s
soft whisper to Molly Bawn.
The cheery prospect of lending a hand in
the stoking department of the outgoing
Maoriland steamer is still open to Dr. Oscar
Paul, who married Miss Gwyneth Jones on
Saturday last. The bride is one of the sev-
eral daughters of the late Richard, of the
Harrison, Jones and Devlin wool firm.
Whether the honeymoon couple will take the
risky joy of helping an amateur crew over
the bumpy track to Maoriland, while the
seamen’s strike is dying hard, is uncertain as
I write (Monday). Several M.L. boats are
boldly anonncing their intended departure
this week. Other trousseaued passengers
who had intended jumping aboard an M.L
craft, are giving it up. And the Oscar Paui
couple may also put off their trip till the
scratch crews —lawyers, doctors, dentists
etc.—get back to their own trades.
The McCall-McCowans (this is a name
that needs to be handled with care) will
eat their Christmas dinner at Kosciusko.
He is superintendent in Australia of the
huge pastoral concern known as the New
Zealand and Australian Land Co.
Managing-Director Newman, of the
Howard Smith line, is going to petrol up to
Kosciusko this side of the Christmas plum-
pudding. The rod-and-line craze is luring
him and a party of friends that way. And
some Brisbane folks; Dr. and Mrs. A. J,
Turner, are bound for the Upper Showy in
January, also with rods in their baggage.

The Lucas Tooth family is still bom-
barded with placards imploring its presence
in our Best Suburbs. It hit back last week
with an evening party at the Australia.
This has been respectfully described in every
detail except as to the pattern in evening
socks preferred by Baronet Robert Lucas.
Jessie Ackermann, F.R.G.S., spent about
two years in search of copy for her book
“Australia from a Woman’s Point of
View.” Most women hate statistics in any
shape; but the German parentage in Miss
Ackermann seemed to lead her to the door-
mat of every dull office in the leading cities
of Australia. Arrived there she opened her
mouth and some vassal poured arithmetic
down her throat. Off the track of politi-
cians and statistics the book has a few read-
able patches. Of the Australian girl she
says: “A new brand is in process of evolu-
tion. ... Speaking generally, they (Aus-
tralian girls) are really delightful creatures.
Their charms are so refreshing, one hesitates
to sound a note of criticism... .Free, in-
dependent and unconventional, the younger
generation stands next of kin to their neigh-
bor maidens across the Atlantic —American
girls.”
The Queen’s Club, which formerly reared
a spiky wall of regulations against new mem-
bers, has lately slipped off its old
haughty pedestal. One of its members
sighed through a mouthful of buttered toast
the other afternoon re some recent additions
to the exclusive menagerie. “We used to
make rather a point about grandfathers—
now we’ve ceased to worry about the social
status even of the fathers of new members.”
I understand that the Labor party is in-
directly blamed for this catastrophe, among
others.
Fashion, which has been rending our nether
garments in twain, and tearing our upper
garments asunder at the throat, is getting
back to its old, high-necked attitude. Clothes,
if the Paris winter models are a forecast,
will be regarded as a covering for the fem-
inine form in our next autumn. A fashion
magazine that came by the last mail shows
ladies who are trussed to the ear-lobes. For-
tunately for the race, very few women have
figures like the dolls in French fashion-
plates, or Woman in the year-to-be would
be boned to the brain in high collars that look
like lace walls flanked by girders of steel.
Another mendicant holds out both hands
for help. This time it is the N.S.W. Home
for Incurables at Ryde. The Home provides
flower-decked gardens and comfortable stor-
age for the physically broken who can never
hope to be mended this side of Jordan. Secre-
tary F. C. Moore remarks that hundreds are
waiting for beds in this picturesque haven,
but they’ll have to camp outside till more
bricks and mortar are put together. £7500
is the sum required.
Mosman, which hangs like a green eyebrow
over rounded bays, is going to swing out its
carnival lights on Saturday next. There will
be carol singers, who will warble in the glow
of Chinese lanterns, and motor-boats are
politely requested to refrain from snorting
in the chorus.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.
So hopeless is Labor’s position in the
Vic. House of Landlords that the first Labor
Ministry included every member of the party
in that Chamber. There are two salaried
and two honorary Ministers: Evans, a
fruit-grower at Ringwood; small-goods
manufacturer McLellan, of Richmond; J.
P. Jones, tailor; and Fielding, of Footscray,
who ousted W. H. Edgar recently and thus
attains Cabinet rank in his first session of
Parliament.

The “IWAN” Pattern
Post Hole Digger
makes fencing or any earth boring
dead simple.
No more pressure is needed than
to lean on the digger and turn it
to the right. Hard, dry ground,
of course, needs more weight. Six
half-turns usually fills the digger,
and six times filling makes your
3-feet hole.
Bores the hole just about right
for the post, leaving very little
earth to shovel back and ram. 10
inch is the usual fencing size, and
120 holes in a day is good going.
Splendid for planting fruit trees,
and effective in boring for water.
The “IWAN” Pattern
Post Hole Digger
is a success because it’s built
right. Perfectly balanced, rigid,
and no loose parts to wobble or
rust up. Get one !

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, December 15, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Denman dame, in peacock blue in-
terrupted by crushed strawberry ninon,
and under a spiky black tulle hat, opened the
shining doors of the new Y.W.C.A. build-
ing, in Russell-street, on Saturday after-
noon. The handsome premises, which cost
close on £20,000, are the gift of the Conni-
bere family, and stand as a useful memorial
of George Connibere, the deceased head of
the house. For the moderate sum of 15s.
a week, the Christian Young Woman can
be boarded and lodgtd in a room so white
that she will dream of Heaven at least three
nights in seven. Water is laid on in each
bedroom, so the cleanliness which is next to
the other thing should be assured. Anglican
Archbishop Clarke breathed blessings into
the edifice, and, after he was through, Par-
son Spun- added his little bit to make cer-
tain. Chamberlain Nevill stood to attention
while the religion was being instilled. Mrs.
President Pearce, in Durple featherings, spoke
earnestly, and refrained from step-dancing;
Mrs. Robert Harper decorated the platform
in black and white; and Archdeacon Hindley
tucked his gaiters under a chair. Robert
Best and Ernest Connibere were listened to
reverently by the hundreds of white-mus-
lined girls in the hall; and, later on, there
was tea. Come to think of it, tea follows
every function of the sort. Sometimes I
think the Hereafter will be all tea —and
buns, perhaps.
The five Harold Pearson babies, closely
attended by their parents, were conducted up
the gangway of the big ship Orama last
week. The G-G. pair farewelled them from
the pier, and then hurried shorewards to
hide their distinguished tears. Mrs. Harold
made herself liked among Melbourne’s Best
People during her stay, and helped to set
the fashion for straw-colored hair-switches.
So long as straw switches are in she will be
fondly remembered.
A link was forged last week between the
Victorian Bench and the British peerage.
Margaret Hodges, the second of Justice
Hodges’ pretty daughters, married a younger
son of the Earl of Glasgow, and became the
Hon. Mrs. John Boyle. She is slender and
dark, with a fragile look that is awfully
appealing. She used to decorate most Mel-
bourne functions that deserved it. Sister
Mrs. H. Clifton Brown sheltered her up to
the moment of the ordeal, and the giving-
away solemnities were allotted to the mili-
tary brother-in-law, Colonel H. Clifton
Brown. (For goodness sake, don’t forget
that “Clifton”!) People who are interested
in such things reckon the wedding one of
the great events of the year. By the way,
Margaret’s stepmother has just fitted up the
country refuge at Macedon with furniture
which is up to the best traditions of that
high vice-regal roost. Capt. Boyle, a
brother of the new bridegroom, was aide to
Gov. Carmichael when he reigned in Mel-
bourne.
The late Sam Gillott, whose rich estate
paid probate duty the other day, was a mere
boy in comparison with his one-time junior
partner and brother alderman, Arthur
Snowden. Snowden had his 85th birthday
recently, and last week the singing members
of Melbourne’s only surviving Liedertafel
serenaded him in honor of his long connec-
tion with their musical body. Eighty-five
of them went down to the Snowden domicile
at Abbotsford for the nurpose of warbling
to their antique vice-president in the garden,
where everything was lovely, including the
refreshments. Talking of octogenarian
Arthur and septuagenarian Sam, one is dis-
posed to wonder as to the exact youthful-
ness of Croker —of the sylphlike figure—
who was sandwiched between them long ago
in the legal firm of Gillott, Croker and
Snowden.
The Overseas Club had a garden fete on
Saturday in the W. H. Felstead grounds at
Kew. Mrs. Hennessy, in biscuit-colored lace,
supplied the opening remarks on a corner of
the lawn behind a bouquet of sweet peas.
The Gaud Mayor added some superfluous
details, and Dr. Springthorpe got rid of a
few observations he happened to have about
him. Mrs. Felstead beamed pinkly on these
proceedings in expensive Irish crochet lace
and a pink-lined hat- while her white-mus-
lined daughter looked on wearily from the
background. Pa Felstead modestly endea-
vored to be mistaken for an ordinary
guest. A big mob of solemnly-Imperial
Overseas people then strewed themselves
about the premises and rode for the glory of
the Empire on merry-go-rounds and don-
keys. The proceeds (swelled by a big cheque
from the Felsteads) are to supply the
women-members of the Overseas Club with
rooms wherein to read back-numbers of illus-
trated papers, write home, whisper,
straighten their hair, borrow hairpins and
do up one another’s blouses.
The Harper-Barton alliance was effected
the other Tuesday afternoon, with much
pomp and circumstance, at Toorak’s Presby-
terian Kirk. The holy edifice was cumbered
with lilies and greenery, and the Best People
stood on one another’s feet and craned their
necks to see. Mrs. Albert Miller and the
Staughton widow (the latter in magpie
varb) were amongst the foremost onlookers;
Mrs. Dunbar Hooper purred congratulations ;
and the Youngmans and Laidlaws smiled
theirs. The Knox boys and some
Bretts were elegant spectacles in the polite
crush. Margaret Barton played the part of
bride in a cutely-cut ivory moire coat and
skirt which had the latest waistless look
about it; but her pretty hair was hidden by
an unbecoming tulle headcovering. Step-
brother-in-law George Knox gave her away,
Papa being absent on business. Bridegroom
Arthur Harper, who is something under
seven feet without his boots (he had them
on while he was being married), appealed
for guidance to his stout groomsman, Lind-
say Docarty, and was supplied with the pro-
per steering directions. Gretta Laidlaw,
the lone bridesmaid, performed her duties
in white and red upholstery. Mrs. Robert
Harper, clad in black Chantilly spread on
white, with mauve sash and flowers, helped
the bride’s step-sister, Mrs. McPherson-
Knox. The wedding taxes were spread out
for inspection at Illawarra, Toorak, where
refreshments were also available. The
wedded pair escaped later in a new car of
their own, amidst a blizzard of confetti. The
honeymoon tour includes Tasmania, Japan
and America, in that order.
The first Dufault concert drew a good fat
audience. Paul let himself go as he never
did when in the shadow of the gorgeous
Nordica. The usual adoring contingent of
pink-necked flappers burbled, and gushed,
and yelped about him until someone sug-
gested it would be a good idea to bring all
such young things in crates and throw a
cloth over them when they got too excited.
Violet Clark’s platform frock was composed
of maize-colored cloth and lace. One of
Dufault’s encore items was announced as
“A Sprig of Boronia,” by Florence Hull, of
Melbourne. (“Florence Hull,” of course, is
Mrs. Kenny.) Tenor Walter Kirby was in
a front-row seat, criticising gently for the
benefit of some powdered ospreys. So far
as I could see, he was hemmed in. Fragile
Leonie Peterson-Mackworth accompanied
her military husband in pale blue chiffon.
Mamma Pinschof was attended by Pa. The
platform, dressed in trellis, gum leaves and
green cloth, was a great improvement. In
its usual unadorned state it looks a bleak,
lonely place.
The gentle Paul is the most soothing male
singer in the business, and he caresses his
words like doves or prize fowls before he
allows them to fly away into silence. When in
action he reminds me of a recent poem by
Shaw Neilsen, with the refrain, “Let your
song be gentle.” I guess the Canadian must
have inspired it. Harold Whittle, who was
well aware that the finished Romayne Sim-
mons had recently occupied the accompa-
nist’s stool, did his very best. Ernest Toy
fiddled one of his own madrigals and some
well-worn “pieces,” and Violet Clark sup-
plied a few nice but unsensational soprano
items. It is a pity Dufault does so much of
the limp, drawing-room, “Only-a-leaf-of-pars-
ley” type of song. It may hit the public
over the heart which has fatty degeneration,
but he could mix in a larger proportion of
good stuff without doing any damage to his
programmes.
The Greens’ juvenile fancy ball, which
skipped and frothed at the Town Hall the
other Monday, produced so many pocket
Genees that imagination staggered and had
to be taken home in a cab with its boots
hanging limply out the back. While the
pretty kiddies were stepping and winning
applause, rival mammas were busy with
their little hatchets. One always expects a
certain amount of this in a competitive atmo-
sphere; but the execution done at the Green’s
spree was tremendous. During the evening
there was one awful moment of suspense.
Two of the chubbiest babies (cherubs of not
more than three and a bit), decorated with
rosebuds and tulle, suddenly became stage-
struck, and refused to budge from the en-
chanted spot of lieht in the centre of the
platform. Loud stage whispers, commands
and low whistles failed to shift the pair,
who stood perfectly still, until Mrs. Green
herself, in lilac and lace, rushed on and
grabbed them up. Sadie Purves (who used
to be Sadie Green) assisted mamma, in be-
coming black. Husband Jim Purves hovered
on the outskirts and criticised. When Gaud
Mayor Hennessy, in an almost inaudible
speech, sought to press purses of sovereigns
upon daughter Florrie and her mother,
they (I don’t mean the sovereigns)
became panic-stricken, and had to be dragged
into public view. I thought, for one oalpi-
tating moment, that the Gaud Mayor would
be forced to pocket the cash and wait his
chance; but he eventually got rid of the
money in public.
John Thomson, one of the Liberal whips
in the House of Reps., seized the opportunity
to get married whilst the sun shone. The
party bought him a silver tray, and chief
Cook presented it, with a few remarks, on
the Parliament premises. The remarks weren’t
printed, but I do hope he didn’t call any-
body a cow or refer to tripe more than three
times.
Will Longstaff has scrawled his name in
the corner of another highly-colored commis-
sion, representing an incident in Gordon’s
“How We Beat the Favorite.” The public
is to be allowed to gaze on the work of art
for one brief fortnight, at Gill’s Studio, in
Centreway. The effort will probably be con-
demned by the new school of impressionists,
because it really means something, and can
be described in plain language. A rider in
pink is just passing a rival competitor in
pea-green. Both riders and horses are shown
in mid-air above an artistically-trimmed
ditch, the whole scene being designed to
illustrate the cantering line, “Between sky
and water, the brown came and caught her.”
It’s dreadfuly clever of Pink Shirt to have
“caught her,” as his gallant steed appears to
have put its forelegs out of gear at one of
the previous jumps. The picture is the pro-
perty of W. Grattan, of St. Kilda, who is
said to have conceived the subject and
bullied Will into doing it. As a matter of
fact, Longstaff always said that horses
weren’t in his line.
Artist Septimus Power is acting as one of
the judges of the National Gallery students’
brush efforts. When that important duty is
disposed of, he is off to take some shorthand
sketches of the Murray. He leaves for
London next March.
Your Johanna’s comments on the latest
Automobile Club happenings were suffici-
ently inaccurate (inaccuracy is the badge of
all my tribe) to wound the sporting feelings
of toot-tooting enthusiasts. The paragraphs
sinned in speaking of H. Y. McKay as a
“non-successful” competitor, addicted to
spills ; I am assured he is a persistent prize-
winner, and has been the luckiest of motor-
ists. All the time he has been petrolling he
has suffered only the one mishap, which
occurred near Nagambie when he was trying
to dodge a broken culvert. The car was
soon righted, and it won a principal race
later in the same week, with H.V. in per-
sonal command. He has never had a car
“explode in his face”; it was merely an
acetylene lamp that behaved so badly some
six months ago. At the ’Mobile Club’s hill-
climbing contest t’other Saturday the gallant
McKay gained the best formula result in one
of the classes, whilst another of his Vaux-
hall cars came third in the Open Event.
There was no hill-climb tooting at Mace-
don last year, but in 1911 H. Y. McKay won
the first prize, and two other Sunshine cars
followed him home.
Seems to me that the she-sexagettes, with
their flowing hats, easily begrimed draperies
and slim shoes, are as much interested as
the other fellows in “Durable, Dustless, Wat-
erproof Roadways.” A discourse on ideal
streets is to be delivered by Mr. T. H. Fffire
at the Masonic Hall on Thursday night.
He will explain a new method of dustless-
road construction ; but, seemingly, men only
are “courteously invited.” An infusion of
feminine element is certainly called for. It
is quite time that a league of determined
spinsters shrieked a protest against man-
made roads in dusty Melbourne.
Veronica Bedford, of the Marshall Hall
Conservatorium, shone at the Conserva-
torium concert both as contralto and com-
poser. Mrs. Weidermann has trained her
voice for the last four years, and both Mar-
shall ITall and Fritz Hart predict a career
for her as a composer. At the Auditorium
concert she sang Brahms’s “Sapphic Ode”
and one of her own songs, and other songs
of hers were sung by Violet Clark. Miss
Bedford will make her first Sydney appear-
ance next year.
The T. B. Ryan dame, in collaboration
with Mrs. Alec Boyd, gave a garden revel bf
some sort on the Boyds’ Auburn grassplot
last Friday. The favorite stunt of the
moment, at teas and other fatuous gather-
ings, is fortune-telling, and the she-suburbans
of Auburn and East Kew were thrilled in
turn by an expert futurist, who is being
rushed by the hostesses still in town. A
Zulu witch-doctor with some company man-
ners could make a lot of money by blithering
to our most expensive hatters.
Last Wednesday afternoon the local peer-
age spread its best chiffons and its alleged
Paris frock on the front lawn of the Arthur
Paynes’ Toorak place. The lady of the
mansion does not make a hobby of entertain-
ing, consequently the affair was a social sur-
prise. The hostess herself created little rip-
ples of feminine envy and waves of rage by
promenading in yards of painted chiffon
over a discreet quantity of satin.
The Brighton Williamsons’ Dutch daugh-
ter-in-law, who has been visiting her hus-
band’s family and parading her three infants
for inspection, is steaming back to South
Africa, where the Australian-born Alfred is
deputy-mayor or something equally disgrace-
ful.
The Norman Armytage matron recently
got the full value out of some expensive floral
decorations, ordered for a feminine midday
banauet, by inviting a second batch of friends
for afternoon tea and buns, a third squad for
an evening feed, and a fourth battalion for
a bridge orgy. By the time the bridge was
over, every blossom was fainting on its stem
and the violet was resting its forehead on its
roots.
Tennis champion Alf Dunlop will be
escorted on his sporting travels by Mrs. Alf
and the children, nurses and governesses-in-
waiting, and a few of the Treadway clan.
I wish somebody would say something in-
sulting about the people who select the
juvenile photos, for the Christmas numbers.
I bore up pretty well this year until I
struck a nightshirted boy standing on a
studio step. He held an unlighted candle in
one fist and a cheap doll in the other, and
he isn’t the right sort of small boy to be
published with advantage in his nightshirt,
for he isn’t chubby or winning, and his
forehead sticks out like an inflated balloon
over his professorial eyes. He is the sort of
child who will become a stooping suburban
chemist, with enormous spectacles and reli-
gious doubts. The other sort of kid who is
thrown at the public during the overfeeding
season is the fat, stolid sub-flapper with her
hair tied up in 14 Chinese puzzles with
lengths of ribbon. Nine times out of 10 she
is shown at the telephone, above the banal
title, “Speaking to Daddy.” Sometimes she
is holding the collar of a massive, three-
volume dog, with a long, hairy face. This
enormous animal seems to be kept alive from
year to year for this one degraded purpose.
The label in this case is always “Friends.”
Then there is the smirking, actressy type
of fluffy girl-child, with her frizzled head set
at a woodenly coquettish angle by the per-
spiring photographer. She is least offensive
when she is simply “Father’s Girl” ; but I
have met her as “Sweetheart,” or “Our
Treasure.” There was one terrible yeai
when she was “Darling” in one rag and
“Dumpkins” in another. That was the year
I most wanted to smack her. I suppose
suburbanites deserve this sort of thing, or
they wouldn’t get it regularly, year after
year.
English women story-tellers are working
the white slave traffic for all it is worth—
and more. In one of the latest—“ The
Shadow of the Dragon,” by Cecilia Moore—
the heroine is Hepsie McLeod, the “beauty”
of an Australian liner, who is travelling in
the steerage. The swells of the first-class
saloon loiter around her like bees on a honey-
suckle. She has “hair of a Titian auburn
and a skin like strawberries and cream.”
But a couple of white slavers on board have
marked her down for their prey. They
entice her ashore at Colombo, whither she is
kidnapped to Shanghai, whence she is
rescued after some thrilling adventures by a
young English lord whom, of course, she
marries. All roads lead to the altar when
a woman, writing for flapperdom, is the
surveyor.
“Rosna’s” line or two from London:—
Harold Colonna, playing Dunois in “Joan of Arc”
at Covent Garden, is alleged to be the tallest tenor
on the operatic stage, there being well over two
yards of him. He is also the first Australian tenor
to play a leading part in Covent Garden. A chip of
the Marshall Hall Conservatorium, he appears to have
a lucky star. After three years’ study in London, a
kind-hearted millionaire came along and despatched
him, carriage-paid, to Paris for a year or two. He
studied with Duvernoy and Hettich, gave a concert in
Paris, and then jumped straight into the lead for
the Covent Garden season. He is to play in “Faust”
and “Lohengrin,” and be Don Jose in “Carmen.”
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The Colley-Stirling wedding will occur in January
at the Pier Hotel, Glenelg. Which reminds me that
Miss Kathleen, the second of the W. H. Phillipps
daughters, has consented to enter for the Great Gam-
ble with Mr. “Willie” Dean. There is now but one
member of the G. H. Dean clan matrimonially un-
accounted for. Really, no one is safe these marriage-
able times.
The new Rhodes scholar is Alan Morey. He is only
20, and is thus the youngest S. Australian to win
the scholarship. His mother and a younger brother
are going to England with him.
Dr. Burgess’s place as secretary of the Children’s
Hospital has been filled by Mr. W. H. Sharland, who
was for a good' many years in the service of the
South Australian Co. Dr. H. Gilbert and Dr. Risch-
beith are the new honorary surgeons.
The old Royal Exchange, wherein the Common-
wealth Bank is situated, was nearly wiped out by
fire on Tuesday evening. Also, the Register office
had a close shave. It is separated from the old Ex-
change by only the sort of narrow lane that a healthy
fire leaps across with ease.
The artistic Follen Bishops are at Victor Harbor.
The Oldest Inhabitant has sworn an affidavit that
the locality hasn’t been painted so comprehensively
and well since civilisation started to dawn there.
The H. P. Gills left for the Island Home of the
maddened Suffragette in the Benalla on Tuesday.
When they tear themselves away it will be by a
devious homeward route, comprising Switzerland and
Italy.
The Finches are leaving us, not only for Brisbane,
but for good.
Mr. Thomas Rhodes, who has been president of the
State Children’s Council for 22 years, resigned at the
last meeting. He and the Charles Goode knight are
the only remaining members of the second council,
which was appointed 25 years ago.
Francois Pinard, who has been in business here for
30 odd years, holds a medal for things he did in the
Franco-Prussian war of ’7O and ’7l. He was in
Paris during the siege, a festive period when fried
horse was a luxury and fricassee of large grey rat a
thing attainable only by the most bloated of pluto-
crats. One effect has been to make him as furious
a defence enthusiast as any man in Australia.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

These are the selectors who depleted their
country's timber by fire in order to make one
blade of grass grow where two trees grew
before.
“Gouger” : There is a spider in N.Q. that
always carries his life-saving apparatus with him.
Often in the rainy season he, in company with his
household effects, wife and other encumbrances,
is blown into the river. The spider then
produces a web and shapes a light bal-
loon on the end of it. A breeze springs up, the
balloon rise? slowly, the spider pays out the line,
and in ' time the balloon catches on an overhanging
branch, or on the mainland. The proprietor tightens
the line, and makes it fast to the log, or island, or
drowned missionary, or any other old affair he may
be stranded on. Then he climbs the hawser, and
commences the perils of life afresh.
“Silver Morn”: Lawyers have their uses
occasionally. Joe and I had just finished a fencing
contract and decided to have a few weeks’ spell at
the township. With a cheque for £2O and a few odd
sovereigns in his pocket, Joe waltzed me into the
Shearers’ Arms at the conclusion of a 16-mile walk.
Pretty soon he was well corned. Being a mild drinker,
I suggested that he should give me his cheque to look
after in case somebody balanced him for it, but he
told me he had already given it into the care of the
publican until the morning. When the morning came
and Joseph went for his wherewithal, the pub-keeper
indignantly denied having ever seen it. He was quite
annoyed about it, and, as Joe couldn’t remember any
witnesses of the transaction (if there were any), it
seemed clear that Joe was nailed. However, we went
over to the solitary lawyer of the place (he happened
to be a friend of mine), and explained the case. He
shook his head. “You’re done,” he said and sat
tapping his table, reflectively. Looking up suddenly
he exclaimed, “Dam ait, it’s fair, anyhow. Knock
about town to-day, have a drink now and again at
the pub where you are staying, pay for them on the
spot, but keep perfectly sober, and come to me at
five o’clock this evening.” Joe followed his instruc-
tions, and five o’clock found us once more within the
office. The lawyer handed my mate £4O in five- and ten-,
pound notes, with the following instructions. “About
eight o’clock to-night pretend to be pretty drunk,
take a friend with you to the pub, shout him a drink
or two, and while he is with you at the bar, hand
over to the publican these notes to look after for you,
until the morning. In the morning early, when you
can secure an opportunity of catching the boss alone,
ask him for your £4O. If he gives you the money,
count it before leaving the bar, and bring it to me,
but be sure that there is no one else about when you ask
for it.” Joe acted accordingly, got the money early
in the morning (the publican knew that in the event
of his refusal to part up Joe had a witness to
straighten things) and handed it over to the lawyer.
“Now,” said the Gent One, “get your witness of last
night, go to the pub scoundrel, and ask him for the
£4O you left with him overnight. If he refuses to
pay, summon him.” When Joe asked Bung to part up
the 40 quidlets a second time, the wrath of that
party was like a locomotive falling down a precipice.
He refused abso(blanky)lutely to pay over; so Joe
conveyed the argument to the local law court, where,
after carefully sifting the evidence, the P.M. gave
a verdict for the plaintiff. Mr. Public House’s few
subsequent remarks to Joe and me (I happened to be
Joe’s witness) glowed with ardor. They glowed so
much that I was afraid they’d set fire to the verdict
in my hip pocket, so we moved on to a milder
climate.
* * * *
“Oesten”: The cuckoo isn’t the only
loafer of the bush. I was once camped on the banks
of the Three Moon Creek. In the branches of the
big, gum under which we were sheltered was a pee-
wit’s nest. At feeding time I noticed that one of
the infants in the nursery was twice as large as the
others; and on closer inspection discovered it to be
a young storm-bird. As. the storm-bird and the
peewit are about as much alike as a gridiron and a
grindstone, I am puzzled to know why the old ’un did
not tumble to the deception and pass the intruder
out.
* * * *
“Gordon G.”: Here’s another yarn con-
nected with the Burke and Wills expedition which
has not yet been told, I fancy. A camel escaped
from the party near the Cooper’s Creek dep6t, and
wandered over in the direction of one of the small
settlements near Mount Hopeless. The first human
being it caught sight of was a
big buck Binghi asleep under a
gumtree. He was supposed to be
tailing cattle. The beast of bur-
den seemed to recognise an old
friend—probably it had in mind
some ancient Afghan driver. Any-
way, it sauntered along noiselessly
on its spongy soles till it got
right up to the abo. ; whereupon
it proceeded to lick him with a
long, thick, rasp-like tongue.
Jimmy Baboon (that was Binghi’s
appellation) had been suffering
from one of the worst varieties of
jimjams during the preceding
week. He screamed with terror
as he opened his eyes and gazed
on what he thought was the last
word in bunyips and debbil-deb-
bils. Then he raced for home,
five miles off, with the faithful
quadruped in close pursuit. It’s
a solemn fact that Jimmy didn’t
speak to a living soul for three
months after that experience.
When he at last recovered he
cracked one of his wives’ skulls to celebrate his re-
turn to sanity, and wound up his career in plain,
blank, unromantic quod.
* * * *
“Wallaby”: A mystified man up this way
is inquiring the relationship between a homing
pigeon and a plain hairy old goat. The lighthouse-
keeper at Point Charles (near Darwin, N.T.) has
a large flock of nannies, and recently introduced a
couple of angora gentlemen. On arrival at the
lighthouse the immigrants were tied up with the
rest of the herd for about a week, and then let out
with the mob to feed. The old nannies came home,
but no sign of the squires. Exhaustive search by a
mob of Binghis revealed tracks some five miles dis-
tant, and another five miles brought the trackers to
what was left of the two goats. They had set out
on an 80-mile journey, and when found were going,
as the crow flies, in a direct line for home and
mother, when they provided a meal for a mob of
dingoes.

"Cleveland”: Tlie Torres Strait native is
really an aquatic fowl. During the unpleasantness in
January last, which played skittles with the Inna-
mineka and Palmer, and sent the Dancing Wave to
her last port, a Murray Islander named Douglas left
the Yarrabah Mission Station (near Cairns, N.Q)
to try to reach Cairns in a motor launch measuring
35ft. overall. lie had two black brethren with him,
and had got nearly as far as False Cape when the
two brethren, not liking the solid chunks of atmo-
sphere that were flying round, mutinied and swam
ashore. Douglas then decided to meet the cyclone
on its native heath right where he was, so he
dropped the kellick and sat tight. About one next
morning he discovered that the anchor was not hold-
ing, so he gave her another small hedge, and tried
to remember how the Lord’s Prayer began. The
anchors still dragged, so Douglas fashioned two
stakes and went over the side, down the anchor
chains, and staked both anchors to the bottom.

swam off and begged to be taken back as prodigals

was agreed to on their forfeiting a week’s pay for

anchors that were staked; and if he goes on dis-

where the staking was done.
