A-THINTUN’.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No wedded woman in the land
A heart more joyful carried,
When, after many sought my ’and,
My Tom an’ me was married.
But long those ’appy marriage bells
’Ave ceased their ’appy chinkin’.
An’ in the loneliest of 'ells
I sit, an’ do be thinkin’.
The times was ’ard, but Tom was brave,
An’ I was strong for fightin’—
Ah, God! the womanhood I gave
That I ’is load might lighten.
The fight was fierce —’e gave it up,
An’ then he took to drinkin’;
So now I drain ’is bitter cup,
An’ sit ’ere, lonely, thinkin’.
The children came, as children do—
An’ God knows ’ow I loved them!
The world it was, not me, that to
Perdition’s edges shoved them.
I swear they never ’ad a chance;
The Bush was alwiz clinkin’
The awful chain of circumstance
That binds me ’ere, a-thinkin’.
Young Jack (’ow Mem’ry tortures me!) —
I can’t forget the feelin’:
The way my poor ’eart bled when ’e
Was gaoled for cattle-stealin’.
I often see it all again,
The ’eart within me sinkin’—
I feel again the chokin’ pain
When I sit ’ere a-thinkin’.
Then Mary gave ’er trust to Jim
When ’e with soft words sought ’er
(Let God forgive the sin of ’im,
But I’ll forgive my daughter).
They shroud the secret, but I know
That even now she’s slinkin’
Along a pavement, to and fro,
While I sit ’ere a-thinkin’.
An’ little Will (my eyes grow wet)
Ten years ago went drovin’;
’E’s never written to me yet—
I wonder where ’e’s rovin’.
’E said ’e’d think of “mummy dear,”
’ls sleepy eyes a-blinkin’—
I see it all through many a tear,
As I sit ’ere a-thinkin’.
Maybe it’s all what God ordains,
An’ p’r’aps it don’t much matter;
The children come in mother’s pains,
They grow—and then they scatter.
Yet sometimes in the embers cleft
I see their dear eyes winkin’—
The boys an’ girls who grew, an’ left
An’ old Bush mother thinkin’.
N.S.W.
R. J. CASSTDY.
“Eddysoii” : This is a story of luck —of
luck in which you and 1 don’t believe for one
moment. At Sandfly the hunt for the reef had been
going on for close upon 20 years, and Billy Lees
was the most assiduous hunter. Billy was only 36,
but he had commenced prospecting at 17, and he was
still at it, and as enthusiastic as when he had d”g
his first trench. Billy Lees had no other vice; he
was prodigal in nothing besides. He worked bard at
any old job that offered until he had accumulated
money for another prospe ting orgy, and then he had
one more dash at the con pletion of his grand scheme
of bisecting the ridge witi. .’sittings. A man couldn’t
miss it in those circumstances, he said, unless he
had the luck of a scalded cat. The reef had out-
cropped somewhere at Sandfly at some time; stray
old weather-worn specimens had been picked up, and
the alluvial along Weeping Creek had contained gold-
bearing stone. Every pioneer had a specimen or
two as a sort of guarantee of good faith. A com-
pany had put down a deep shaft, the diamond drill
had been at work in the district for 16 months, and
a score of ancient miners had wasted years
of their lives trenching Sandfly and miles of high
adjacent country without result. The new genera-
tion was bitingly satirical about the alleged quartz
lode. They regarded faith in its existence as an
indication of long-standing insanity, consequently
Billy Lees came in for many gibes. He stood the
elderly wheezes very well from most people; but
when Andy Jamieson began to throw off, Billy grew
restive, and eventually ended the matter by punching
Andy fair and hard on his rather prominent nose.
Andy put up a sort of a fight, but was finished in
less than half a round. You see, Andy was some-
thing of a new chum, and derision seemed to come
rather badly from a Scotch cockney who didn’t know
a quartz specimen from a cowslip. Besides, Lees
had been rather generous to Jamieson when he first
came into the district, had “lapsed” one hundred
acres of pretty good land to enable “Jammy” t®-
take it over, and had given him the two-roomed slab
hut built on the rise above Weeping Creek. In addi-
tion to all of which Andrew was now doing well with
poultry, and promising to do better with fruit, and
was going strong with Mary Billings, between whom
and Billy a sort of an engagement had existed for
two years past. But Andy was only 27, and looked
like being a fine provider, and Billy was 36, and
still wasting his substance in fanatical quest of the
Rainbow, which was the name given to the undis-
covered lode. No wonder Billy punched Andrew on
the nose. Andrew continued to jest in a Scotch-
cockney accent about Billy’s fatuous hunt, but he no
longer did it in Billy’s presence; he reserved his
choice humor for the edification of Miss Billings. A
month later came the announcement of the engage-
ment of Andrew and Mary. It was a hard knock on
the chin for Billy, but he put a good face on the
matter. When they were married Lees went on a
prospecting expedition along the Fly, and worked
harder than he had ever done before in his life. On
his return he found Sandfly in a state of unwonted
excitement. Weeble met him at the bridge. “Same
old luck, ’ said Billy. Weeble looked him sadly in
the eye. “Ain’t you heard?” he said. “Heard
what ?” “They’ve cut the reef.” “My God! you
don’t say. Where?” “Here.” “Who found her?”
“Andy Jamieson.” “Andy Jamieson!” Billy stared
at his friend like a man partially dilly. “Yes,” said
Weeble; “he struck it just nine inches under the
floor of your old kitchen in the slab hut the morning
of his wedding. The land is pegged out for miles
along the line.” Then Billy Lees left Sandfly.
Heaven knows where he went.

“P.H.”: The argument as to whether
townsmen or bushies read most cannot be settled
in “Albion’s” offhand style (B. 27/10/T3). The
reading habit varies in the bush. In Southern
Riverina, in the middle ’7o’s, every man and most
women were readers of fiction. On every station
there was a little library, usually run privately by
the storekeeper. Every general store in the town-
ships sold and exchanged books. Every hawker had
a few hundred volumes in stock. Sixpence a volume
was charged for exchanging. Swagmen often carried
two or three volumes apiece. Almost the first ques-
tion whalers asked when they met was, “What books
have you?” The favorite authors were such as
Dickens, Scott, Wilkie Collins, Charles Reade, Cap-
tain Marryat, Bulwer Lytton, Charles Lever, Alex.
Dumas, Victor Hugo and Eugene Sue. Byron,
Moore and Scott were the favorite poets. I met
scores of men who had read and could criticise
entertainingly more than half the works of all
these writers. A fair percentage were familiar
with the poets, and a larger number were conversant
with the incidents of the American Civil War and
the Franco-Prussian War. Napoleon and Welling-
ton, Gladstone and Disraeli, Henry Parkes and John
Robertson, had well-informed and enthusiastic ad-
mirers. Slush lamps often burned in the huts till
the small hours, to enable some fascinated devotee
to finish a novel. Conversation in the bends fre-
quently turned to literature, to the exclusion of cod,
bream, catfish and the liberality of sundry cooks
and storekeepers. On the other hand, in the sum-
mer of ’76-’77, I found the reading proclivities or
non-proclivities of the Tumut and Monaro workers
just as “Gip” (B. 20/11/’l3) says they are now.
On South-Western Queensland cattle stations in ’7B-
- I found readers few and far between, but the
exceptions were bright. Stockmen spent their leisure
talking shop, plaiting whips and “specking” opal.
The group of readers —rfbout 10—devoured Austra-
lian and British weeklies and periodicals, and read
pretty widely in fiction, poetry, history, biography
and philosophy. It was in this company—the best
I have ever met —I read Darwin's “Origin of
Species” and “Descent of Man,” Macaulay’s and
Gibbon’s histories, and saw my first copy of The
Bulletin. Then followed a period of deadness on
West Queensland sheep stations, with not a book to
be seen, till the great strikes of ’9O and ’9l turned
the attention of the workers to economics. Bel-
lamy’s “Looking Backward” and Blatchford’s “Merrie
England” then led in a new stream of literature
which increased in volume till I left the West in
1898. Next, a year in the N.Q. sugar country. Not
a book to be seen there. Nothing but work and
sleep. Blank ignorance and stupidity all around.
Now, as regards city workmen. Few read books.
A half-hour’s glance daily at the morning or evening
papers satisfies them. The attendance at Prahran
Free Library—a very good one—of easy access seven
days a week to a population of 60,000 or 70,000,
does not average 100 a day. But trade unions, pub-
lic speaking and picture shows supply education of
a sort. On the other hand, nearly every metropoli-
tan woman reads novels. Even the weary housewife
finds “surcease from sorrow” in penny “stories.”
“Caius Marius’': “A.G.” (B. 13/11/T3)
states that “the Adelaide River is a large stream
infested with alligators.” As a matter of fact
there are no alligators outside America and China.
A HUNT CLUB EPISODE.
MICKO : "So yer chucked Stinker outer the
club?”
DICKO : “ Yairs. The loon riz up ter thank
ole Mother Solomons , our patroness, for de
deener donation she slung us, an' mucked
it up a treat by makin' reference ter de
proverb, 'Don’t milk de ole cow dry .’”
The crocodile (O. porosus ) which belongs to Aus-
tralia is very similar to the sacred saurian of the
Ganges. In the alligator the hind legs have no
fringe, and there is no web on the toes. The
canine teeth fit into a pit on the outside of the
top jaw, whereas the canine teeth of the crocodile
actually protrude through two holes in the jaw
itself. The Northern croc, has five toes on the
fore feet, and four on the hind, each of the three
inner toes on all the feet being armed with a claw.
Professor Baldwin Spencer, when lecturing in Syd-
ney recently, made a wild statement that there
were alligators in Queensland, which induces from
me a loud adverb of negation against the authority
who denied the eucalypt its popular name, gum-
tree, and "denounced the bushman’s sacred name
for the tree-climbing marsupial, the ’possum.

“Kodak”: Has the cow a soul —and a
conscience? Is it troubled with doubts, like those
jumpy Russian novelists who explain their souls and
how they wear them in 600 pages of close print, to
the utter confusion of reviewers? What is the
difference between a cow and a
Russian novelist? Does the dif-
ference lie in the one strange,
beautiful fact that the cow can't
write about its soul? And (finally)
could the soul-explanatory Russian
novelist explain the cow's spacious
soul, if he were allowed six
volumes to do it in ? These queries
are prompted by the news that a
cow of Gisborne (Vic.), whose
milk yield had fallen away to
half the usual amount, suddenly
went mad (or sane), and galloped
through several paddocks and
three wire fences before jumping
a 6ft. hawthorn hedge into the
ground of the Catholic presbytery.
My opinion is that the conscience-
stricken cow suddenly discovered
that it had a soul and hurried
off to consult his Reverence about
it. But, before it could do so, it
was shot by a policeman named
Murphy, who probably attends
Mass regularly under the mis-
taken impression that he has something to save.
* * * *
“Warrien”: The bushman can’t help
being unconventional. Even when he is calling a
public meeting he gets out of the rut. For in-
stance, this notice, placarded on a tree in our
district: —-
LABOR.
Messrs. Smithers and McMullen will be here
Saturday night. Come any time after 7 o’clock.
The yarn starts at 8 o’clock. Accommodation
guaranteed.
There was no doubt about the accommodation —
millions of acres of it.
“Whip-Poor-Will”: Shelford (Vic.) and
district are making a big willow tree on the bank
of the Leigh River a sort of shrine. In 1853 ex-army
Capt. Ormond, in a tour of the world, paid a visit
to Napoleon’s grave, and secured a cutting from the
weeping willow over the Man of Destiny’s resting
place. He brought the stick out to Australia with
him, struck Shelford (then a halting place for wag-
gons travelling to and from the Ballarat diggings)
and planted the cutting on the bank of the Leigh,
where it has thrived wonderfully. As a picturesque
sight the willow fairly loses S.A.’s old gumtree at
Glenelg.
“Lagani-Namo”: When a death occurs
among the Papuans of the Bartholomew Range coun-
try the body is put on a platform in the village,
and a leaf is placed on its breast. It is then man-
oeuvred so that the feet point toward the various
neighboring villages in turn. The corpse is asked
whether anyone in such village is responsible for its
deadness. If the leaf blows off, the people know
that someone in that village pourri-pourried (magi-
cally slew) their brother. On one occasion a woman
had died, and some of her connections, in passing
through another village where a dance was in pro-
gress, saw her ghost cavorting among the perform-
ers. They got up a full head of steam and sprinted
home to their own village constable, who set out
instanter, arrested three of the likeliest-looking canni-
bals, and dragged them through streams and over
mountains to the Resident Magistrate on a charge of
murder. When the R.M. ordered their acquittal and
release the native V.C. poured out his disgust and
tore home raving about kava-kava (silly) white men
who don’t know evidence when they see it.
L.L). . 1 alking of r whale in r match-
box,” said Mackaboy, the ambidextrous and versa
tile pump hand behind the bar, “what do you think
■of a lion in a wardrobe? It all happened to Clar-
ence Bartlot, drummer, representing Umber, Umber
and Nicholson, American tinned stuff and mixed
groceries. Clarence was a fair man, with a
trim figure, a large, lucent eye of forget-me-not
blue, a rich, Saxon complexion, and a nest of the
sweetest blonde whiskers that ever blew. He came
to do the Wimmera with a carload of cases ham-
pers and bags, and made Joseph’s hotel at Hamilton
his head centre. From there he radiated in 17
directions, working a winning smile that was the
envy of every bagman south of the equator, and
laying a two-foot deposit of Chicago pork and beans
in country that had hitherto been humbly content
with mutton and scraps of superannuated bullock
On the third night of his stay at Joseph’s, Clarence
Bartlot was disturbed in the midst of refreshing
dreams of orders by a curious purring sound below
his window. Clarence had a health fad about open-
air sleeping, and his windows gaped their utmost.
Angered by the persistence of the purr, our Mr
Bartlot arose and flocked to the window. What he
saw caused him to retreat with marked unanimity
So precipitately did he retire upon his base that he
tripped over a rug, and hit the hotel. At the same
moment a large, dark object loomed against the
crescent moon, and hurled itself with unnecessary
violence through the open window. The large lion
for such it was, missed Clarence Bartlot by a hare’s
length, and was hurled head-first against the mir-
rored door of a great oak wardrobe. The door
caved in, and the king of beasts went with it;
but it rocked off its balance, came over, and crashed
down on top of the lion, its unhinged door just
accommodating Leo. The net result of these pro-
ceedings was an adult male lion imprisoned in a
orostrate wardrobe. With readiness of mind not at
all remarkable in a commercial traveller, Clarence
hurled half a ton of his case goods on top, and
added his own 14 stone. Then he began to emit
sounds. I was one of the first to respond, and,
gathering that Mr. Bartlot had some desire to
weigh down the wardrobe, I swung the iron bed
across it, and Clarry and I added the chest of
drawers and then surmounted the pile with our
own proper persons. While the rest of the hotel
was stirring like a ravaged anthill and making
passionate outcry about fire and thieves, Bartlot
and P. Mackaboy listened to the strange sounds
emanating from the wardrobe, strange, crunching,
gluttonous sounds. ‘Mother in Heaven!’ whispered
Clarence, ‘he’s eating my samples.’ It appeared to
be so. The sounds we heard were identical with
those a strenuous beast of the cat tribe would make
in squeezing the nutriment out of large tins of
potted hog. ‘Swosh’ went a tin, and Leo slopped
and sloshed like a recently-enriched bookmaker tak-
ing in a quart of Scotch broth and two plates of
Irish stew. ‘There goes one bag of potted prawns,’
sighed Clarence, ‘and a suit case of Chicago stewed
frankfurts to follow.’ Clarence had stowed some
hampers of samples in the wardrobe, and no reason-
able doubt remained ; Leo was taking in the nutri-
ment and was in no haste to get out. He had come
home to a late dinner of six courses, and meanwhile
his business with C. Bartlot could wait. Eventu-
ally the policeman and seventeen lion-hunters from
Gaylor’s Circus came along, and took all responsi-
bility off our hands. Clarence and I joined the
proprietor and the rest of the regular lodgers on
the roof and the chimneys, till Leo was enticed
out of the wardrobe, which did not happen till he
had mopped up all Bartlot’s edible samples and
wiped his obscene whiskers with the drummer’s dress
clothes.”

“Larrikin”: What wonder that native
game is disappearing? Less than two years ago,
on the Ashburton (W.A.), I saw one consignment
of 7000 kangaroo skins! In Queensland on one
station alone, 785 dingoes, 10,465 kangaroos am
75,989 wallabies were brought to the receiver * n
months. One scalper, who employed a large number
of hands, white and discolored, brought in 43,720
wallaby scalps.
A DAINTY DISH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Both inland and coastal tribes practise cannibal-
ism. The inland tribes boil their captives. On the
Kikori prisoners of war are roasted, the river tribes
despising inland methods of cookery.
-—Papuan official report.
Where bucks have mops of fuzzy hair
And clothes are worn by few or none;
Where black’s the girl they label fair
And fighting counts for purest fun;
Where meat’s a diet hardly won
By stalwart braves who bludgeons swing,
You’ll hear them chant when battling’s done
“A Roasted Foeman is the Thing!”
The coastal warrior leaves his lair
And dances jocund in the sun,
And romps to battle huge and bare,
His heaven on earth a fight begun.
With mighty blows that crush and stun
He breaks the inland warriors’ ring,
And howls behind them as they run;
“A Roasted Foeman is the Thing!”
For boiling let the hillmen care,
A race whose life is dull and dun ;
But river-folk love dainty fare
And paltry stews and bakings shun;
Not furious Frank nor fiery Hun
Could mightier blows in battle fling
Than these who cry from sire to son:
“A Roasted Foeman is the Thing!”
Envoi.
Of lordly dishes many a one
Do cunning cooks concoct and bring;
But fierce Kikori takes the bun—-
A Roasted Foeman is the Thing !

Papua's brown skins, is now arranging for
the absorption of the faintly-tinctured
people of Norfolk Island. The strain de-
rived from the mutineers and dark skins in
the Pitcairn days caused Fisher to go slow
when absorption was broached; but the
Cook Government has taken it on. The
little island has been hawked and buffeted
about for nearly a century. For years it
was identified with tragedies—mainly be-
cause it had been a depot for murderers.
When the British Government tore it away
from N.S.W. and attached it to Tasmania,
worse followed. Now, however, all is quiet:
the simple life takes the place of lurid de-
lirium. Officials in the old days, even when
they got away from the island, didn’t get
away from its evil fortune. The first
magistrate sent from Tasmania was drowned
on return, and its last was murdered at
Pentridge.
“Watch Below”: The Volturno tragedy
rams home the weak points of a liner’s boat
equipment. What sense is there in yelling
about “Boats for All” when a rough sea
smashes them as quickly as they can be
slung over the side? A modern steamer
doesn’t carry real seamen; and it takes a
full crew of expert oarsmen to pull a boat
clear of a rolling hull, especially when half
the boats have to be launched to windward,
and a 70-mile blow and a billion-ton sea are
at work. As a liner doesn’t carry the neces-
sary oarsmen there is only one thing to be
done —Insist that they carry mechanical
power. The Boats and Davits Committee
appointed after the Titanic disaster (which,
if it had occurred in a Volturno sea, would
almost certainly have resulted in the smash
of every boat and the drowning of every
soul) recommended that a liner should
carry one or more motor life-boats. There
is no difficulty in finding efficient marine
motors. They exist now, and will work
effectively at any tilt or angle, from straight-
up-on-end to upside-down, and will keep on
calmly churning out knots even if broken
water is flooding the boat every other
minute. A motor-lifeboat would be almost
safe, or at least 99 times safer than a row-
boat manned by cooks, waiters and firemen.
The second it touched the water a turn of
the hand would set its horse-power at work
and the boat would jump clear, where the
oared boat must either have its oars sticking
out and risking smash as the boat swings
down, or keep them inboard till lowered and
then waste precious seconds getting them
ready. And when they are out any oars-
man knows the contract it is to get way on
a boat loaded gun’P deep with passengers
and with people cluttering up and hamper-
ing every stroke. Abandoning ship is a
bad-enough job in the Atlantic, but at least
they can wireless for assistance there and
get the shelter of another liner’s lee and
oil-smoothed waters. But there are some
ocean stretches on the Australian route_ and
eoast where a ship might wireless itself
black in the face, and sink or burn a dozen
times over before assistance buzzed up. It’s
bad enough to drown when you have to,
but it would be most annoying to be drowned
knowing that the price of a motor might
have saved the unpleasantness.

Y.Z. : Being called upon to live down
an unfortunate advertisement arising from
a particularly succulent divorce case, one
Melbourne caravansary of the non-alcoholic
sort, where guests introduce their own mtox-
icants in bottle or bulk, suddenly developed
an awful severity of demeanor and a striking
moral stringency in its rules. The guest,
old-established or casual, who wished to en-
tertain a former relative by marriage or a
spinster aunt from Pip lip or Pipoone,
found it more difficult than landing a ship-
ment of pure Greenland ice m Gehenna,
Ingle was living at the Decorum. He had
been there about five weeks, when his wife
arrived from Ballarat. Ingle, forgetful of
6be. stringent rules of the establishment,
which under strictly new management was
now. opened with prayer, took Mrs. Ingle
straight, to his room. The influence of evil
stirred in the chaste atmosphere. Shocked
underlings hastened to the good superin-
tendent, and made horrified whisperings in
his ear. The superintendent was a dour man
with a Wesleyan upper lip and a Baptist
conscience that was dipped daily. He went
upstairs with the demeanor of a Galvin
about to eject devils. He knocked on Ingle s
door. Then he opened it, and stood at the
portal, a figure of Retribution. Ihis will
never do,’ he said. You know the rules with
regard to introducing ladies, Mr. Ingle,
“But I am his wife, cried Mrs. Ingle, with
all the dignity a ring confers. Excuse me,
madam,” the presiding elder responded
frigidly, ‘ this young man has already had
more wives here than the rules allows,
Then the trouble began. Ingle escaped
divorce by the narrowest possible margin or
points, and he will never be happy again till
he meets that manager alone in a large
paddock.

bapphire s hard sense :
Some pointed remarks are called for regarding the
supervision of N.S.W. public school youngsters when
they make their descent, as per programme, on the
swimming baths. For eight summers I was fool enough
to take some 100-odd boys—and, at times, girls—
for their weekly swim. Those who couldn’t swim
paddled, and at the end of the season they couldn’t
paddle any better. The whole business as at
present “conducted” is idiotic. The kid who can
swim doesn’t need a tutor, and the teacher is too
busy looking after the kid who thinks he can, and
can’t, to bother about the one who knows he cant
(That is, when the teacher is busv. Sometimes he pre-
fers to find a cosy seat ) Sort out, I say, the youngsters
who can’t swim, and have a day for non-swimmers.
Put them in the hands of an expert —every fool can’t
teach swimming—and when they have learnt to
swim, and know one or two things about life-saving,
get a fresh batch under way. The youngster who
can swim will come along on the other days, and
enjoy half an hour without being grabbed by a sink-
in? class-mate

The Oldest Inhabitant on the Rocks : —
In re-forming the oldest part of Sydney—a part
that used to be known as Bunker’s Hill—extraordi-
nary havoc has been made among 1 what were once
the homes of our “nobility.” The place wherein
Conrad Martens, the. artist, lived is now levelled. So
is the house of Dr. Mitchell, father of the donor of
the library ; the son was born there. Higher up the
Rocks some of the old houses still remain. That of
Captain Joseph Moore, close upon a century old, and
Colne House, the old-time residence of Colonel Wil-
son, P.M. and newspaper proprietor, have so far
escaped. From Harrington-street that was you can
look at the rear of the house where W. B. Dailey
was born, in Lower George-street. Alongside are the
ruins of a watch-house of the ’SO’s. Some of the
old cottages of the Macquarie era stand high up,
perched out of reach by reason ©f the alteration of
the levels. The oldest house in the localitv is pro-
bably that standing at the end of Globe-street, and
now occupied by a Chinese. This street was named
bv Macquarie Suffolk-lane, and the house in question
was, in all probabilitv. that of Mrs. Redmond, who
had a license in 1813 to sell liquors.

From a Northern garden :
The influx of colored aliens into the fruit-growing
industry in the Bowen district is directly responsible
for the horrible condition of the fruit trade between
“of WrtSfZSS
by our yellow- and brown bothers, Tommy Ah Ch-ii
and Mahommed Shah, that a whole shipment of
otherwise good fruit by the Kanowna was con-
demned. It is not the white orchardists who are
responsible for the presence of fruit fly and maggot
in the cucumber and tomato of Bowen extraction
The blame rests on the discolored alien, who is as
filthy about his garden as he is about himself. Nor
do the shipping cos. give decent help. Their refrig-
erators are not nearly big enough to cope with even
the existing trade, much less help to develop it. The
remedy is apparent. Let the white fruit-growers
combine and charter or buy a steam tank of their
own. A chartered boat would almost pay for her
salt in the freights she would make on her return
trip. But Bowen won’t do any chartering; it will
put up with wholesale loss of good money and trade,
and chance contracting leprosy and other nice
things too numerous to mention, rather than get a
slight hustle on. Bowen gives me the fantods every
time I think of the darned place,
Th Oldest Inhabitant’—
,
George Ranken, mentioned by Archivist (B.
27/11/’l3) among the pioneers of the Bathurst
(N.S.W.) Plains, settled on the Kelso side of the
Macquarie River ; where now is Bathurst was the
convict side, and free settlers were not allowed
across. Mr. Ranken arrived about 1823; his son
James was born there in 1825. There were no
means of getting from Keloshiels and the other
Ranken properties to Bathurst, so Ranken built a
fine bridge below Esrom, at Eglinton. The construc-
tion of this bridge gave rise to a deal of criticism,
and o ne day a young man from Sydney, buying gold
for his uncle, strongly condemned the span. George
Ranken happened to be (it was in the Royal
Hotel), and bore with the criticism for a time. At
last he said, “Young man, that bridge is strong
enough to carry all your race to Jerusalem, and it
will stand until your great Bathurst bridge carries
it away.” The young gold-buyer was Saul Samuel,
afterwards Agent-General in London and a baronet.
In 1855 the Ranken and Denison Bridges were opened
for traffic. In June, 1867, the Denison Bridge, which
connected Bathurst and Kelso, was swept away in a
heavy flood, and in its course it carried on the
Ranken Bridge, thus fulfilling the builder s pro-
phecy. George Ranken and his son James displayed
great heroism in this flood, saving many lives at
the risk of their own. The carrying-away of the
Denison Bridge was a dramatic affair. It was
crowded with people watching the racing waters,
and the men who were trying to guide the great logs
that were hurtling down. Suddenly the middle arch
cracked. The crowd parted and rushed to the near-
est bank, and were hardly on land when the whole
structure went with a roar.

A TIMELY THOUGHT.
LITTLE JOE : “I'm sure Greggy Wade’s en-
joyin’ himself; but now I remember that the
Senate was against me goin' swimmin' last
time I mentioned it, so perhaps I’d best not!”
The N.S.W. experience on Saturday
showed that the second ballot isn’t so much
a contrivance for securing absolute majori-
ties as it is a plan for giving the tired elector
a second chance to turn up. At Willoughby,
for instance, a thousand more votes were
polled at the second flutter than at the
first; and in that and other cases the second
contest was decided by people who hadn’t
voted at all on the first time of ask-
ing. Now 50 or so defeated candidates
are entitled to say that they would have won
if the electors in their districts had been
given a second chance; and why one lot
should be given a second chance and another
lot be restricted to one chance isn’t as nlain
as it might be.
In Anew of its complete dependence on the
pawnshop, perhaps we had better call Hol-
man the leader of the Laboraham party.
Senator lie Largie, the chairman of the
Chinn Inquiry Board, has further distin-
guished himself by asserting his objection to
Australian Naval Defence. He has always
been opposed to the establishment of a
national fleet, he says. Probably his appro-
val could be secured by putting Chinn in
command of it.
It is in dry seasons like this that the
\ ictorian mallee doesn’t seem much of a
substitute for a graduated land tax and
the breaking-up of the well-watered sheep-
lands of the Victorian Western District. A
pre-harvest estimate gives the three counties
of the Western District an average of 165,
18| and 17 bushels of wheat, while in the
three counties of the mallee country the
cocky has had all his toil for 6£, 6f and 10
bushels respectively.
The Westralian Agent-General has been
telling John Bull that every sovereign he
has lent that State has been put into the
same old permanent and reproductive under-
takings. It is to be hoped J. Bull never
reads the reports of the State’s Auditor-
Weneral, otherwise he may ask the State’s
stipendiary ear-biter what interest he ex-
pects to get from the loan money which is
spent on buying meals for immigrants while
they await billets in Perth. Bull might even
ask him whether the meals are permanent
improvements, and, if so, how long he rec-
kons they will last with care.

people, in spite of D. R. Hall’s strong advo-

As a side-line, the Tasmanian Govern-
ments coldly-received Bill to abolish by-
elections made the sensible proposal that
when a State Parliamentarian wants to con-
test a Federal seat, his State seat shall be
kept open for him until after the Federal
poll is taken. Last Federal elections State
-Labor-member Woods was barred from con-
testing a seat for the Senate because the
State Labor party feared that his vacated
seat could not be regained by a Labor man.
If this becomes law, next Federal elec-
tions may perhaps see Premier Solomon up
as a candidate for the House of Reps. That
is, if his health improves, and if he doesn’t
accidentally fall out of the Attorney-
Generalship into a judgeship in the mean-
time. Strange and unexpected things occur
in and about politics. And among the most
unexpected is the number of patches it is
found necessary to solder on to the electoral
system of Tasmania.
Election fragment from “H.S.” :
The Second Ballot results in N.S.W. were made
known on Saturday night, and simultaneously many
notable events occurred. O. G. Wade asked himself
if there might not be something Avrong with his
leadership, after all. Joseph Carruthers, William
McMillan and Philip Morton, while presenting an
aspect of courteous gloom in public, found it all they
could do to refrain from smiling broadly in the
privacy of their homes. Alice Wilkins and Dan Levy
were unanimous in regarding the whole occurrence
as an outrage. W. H. Wood accepted the indignity
of _ being flung out of St. George in the philosophic
spirit of a man ivell used to being flung out of
places, he having been heaved out of the House at
u 6 r . m f, ance of everv Speaker, from McCourt to
H. D. Morton. The Sydney morning papers resolved
that the first leader-writer who again used the
phrases, ‘turn of the tide,” “marked reaction against
Labor tyranny, and “wave of Liberal enthusiasm sweep-
ing triumphantly over the country,” should be sacked.
Labontes to the number of 27 found themselves in
a position to continue their overdrafts on satisfactorv
terms, simultaneously 44 tradesmen feverishly seized
pen, ink and paper and wrote informing five potential
Liberal Ministers and 14 members of Wade’s rank and
hie that immediate payment of disgracefully overdue
accounts would oblige. H. D. Morton, brooding over
the fact that the Speakership is a lucrative and
honorable billet, wished that the Holman majority
had been a shade less—something in the vicinity of.
say, one. Upwards of 3000 Orangemen proclaimed
joyously that it was the greatest victory for Protes-
tantism since the Battle of the Boyne. A similar
number of militant R.C.’s remarked that the Church
had made her power felt once more. A suburban
lady Liberal who had gone to the expense of providing
the lower orders of her electorate with three elec-
tioneering teas, mentioned that the sickening ingrati-
tude of the proletariat was worse than that of any
serpent. Joseph Cook said nothing, his feelings
being too deep for coherent speech. G. H Reid
shook himself warmly by the hand. The State
Governor and the Legislative Council remained
apathetic, knowing well that no danger menaced
them. On the other hand, far away in London a
panic-stricken Hebrew moneylender hastily barri-
caded the front door of the pawnshop, and "prepared
for the Avorst.
The N.S.W. Labor crowd put in some
quick, smart work in connection with the
second-ballot campaign in the Sydney sub-
urbs. The necessity for the second ballot
was, of course, not known until late on
Saturday night; yet on Monday night the
campaign was in full swing, with Holman
and Griffith in the front rank of the speaking
lines. Moreover, the placards were out on
Monday morning in time for many people
to see them on their way to work.
“ REDUCTION .”
STOUT PARTY : “ What's all the crowd about
THE OTHER : "Oh, they voted ‘Reduction,' and they’re expecting beer for tuppence now . "
At the local-option poll Mosman (N.S.W.)
carried “reduction.” There are only two
hotels in the electorate, one at each end, and
over one mile apart. The Act empowers
the special Court to reduce the number of
hotels up to a maximum of 25 per cent. —
or one in four —where reduction has been
carried- Moreover, the Act is mandatory;
the Court shall carry into effect the deter-
mination of the electors. So the licenses
must be reduced, but they may not be
reduced beyond 25 per cent. ; and as 25 per
cent, of two is one-half of one there are going
to be Costs for somebody.
Archibald, M.H.R., relieved the dullness
of a debate on the Australian Notes Bill by
remarking that Bruce Smith was at once
the most pompous and the most ignorant
person in the House. To which the blas-
phemed Bruce replied that Archibald was a
gramophone who talked like a parrot. After
all, _ politics is considerably indebted to Mr.
Smith. One way and other he is respon-
sible for a lot of what is euphemistically
known as “personal tone in the proceed-
ings.” He invites abuse even in repose.

State Parliament. On the whole it wasn’t
alarmed, believing in the words of the presi-

occurred would clear the air and leave the
way open for the formation of a united
Liberal party, with such a Minister leading

Macartney Abbott, who defeated ex-
Speaker Willis for the Upper Hunter
(N.S.W.), is a son of ex-Speaker Abbott.
He is a solicitor.
N.S.W. mining-town seats are still the
seats to fall into if a man wants the com-
fort of a thumping majority. For Cessnock,
Kearsley polled 5597, and his most success-
ful opponent 560; Wallsend gave Estell
5671. and his nearest neighbor less than a
thousand; at Wickham, Grahame’s majority
was two to one ; the undertaker of the new
House, Jabez Wright, scored 4715 against
W. J. Ferguson’s 1021 for Griffith’s old
Willyama (Broken Hill suburban) nest;
Cann (Broken Hill) had a walk-over; and
Newcastle, Cobar and the South Coast coal
district gave a block vote on the same ruy-
party-right-or-wrong principles.
The displacement of Victoria’s Watt Min-
istry once again stresses the stupidity of the
system of Party Government. The loss of
the Redistribution of Seats Bill, under
which six country electorates would have
had as much voting power as 12 metropoli-
tan districts, matters little; but with the
change of Government no less than 75 bills
go overboard. Many of these were of prime
importance; most would, with amendments,
have received the support of both Houses.
Of course everything had been put off to the
last nioment (Weary w T as tired, Watt pro-
crastinated, and none of the others counted
for much) ; so the session that began with
a full programme—supported by 44 members
and nominally opposed by 20 —closes with
hardly a measure passed, hardly a thing
done.
JOE COOK’S DISSERTATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I could stand my horful triles, I could greet you
wropped in smiles,
I could ’earken to your speeches every day ;
In the ’Ouse I’d be as bland as a girl ’oo ’olds your
’and.
’Cause I rather like the glory—and the pay.
I’d consent to give an’ take just fer dear ole frien’-
ship’s sake,
I’d forgive yer every cunning little rooze;
Yes, even Senate blokes I’d allow their little jokes
If it wasn’t fer yer langwidge, Mr. ’Ughes!
W’ieh I’d like yer to recall that when Joey Cook
wuz small
’E was brought up very strict an’ very nice ;
’E was taught say “Please” an’ “Thank’ee,” and ’e
never once said “Blanky,”
Though ’e might ’ave ’ear'd it mentioned once or
twice.
On the ’ustings I may scream, but I’d scorn for to
blaspheme,
And to call a bloke a cow I would refuse;
Eor I ’ope an’ trust this day that my tongue will
never stray
To the langwidge of the pot-’ouse, Mr. ’Ughes.
If a aitch or two should drop w’en I’m caught upon
the ’op,
At any rate I trust my lips are clean ;
That I ’aven’t, first or last, said a blanky nor a
blast,
Nor anythink the Court could call obscene.
I may ’ave bin a rat from a party—wot of that?
I still ’ave got a character to lose;
And I thank the Lord to-night that they never ’ad to
write
My evidence on paper, Mr. ’Ughes!
I’m Prime Minister Joe Cook, w’ich will ’ave you
brought to book
For evil words I’ve ’eard the Senate say ;
1 will ’ave you branded yet as a cursin’ suffragette,
Yes, twist an’ plot an’ wriggle as you may.
I need never make no tax, nor I needn’t pass no acks,
Them things is only trifles to amuse;
I will sweep this country clean as the foe of the
obscene.
While you perish in a pot-’ouse, Mr. ’Ughes!
N.S.W.
A. M.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
How a would-be blackmailer got diddled is
one of this season’s Tatt. Sweep stories. He seri-
ously intended to scratch his precious gee for a cer-
tain race (although the animal was quite “fit and
seasoned for the passage,” in a Hamlet way of speak-
ing) if he could not frighten the Tatt. ticket holder
into promising him a fat percentage of the prize
money in the event of a win or a place. The gee
was paraded as a doubtful starter what time the
owner was waiting for developments. Eventually
he got on the track of the ticket holder, or his agent,
and demanded the best offer the lucky person could
make. This was forthcoming verbally, and accepted.
The horse ran and won. But when the owner applied
for his big slice of sweep money the person he had
interviewed denied that he was the real ticket owner.
Also, he denied that he had made any serious offer at
all. So the would-be blackmailer got never a bean.
It is to be hoped that this incident will direct the
stewards’ attention to Mr. Tookdown’s precious gee
next time he brings it out for blackmail purposes.
The A.J.C. (Sydney) has dropped upon
F. W. Coombes to fill the hole left by J. Hogan as
deputy-stipendiary steward. The gap was caused by
Hogan accepting a similar job in S. Aus. F. W.
Coombes is an authority on most matters connected
with sport, and, as sporting editor of Wellington
(M.L.) Times, put in much time with the upraised
axe on turf practices which smelt like week-old
prawns. Like brother Dick Coombes, the newly-
appointed stipendiary dotes on amateur athletics, and
will probably lend a friendly shoulder to the wheel
of amateurism in the Ma State in spare moments,
* * * *
The High Court (Eng.) recently caused
consternation among motor-cyclists by ruling that the
law of 1904 requires motor-cyclists to carry a tail
light. Motor-bikes do not carry their numbers at
the back, so the light can be useful only to prevent
the motor-bikist being overtaken and run down; and
considering what an infernal row the average motor-
bike makes, there does not seem much chance of that.
Still, if a tail-light makes for increased safety, it
should be compulsory. One trouble is that the
motor-bike is not fitted to carry this light; but that
can be remedied at very little cost.
From particulars to hand by mail it seems
that Percy Lambert’s car did not capsize on the
banking at a turn—a statement which suggested that
the banking of the Brooklands track was not scientifi-
cally correct, and unsafe for speeds of over 100 miles
per hour. What really happened was that a back
tyre burst, and, jambing between the wheel and the
axle, cause a terrific one-sided strain. When the
wheel skidded, this strain caused the car to swing
right over and hurl Lambert out. He was travelling
at 109 miles an hour when the smash occurred.
* * * *
A dispute is raging as to where the first
Australian automobile track-race meeting was held.
Records in possession of Harry B. James indicate
that the pioneer affair was the Victorian Auto Club’s
meeting at Maribyrnong Racecourse on April 30, 1904.
The second was at Aspendale Park (Vic.) on Novem-
ber 7 and 9, 1906.
There was some daring, not to say reck-
less, motor-cycle racing at Canterbury Racecourse
(Sydney) on Saturday, when the Sydney Club held a
meeting to raise funds for the local hospital. Con-
sider the spectacle of men racing round a grass track
without any banking whatever, at speeds ranging
from 50 to 60 miles an hour. That is what these
reckless wights did, and got through without an acci-
dent, too! The final of the All-Comers’ Handicap
was a fine sight and fiercely contested over a distance
a shade under five miles ; the winner, Pearce, cut it
out in. smin. 20sec. He was riding a 2J-h.p. machine,
and had hung desperately to the wheel of F. Rose,
on a 6-h p. jigger. Suddenly the high-powered
machine lost its speed, and before it could be re-
covered the lighter-powered one had swept past and
won. At one part of the race they swung round a
totally unbanked turn at fully 60 miles an hour.
Evidently we have some mighty skilful riders run-
ning loose in this country.
Man hath sought out many inventions, and
keens; on doing it. One of the most useful connected
with motoring or cycling is the “anti-glare cap,”
devised by the well-known English motoring expert,
H. Sturmey. It is an ordinary cap with a trans-
parent screen of some colored material, which in-
tercepts the violet rays in the glaring light that
pours from an automobile’s lamps. After passing
a car at night cyclists and other motorists are tem-
porarily blinded. This anti-glare does awav with
that. Pull down the screen, block out the violet rays
and the sight is as clear after passing the lights as it
was before.
Some of the Yankee firms are trying hard
to boom the big 7-h.p. twin-cylinder motor-bikes. No
motor-cyclist, unless he wants to achieve fame and
sudden death on the racing tracks we have not got
in Australia, requires 7 h.p. on his bike. For road
work 7 h.p is no better than 2# or 3# h.p., but it
eats petrol about twice as fast. Even if one wants
to take his friend’s best wife out in the tandem or
side-car attachment, 5 h.p. is ample to drive the
thing anywhere. The fact that some of the big
Amurkan manufacturing houses are subsidiary com-
panies in the Standard Oil Trust may have some-
thing to do with the attempt to force on riders these
voracious oil-devourers.
The N.S.W. League of Wheelmen got out
of its coffin and broke hilariouslv into a champion-
ship meeting at Sydney Sports Ground on Saturday
night. The chief events were the N.S.W. Mile and
the 25-Mile chamnionships, and they were both a
gift to G. H. Horder. He won the third heat of the
Mile and then routed the opposition in the final,
winning in 2min. Bsec. by three lengths from Ham-
mond, with Mcßeath third. In the long-distance
event he never once looked a winner till he was
actually It. At the sprint he was lying almost last,
with Les Hammond a good 100yds. ahead. Then
Hammond’s tyre burst, and he ran off. Horder un-
wound a beautifully-timed sprint, and was going
great guns when Erskine came at him in a way that
looked very business-like, and Priestly was hovering
close by. But Border managed" to get an extra
twirl or two out of his hind feet, and by two lengths
beat Erskine, who dished Priestlv bv just half a
length. The time was all but record—sBmin. 4 3-ssec.
Horder was carried shoulder-high from the ground.
Bananaland collars the amateur bike glory
for the coming season, when the Australasian half-
mile and one, five and 10 miles events will be held
in the northern end of Australia.
The Maoriland Cricket XI. opened its
Australian campaign with a match against N.S.W.
Northern Districts at West Maitland on Friday. The
visiting batsmen were not at ease in their first
innings, and though the bowling was anything but
virulent they succeeded in making only 228. But it
was enough, for what the Maoriland batsmen lacked
the bowlers made up for, and the local team was
smashed up for a miserable 120. Sandman was the
chief destroying angel, taking three wickets for 23.
So the Maorilanders started on the second innings
with 128 to the good, and, feeling more at home,
batted with grace. With seven wickets down and
285 on the board, they declared. Northern District
recommenced 393 behind, and the hopelessness of the
job broke its collective heart. The effort was a
most feeble one; only three men reached double
figures, and the top score (Mackay’s) was 24. Sand-
man took eight wickets for 29 runs, and Reese one
for 17, and the side vanished for a wretched 90,
leaving Maoriland on top by 303. The visitors will
find something very different in Sydney.
* * * *
Although Queensland XI put up a good
stand at Sydney Cricket Ground, the inter-State
cricket match ended in a regular rout. By steady atten-
tion to business Queensland piled up 311 on the
first day, Fennelly 78, Rowe 53 and Redgrave 48
being the top scorers. The destroyer was the reli-
able Folkard, who smashed seven wickets for 65
runs. At the drawing of stumps N.S.W. had lost
two for 71, and Queensland decided there was no
cause for alarm. The disaster commenced on Satur-
day, when Macartney and Scott collared the bowling,
with 195 and 100 respectively, and the others gave
such good backing that a mountainous score of 571
was heaped up. It looked a hopeless task, but the
Queensland boys made a start on it. This time,
however, Kelleway fell on them and got seven of
them for 35 ; and the innings ended for 232, leaving
N.S.W. on top with an innings and 28 runs to
spare.
THE KANGAROO “STUNT."
How jumping Jehoshaphat Stone tickled Aus-
tralians while defeating his brother Yank, Pal
Brown, at Sydney Stadium.
Saturday saw the conclusion of the inter-
rupted fifth round of Vic. Pennant cricket, when the
still-undefeated North galloped over Fitzroy—2sB
in its first innings to ’Roy’s 174. South surprised
East by answering its first effort of 221 with 311,
which any competent mathematician will declare was
an advantage of 90. P’ran, faced with Colling-
wood’s moderate 279, failed to grasp the nettle, and
could only collect 196. St. Kilda, in reply to
Northcote’s 151, whacked 307 for seven wickets, and
then declared. But when it proceeded to demolish
the Brickmakers a second time it failed to get the
desired three points. ’Varsity, having erected the
steep tally of 473 for six wickets on the earlier
Saturday (Park 209 and Doig 81), closed at once,
and ran Richmond through for 208 runs. Now that
nearly half the season is over it looks as though
the issue lies between ’Varsity, North and St. Kilda,
with ’Roy and ’Wood hanging on to an outside
chance. If Noses were betting on the game, it
would probably be “Even money, bar one,” the one
being the Students. They have a strong team.
The Yankee Athletic Team will strike Mel-
bourne on January 31, and continue to do so on
February 7 ; but to manage it the visit to Tassy has
to be cut out. Which is just as well for Australia’s
athletic reputation. On February 14 and 21 the team
will be in Sydney; and there are fond hopes that a
few of them may find unhallowed graves for their
reputations there.
Templeton, one of the most boomed of the
visiting United States athletes, met his match at
Christchurch (M.L.) on Saturday. In the 120yds.
hurdles, Templeton and Keddle, a Maorilander, rose
at each hurdle together; but in the final run to the
tape Keddle won. In the final, however, Keddle was
beaten by Pratt, a local man with 15yds. start, in
15 2-ssee. Keddle had taken 16sec. when he beat
Templeton. The rest of the events were mostly won
by the visitors.
Sydney public is evidently not the least
interested in amateur athletics, as the Association
found to its sorrow on Saturday, when the vast
empty spaces of the Sydney Sports Ground greeted
the splendid programme arranged by the Council.
It was the second day of the fourth annual contest
for the Dunn Shield—and nobody except the competi-
tors and a few friends and ticket-holders cared who
won the bauble. In the Three-Miles Walk, Policeman
Pickard, closely pursued by Dent (South Sydney), got
over the distance in 22min 9 4-ssec. That is a record
for N. S. Wales. Walking is one of the things we
do not seem to excel in. In Britain J. W. Raby,
as far back as 1883, shinned over three miles in
20min. 21#sec. Coming down to modern times, G. E.
Lamer, urged on, no doubt, by the necessity of try-
ing to keep warm in the English climate, paced the
same distance in 20min. 25 4-ssec. A youth to
achieve glory on Saturday was S. R. Greenup, who
won his heat and finished third in the final of the
Dunn half-mile, and then came out and won the
second heat and final of the A. A. A. Handicap One
Mile.
Leichhardt and Waverley Red Sox put up
a strenuous game of baseball on Saturday, and made
a very attractive exhibition. The players have struck
form now and are showing very finished play. The
unbeaten Leichhardt team had a horribly close call,
and the scores were actually five all. So an extra
innings was ordered, and Leichhardt managed to get
one and prevent the other fellows scoring at all, thus
winning with six to five. The Blue Sox quite over-
whelmed Petersham by nine to five.
Albert Barry, of the Sydney Swimming
Club, who holds the Australian record for 100 yards,
on Saturday made an attempt upon the 100 metres.
The world’s record for this distance (109.363 yards)
is lmin. 2 3-ssec., put up by Duke Kohana-
moku at Stockholm, 1912. Barry’s time
was lmin. 4 4-ssec. ; so evidently the young Austra-
lian is not yet a match for the Hawaiian. Barry’s
was, nevertheless, hailed as an Australian record,
though, according to Miller’s Sporting Pamphlet,
Harold Hardwick got through the distance in lmin.
3sec. at Sydney Domain Baths in October, 1909.
“M.P.A.”: Mrs. Isabel Newman, de-
ceased, the alleged baby slayer of Mordialloc (Vic.),
blossomed out in 1908 as Madame Isa Bell, champion
lady runner of Australia—a self-inflicted title.
“Madame” claimed at that period to have won at
least 100 races at various picnics held down the
bay. In her “champion” capacity she succeeded in
adroitly pulling the leg of Melbourne Herald. She
was living at Port Melbourne, where her hubby toiled,
and it was at the Port that the writer and a
Bulletin woman-contributor inspected various
brummy clocks and trinkets that “Madame” claimed
to have gained on the cinder path. The tawdry
baubles had evidently been “readied up,” and with
an eye for show the artless deceiver had hubby and
the family dolled up and assembled at the little front
gate to give us greeting and also to impress the
neighborhood. I gave “Madame” a few tips, and at
my suggestion she ordered a pair of sprinting shoes.
She talked “even-time,” and garnishing her vocabu-
lary with all the pet phrases of the game, she
succeeded in putting up a big bluff. She next made the
business acquaintance of a Hebrew firm, which sup-
plied the quids to run a meeting at South Melbourne
in April, ’OB. There she raced Ivy Evans,
a big, hefty lady, who simply put it all
over her in a three-distance match, 50, 100
and 150 yards. I started the contestants on
their wild career. I made “holes” for them, and
they went down, though the way they “set” gave ’em
no advantage over an upright start. When
Madame got licked very badly, she informed me that
an electric bath had “tied her up” in the legs, and
she didn’t show her true form. However, Ivy Evans
very effectively settled “Madame’s” bogus claim, and
afterwards proved herself a very good runner for a
woman. Mrs. Newman gave her height as sft. 2in.
and weight as Bst. 51b. She was quite sturdy for her
inches, and wasn’t built on accepted running lines.
* * « *
Saturday was a great yachting afternoon
in Melbourne, no less than four clubs being out. The
Royals held a cruiser match over 14 miles, and some
interesting racing resulted in a win for Helen by two
seconds from Utiekah, with Thistle third. The wind
was light, but the water was jumpy, and the boats
made a fine show on the run home under spinnakers
and every other scrap of canvas they could crowd on
to catch the fading breeze. Helen, the winner, was
the scratch boat.
* * * *
On Saturday the Prince Alfred Club (Syd-
ney) ran off a double event, with no less than seven
starters in the bigger of them. The breeze was not
conducive to spectacular seamanship, and the job of
the afternoon was to gatch all the wind. Moreover, the
races were badly interfered with by steamer traffic,
which robbed them of still more interest. As it was,
Aoma beat Rawhiti. with Sunbeam third and Culwulla
fourth. The big Sayonara was last. In the 18-Foot
Championship tussle Christian Webb, in the Australian,
simply bolted, and left the rest of the fleet struggling
far in the rear. This win brings Australian’s score
to 10 points against the two each of H. C. Press and
Nimrod, and the one each of Kismet and Arline. As
a picture, Port Jackson was superb, with no less than
14 yachts racing and a cloud of 18-footers darting
about like seagulls.
At Cessnock (N.S.W.) one Hogan is
credited with a ball-punching world’s record of 41hr.
smin. Heaven only knows why Cessnock encouraged
him to do it, seeing that it has millions of tons of
coal that he could have punched far more profitably.
* * * *
Sydney indulged in an orgy of fight last
Saturday night. Battles were raging at Rushcutter’s
Bay and Newtown, and on the boards of all the
newspaper offices Holman and Wade were fighting
their second-last round, and when it was over it was
discovered that Holman had piled up such a crowd
of points that he must win, no matter how the last
round in the country goes. Out at the new Olympia
Stadium Dave Smith met Les O’Donnell for the third
time. True, he won; but it was only on points,
after a full 20-round contest. On the previous occa-
sions he scored one knock-out, and in the other fight
O’Donnell was so badly mauled that the police.stopped
the riot because they considered he had received
enough. So, apparently. Smith is a waning star, or
the other fellow is waxing rapidly. In either case
it does not speak too well for Smith’s chances against
McGoorty. Of course, it doesn’t follow that, because
he gets less effective against O’Donnell each time,
Smith will be ineffective against McGoorty. But it
inclines the public to believe so, and decreases Smith’s
power to draw the money for which they all scrap.
The United States Army is convinced that
it has got the real White Hope in the person of
William O. Vaillancourt, of the Second Division, at
present encamped in Texas City. He is the army
giant, standing 6ft. 94in. in his shoes, weighing 20st.
41b., with a reach of 87# inches. His strength is said
to be abnormal. On one occasion he was seen carry-
ing a kicking mule in his arms to the picket line.
The beast had “jibbed,” and “Tiny,” as Vaillancourt
is called, reckoned it was easier to carry the brute
where he was wanted than to persuade him to go
there. On another occasion it was desired to move
a mess-house. Nine “huskies” took one side and
“Tiny” the other. In spite of his vast bulk and
enormous weight he is said to be light and active on
his feet and ar. excellent skipper. But the chances
are he will never be a success in the ring. He has
never boxed, and has absolutely no idea of the game;
and boxers are like singers—they require to be caught
young.
* * * *
The latest news of Johnson, via the U.S.A.,
is that he waddles when he walks and is altogether
degenerate, and quite unfit to fight any of the top-
notchers. But Yankee reports of the big black have
to be taken cautiously. They detest Johnson there,
and when they detest anyone in the States they
advertise him in their own particular way. The yarns
of Johnson decadent do not tally with the cabled
news that lie has taken on wrestling—a much harder
and more dangerous game than boxing.
The disappearance of Saint Summers
about a week before the night fixed for his fight
with Pal Brown placed promoter Baker in an awk-
ward fix; but he clawed out of it with the help of
boxer Harry Stone, who stepped into the vacant
place. The result was one of the most dazzling
fights ever seen in the old ring. Pal Brown fought
better than he did against McCoy, and was much
faster, but he was not fast enough for the elusive
Stone. All the same, he gave that Mephistophelean
youth the hardest fight he has had in Australia.
At one time there were grave doubts if Stone could
endure 20 rounds of the heavy body-battering Brown
was serving out; for, in spite of Stone’s marvellous
footwork, his splendid ducking, and ' the stabbing
left that he kept poking on to Brown’s left eye,
Brown drove him on to the ropes and there battered
him badly again and again. But his condition was
splendid, and he survived. Stone is essentially a
brainy fighter, and the way he anticipated Brown's
blows was very wonderful. But he could not antici-
pate them all, nor block them all when he did antici-
pate them. He had carefully thought out his plan
of battle beforehand, and carried it out excellently.
Prancing around, soaring through the air, dancing
from right to left, and stab, stab, stab, stabbing all
the time at Brown’s left eye with his left glove, he
was on the defensive at the start of every round.
Then, with 10 seconds to go, his corner would give
a signal, and he would come with a terrific rally,
the like of which has never before been seen main-
tained round after round at the Stadium. It hap-
pened with clock-like regularity. And in every case
Brown either bolted from the fury of it, or hung
on. At these rallies Stone was a picture for
an artist. His face glowered with cynical ferocity,
and he hit like lightning. His blows came so fast
it was impossible to follow them, and neither could
Brown block them —he was simply smothered, over-
whelmed. The crowd rocked with delight. It was a
gruelling fight. Brown bored in all the time, and
in almost every round seemed to have Stone in
difficulties; but the latter always clawed or ducked
out, and then came with his wonderful 10 seconds’
rally. Brown made a splendid effort, but he failed,
and" the decision in Stone’s favor was absolutely cor-
rect. There is probably no cleverer boxer in Aus-
tralia at the present moment. And such is the
vitality of the youth that half an hour after receiv-
ing the verdict he was on the vaudeville stage in the
city, doing a turn.
The vanished Summers was ultimately located at
Katoomba (N.S.W.). He explained that he could
not make the lOst. 21b. demanded by the articles,
without unduly weakening himself, and was disin-
clined to fight again under welter-weight (lOst. 71b.).
To fight Brown at lOst. 21b., and then take on
McCormick a fortnight later, at lOst. 71b., was not, he
said, giving himself a fair show —so'he faded out.
Why he didn’t walk into the Stadium office and ex-
plain things like a business man, Johnny did not
mention. Perhaps he thought there was more advt.
in the disappearing trick ; if so, he can rest assured
it was a mighty bad advt., because it carried with
it a suggestion that he was scared of Brown.
The Oarpentier-Wells assault —it ran
hardly be called a fight—is likely to hold one record.
An English firm thought it had done good business in
securing the picture rights for £2000; and this,
accepting the time as cabled (lmin. 10sec.), works
out at the handy figure of £2B 10s. per second!
“0.K.” : Another caustic comment on the
sincerity of 20-round glove “fights” is the result of
the Carpentier-Wells argument in London last week.
There was £2500 to be scooped by the winner, and
the Frenchman scooped it right away. The fight lasted
a few seconds. Previously, when the same pair met at
Ghent, the Frenchman won in the fourth round, after
having narrowly escaped a count-out in the first. In
a genuine contest between heavyweights a man must
win at once or be beaten almost immediately. A
real fight is a very brief excitement for the onlookers
—no prolonged display of the arts of attack and
defence; no chance of an unpopular decision by the
ref.! Going, going, gone!
Some bowelless person is proposing to
arrange a fight between Bill Lang and Sam McVea;
and Billiam is said to be delighted at the prospect.
Bill’s game, no doubt, but such a fight would be most
detestable butchery, and should not be allowed—
though who is to stop it writer does not know. Aus-
tralian audiences are not partial to colored boxers
unless they are aboriginals ; and then they love ’em.
Jerry Jerome will draw as big a house any night he
likes to fight as anyone, but the manager who puts
up a negro boxer just at present would probably lose
what the boxers call his dough.
* * * *
Just before commencing his battle with
Pal Brown on Saturday night, Harry Stone reaped
the first reward of his victory over Mat Wells. It
came in the shape of a cabled offer of £l2OO to
fight Willie Ritchie, lightweight champion of the
world, at Madison Square, New York, under the
promotion of one Billy Gibson. Baker at once
offered to release Stone from his contract if he
wished to accept, but Stone said he would think it
over and make inquiries. Wise youth.
Harry Levien, recently defeated at Tam-
worth (N.S.W.), after just a third of a
century’s political service, was the baldest
man in the Assembly. An early tip as to
the Tamworth contest of 1913 was pub-
lished some time ago. It appeared in 11.
Kings 2, 23: “Go up, thou baldbead!"
And he went up. In his younger political
days he was the solicitor for the defence in
the big conspiracy trial which sent up Sleath.
Ferguson and other N. S. Wales strike-
leaders. The courthouse at Deniliquin
then buzzed with politicians and men who
were shortly to become politicians. With
Levien’s defeat the last of them has gone.
Of the notables, Jack Want, Levien’s senior
counsel, is dead ; Sleath tragically buried
himself politically; and Ferguson changed
his coat and smothered himself in the pro-
cess.

Advt. from a Sydney morning paper last
week: —
Indian farmer, 38, wishes to correspond with lady,
about 30, view matrimony. This advt. is genuine.
Address, ——, Lismore.
Even the newspapers which say they cor-
dially approve of a white Australia would
evidently rather have a spotted Australia
than lose a shilling. In the circumstances
there is nothing for it but to pass a law
absolutely prohibiting the marriage of any
colored person to a white citizen, and mak-
ing it a criminal offence for any person,
newspaper proprietor or otherwise, to aid or
abet any colored person in securing a white
woman either as “housekeeper” or “with
view to Matrimony.”
In Maoriland Premier Massey has an-
nounced that an amending Bill will reduce
the percentage of recorded votes required to
go the whole prohibition hog from 60 to 55.
The first poll taken on national prohibition
was in 1911, when 55.83 per cent, of the re-
corded votes were in favor and 44.17 per-
cent against. But on that occasion to he
successful the “d.rys” required 60 per cent,
of the votes. Four years’ trading after the
decision in favor of prohibition is allowed
Bung and Co. as compensation, so, if Maori-
land is in the same mind at the end of 1914
as it was at the conclusion of 1911, there’ll
be some cyclonic hustling at the pubs and
breweries up till 1918.

“McNorth,” from Far Tip:—
A great deal of the diversity of opinion re the
suitableness of the Northern Territory for white set-
tlement and agriculture is caused by the opiners
visiting the country at different seasons of the year.
One man who sold his happy home in Victoria, and
migrated to the Territory, has returned and de-
scribed, in a Queensland paper, the waterless and
barren condition of the place. But if that man,
instead of going there in September (one of the dry
months),, had gone in (say) February, he would
probabh' have sent his friends a glowing report of
the rich pastures and fertile soil he had discovered.
The man in the South is apt to forget, or else he doesn’t
know, that the seasonal conditions of Darwin and
Gippsland are not the same. The rain in the North
falls in the summer, and the coastal register is
about 60in. Also, it has never been known to fail.
Certainly, there is very little rain between the “wet
seasons”; but the cocky can be sure that it will
arrive at the appointed time, which is more than can
be said for many places in the South. Then, again,
many people seem to suffer from the idea that any
product of the South can be produced anywhere in
the Territory, and when they find that wheat, and
barley, and macaroni don’t thrive near Darwin, they
rush out wild statements to the effect that the only
successful products of the Territory are Binghis,
buffaloes and Government officials. It is certainly
doubtful if mixed farming, as it is known in Vic-
toria, can be practised in the Territory; but there
is no doubt at all that the tropical plants which
are so successful in Queensland can be grown at a
profit. It’s a good country with a large, healthy
climate, and if we persist in building the railroad
from the wrong end, and don’t get a hustle on in
the matter of settlement, there are hordes of people
just over the way who would deem it a genuine
pleasure to give us a hand.
♦
From an ex-Cohentingenter
Many of us who risked our skins for glory and a
dollar a day in order that the Jews of .Jo’burg
might try Chinese labor as a substitute for nigger
slavery are feeling sick nowadays. South Africa, to
acquire complete control of which Britain wasted
thousands of lives and millions of money, is fast
finding its way back into the hands of the men
from whom only recently it was temporarily wrested.
The result of the Nationalist Conference, just ended,
shows most unmistakably that the Dutch Afrikander,
at any rate, is done with Botha and his temporising,
semi-imperial policy. The glamor of the successful
soldier is fast wearing away ; and the great body of
the Dutch people, always inclined to look askance
at Botha’s British sympathies and leanings, is be-
ginning to prospect for someone else to lead the
way into the Canaan of “Africa for the Afrikan-
ders.” Steyn, Herzog and De Wet —the three
“Free Staters” who were instrumental in wresting
the most favorable terms of peace on record from
their alleged conquerors —have now assumed control
of the Afrikander Nationalist party. The cable tells
us that “a split” has occurred; but. as the Free
State (or, as it is called now, Orangia) follows Steyn
and Herzog as one rhan, and as the Southern Trans-
vaal and the Northern districts of the colony will
most assuredly do the same, it looks as if Botha
will be left with a mere remnant. Natal and the
Eastern Colony are largely British in race and sen-
timent, and he can look for little sympathy from
them. The influence of the extremist section of the
Nationalists under the outward leadership of Herzog,
but with the acute brain of Steyn (most subtle of
South African statesmen and most irreconcilable of
anti-Britishers) behind it all, has been increasingly
evident for some time past. It is shown everywhere,
as in the wholesale evictions of the Outlanders
from the civil services and in the re-instatement
of that mongrel tongue, the Taal, as the primirv
language in the schools and other institutions
Mottling is surer than that the next general elections
will see a great accession to the ranks of the mil
itant Dutch party. The women of S’Africa, esneci
ally the women of the country districts, did not
through ignorance and prejudice, utilise the ballot
box to the extent expected at the last elections
The Afrikander Bond, llet Volk and the “Orange
Unie” have, however, attended to that matter. Aided
by the Predikants, or clergymen of the Dutch R e
formed Church, who are more extreme than the ex
tremists, the dyed-in-the-wool Afrikander vote pro
mises to overwhelm what little remains of the Ini
perial spirit in the greater part of the South African
Union. With Steyn to guide and Herzog to accom-
plish, and such things as the Hindu restriction
trouble as a text to work on, the future of South
African Imperialism threatens to be quite interest-
ing.
♦
“Ricardo” : Willy Watt and his Govern-
ment said that Australia is to be peopled
with rural folk from England ; and the other
State Governments chimed in with “Hear,
bear.” But Emmott, Haggard, and others
of the English Parliamentary Commission
declared emphatically that Australia will
not get the English yeomen and their fami-
lies because England is resolved to keep
them. Certainly Lloyd George is taking
decisive measures to nail the English down
to their own soil, and the Tories profess
to be equally desirous on the point. Marl-
borough has just announced that 1000
acres of Blenheim Park will be farmed for
the first time. A large slice this, for the
whole park is only 2500 acres; yet the rest
will go, too, like Australian squattages be-
fore the selector. The ducal Sutherland,
scenting trouble perhaps, offers Lloyd
George 200.000 acres of deer forest at £2
an acre, and the Government is likely to
accept, just to show that it means business.
These may he swallows to herald the sum-
mer that is coming for the small English
agriculturist and laborer. Yet it bv no
means follows that the summer will" hold
the yeoman who tastes it. He may want a
better summer. The man who is turned
into a little farmer in England may have
his hopes fired to be a big farmer in Aus-
tralia. Nothing that Lloyd George can do
in the way of nailing-down is likely to be
more thorough than that which the Germans
have done ; yet note the flow of Germans to
America and other foreign lands. So “In-
crease your yeoman population!” I say to
England, and we’ll get more English yeomen
than we do to-day—when, in truth, we get
none worth mentioning. For us are the
pale tweed-caps of Lunnon.
“T.L.Y.”: How’s this for the staying
power of the native born? One of the Vic-
torian participants in the baby bonus this
year is a young man who was born in Ho-
bart in 1835. He landed in Vic. in 1852
and is still driving a team of bullocks for a
crust.
