The Inky Way.
“S.T.S.”: Why is punctuation suddenly—within
the past seven years—become a lost art ? The
newspapers are awful. The London-printed novels
are worse. It is the comma that suffers, chiefly.
“This course may in some circumstances be recom-
mended.” That is all right without a comma; or
it can have two, one after “may,” and one after
“circumstances,” with the effect of a parenthesis.
What the printers do is to stick in one, which is
worse than useless. Sometimes the commas seem
to be dropped in out of a pepper-pot. Some gems
from a book just printed in Sydney, and very well
got up in all other respects: “Master Anthony
Bacon, came from beyond the seas, being a gentle-
man whose ability, the world taketh knowledge of” ;
“My course hath been pure and unlearned and never
poor gentleman as I am persuaded, had a deeper
desire—” ; and “Many of our preconceived opinions
of Queen Elizabeth, derived from an unreliable his-
tory will have to undergo, a considerable change.”
All the punctuation there is to learn could be taught
within 10 minutes, one supposes. Isn't it any
literary man’s business to learn it —or to teach?
It is nonsense to blame the linotype. Is the lino-
typer a dummy that he can’t be taught too ?
* * ♦ *
The new evening paper which is to bring the total
of dailies in the peaceful town of Christchurch,
M.L. (population 63,116), to five is getting together
a strong staff under Editor Huie. The latest ap-
pointment is that of Charles Harris as assistant
editor. Marris is one of big-chief Gresley Lukin’s
bright youhg men on the Wellington Post, and
although he has been only five years in journalism he
has crowded a lot of clever work, including some
Bulletin verse, into the time.
“The Oldest Inhabitant”: A quaint, yellow, time-
colored publication is the Wyndham Journal, first
published in manuscript, after the manner of John
Pascoe Fawkner’s Melbourne Advertiser, on board
the ship Wyndham in 1557. The series of nine
numbers and a supplement were later on sent to
Edinburgh to be printed. The ship, of 760 tons,
left Liverpool in June, 1867, chartered by, Gibbs
Bright and Co., and consigned to Messrs. Bright
Brothers and Co., of Plinders-laue and Bond-street,
Melbourne. And, by the way, the Bright Brothers,
Charles E. Bright (who married a daughter of Lord
Canterbury) andTieggy Bright, are still on top. Charles
is 85 and Reggy 80.) The surgeon, Alvara Loft-
house Slater, was a scientist, and had travelled, in
China. On settling in Victoria he devoted his time
to researches in snakebite, and published some in-
teresting data in connection therewith. Andrew
Orr, who died in Ballarat at the age of 87 years,
was on the ship, and was given five years to live.
He lived more than 60, and distinguished himself
as poet and pressman; he left a volume of poems
published a short time before his death. John
Forde, who had been on the staff of several Dublin
newspapers, amongst them the Warder and the
Nation, widely different in politics, was also a
passenger. Mr. Forde has two sons on the Inky
Way in Australia. Lorimer Fison, not then a
reverend doctor, was also on the little ship. The
editor was Joseph .Wilding, who established himself
in Melbourne as an importer of agricultural machin-
ery. Slater, Orr, Fison and Forde contributed
largely to the journal, and there were many anony-
mous contributions of much merit. Some little timeafter
landing, Fison becamemanager of Abbott and Co.’s print-
ing office in Post Office Place (Melb.) while Abbott
was in England. During liis management a pamph-
let was printed at the offide, entirely without Fison’s
knowledge, which disclosed the peculiar doings of
the Wroeite sect at their chapel in Fitzroy. The
police got a copy of the pamphlet, supplied without
hesitation by Mr. Fison. The authorities pronounced
it obscene. Superintendent Hare promised that if
the stock were destroyed there would be no prosecu-
tion, but the promise -was not kept. Fison was pro-
secuted before Chief Justice Stawell and a jury.
He '.defended himself; he was a Caius College (Cam-
bridge) man, and knew how to do it. He was found
guilty and ordered to come up for sentence when
called upon; but he never was called upon. Stawell
complimented him on the “erudition, learning
and research” which his speech to the jury disclosed.
On Abbott’s return Fison joined the kjethodists, and
went to Fiji as a missionary. It was an opportunity
to commence a study for which he always l craved—
the study of ethnology. In this connection the
British Government gave him a Civil List pension
of £l5O a year; but it came too late —a few
months before his death in December, ,1907. He has
left us “Tales of Old Fiji’ and other works, mainly of
a scientific - character. *
“X.P.D.”: In Ipswich, which hasn’t yet finished
grieving over the circumstance that it wasn’t made
the capital of Queensland instead of upstart Brisbane,
people have a way of sticking to their jobs. Cribb
and Foote, the big storekeepers of the place, have a
crop of old men who started there as youngsters. At
least one has been there 50 years. But the daddy of
ail long-service establishments is the local news-
paper, the - Queensland Times. Two of the paper’s
four directors, William Kipping and A. J. Stephen-
son, started in the office as printer’s devils or
message boys well over half 'a century ago. One of
the editors has been on the job nearly as long, and
the other for close-on 30 years. I forgot to mention
that the rest of the directorate, consisting of the
Parkinson brothers, were born in the business, their
father being a former owner or part-owner of the
paper.
Gone up for his final proof-reading, Edmund
Gregory, formerly Government Printer in Queensland,
aged 81. He was a fair boss, and one who knew his
job The patriarchal printer worked successively on
ithe Empire (under Henry Parkes), the S. M. Herald
and the Brisbane Courier. Then he got a billet as l
overseer under Government Printer Beal, whom he'
succeeded in ’93. He retired in 1902.
* * * *
“Diorite”: Westralian journalists have an un-
friendly eye for. Parliamentarians who undertake
newspaper work in order to advertise themselves and
supplement their incomes. The grievance is not that
they do the work, but that they do it at what would
be starvation rates if they made a living with their
pen. And, the most deplorable part of the business
is that the greatest offenders are Laborites. Three
years ago an M.H.R. drawing £6OO a year edited a
sheet without pay; and at present two Labor mem-
bers run other papers at wages which no self-respect-
ing pressman could afford to seriously consider. The
excuse usually is they are doing something for “the
cause,” though I don’t hear of Labor members who
were bricklayers being encouraged to build a Trades
Hall for 3d. an hour in their spare time.
A libel case out of the common was that of Flor-
ence Webbe v. Melb. Age. The Webbes were married
on January 8, 1912, and on March 24, 1913, an
advt. appeared in the Age that their son, aged 11
months, had died on March 20. This was over the odds,
and the lady clamored for something more than an
apology. In defence, the Age proved that the advt.
had been inserted in good faith and without malice,
and that little or no damage had been done to Mrs.
Webbe’s character, as her friends and acquaintances
who saw the misleading notice, knew it to be in-
correct. On these grounds the newspaper might have
escaped with, at worst, nominal damages; but its
counsel put up the further defence that Mr. Webbe,
with Mrs. Webbe’s consent, sent, or . caused to be
sent, the notice to the paper. The insinuation was
that the pair had laid a trap for the Age, and that
the respectable journal had innocently fallen into it.
Judge Box, in his summing-up, declared that this
defence absolutely failed, and by reason thereof plain-
tiff was entitled to recover. A sympathetic jury
thereupon came to the assistance of his Honor as to
the amount to be recovered. They fixed it at £4OO.
Digby Denham threatened the Warwick Argus
with trouble after the Brisbane Daily Mail and
Senator Ferricks had been put into the pot; but he
let it off with costs and the usual serving of humble-
pie.

’Murikan chemist Roberts, who has been
experimenting with prickly pear, claims that
the ash of the burnt leaf is worth £1 per
cwt. in America, and that heavily-infested
land will yie-ld 15cwt. of ash per acre. These
and other of Roberts’s figures are tall; still
he has secured the backing of a Victorian
syndicate, which includes hard-headed
Rupert Clarke and squatter Falkiner, so
there is apparently something in his claims.
The fact that Ex-Premier “Bob” Philp has
announced his impending retirement from the
chairmanship of the Q.M.E. Co., of Ross
River and Pinkenba, gives some color to tne
rumor that this co. may follow the lead ot
the N.Q.M.E. Co. and sell out to the rela-
tives of Swift and Co. To speed the parting
chairman, the Q.M.E. shareholders have had
his portrait in oils executed by Guthrie, a
Scottish artist of note.

IN BANANA LAND
Archbishop Dunne (R.C.), of Brisbane,
celebrated the “Golden Jubilee” of his minis-
try on Wednesday, December 10. He is 80
years of age, and half a century ago came from
Ireland to Queensland. He was consecrated
Bishop in 1882, as successor to Bishop
O’Quinn, who was the first man to carry a
shepherd’s crook in these parts.
When George Reid landed at the Bris-
bane Central railway station there was no
fuss. The only people who looked excited
were the police. One of them ordered the
visitor’s son to move out of the road, and an-
other gave similar instructions to the railway
porter who was in charge of George’s lug-
gage.
“Pitman”: George Reid’s best speech in
Bananaland was thrown at the Journalists
at Brisbane, who fed him at lunch. The
menu cards were splashed all over with cor-
pulence, eyeglass and dog. This inspired
the guest to tell the story of the pup ail
over again. To The Bulletin’s dry-dog
and other artists he really owed very much,
he said —he never knew quite how much till
he once dived into the backblocks of West-
ralia and found that even there the boys,
large and small, knew him quite as well as
his own next-door neighbors. Coming back
to the bird in hand, George took it as a
compliment that rival papers had sent their
staffs in strong force to« meet him. But the
reports of the speech in their next issues
revealed a hoof-mark or two. The fact that
the editor of one old-established daily did not
put in an appearance whs not noticed so
much as that that paper’s report omitted to
mention that the editor of the other old-
established daily presided. It was really
very characteristic of the pettiness of Bris-
bane journalism.
The way the Swifts are doing things at
their new meat works on the Brisbane River
astonishes the natives. It is a whole-hog
concern. Though the work has been in pro-
gress for about a year, the substantial char-
acter of the job is shown by the fact that it
will take another year to finish. The massive
buildings occupy three or four acres of land
along the foreshores, and are flanked by lines
of wharves. The main structures run up
eight storeys. An eminence at the back* the
face of which has been chiselled off to let in
part of the works, gives easy access to the
higher floors, so that the stock can be fed
in to the top of the premises as they arrive by
train. The buildings are mostly of steel and
reinforced concrete, and the works Avill be
sufficiently advanced to treat stock —-sheep
chiefly—m nine months. When the visitor
asks if that is the Beef Trust’s works he is
offered a horrified glare in reply. They call
it the Australian Export Meat Company.
The other day the Brisbane Institute of
Architects went down in a body to lehrn a
thing or two about reinforced concrete. The
thermometer was bubbling at the century.
After sweltering around for an hour or two,
climbing stairs and swallowing dust and
cement, their pilot omitted an important
item. It was a study in energy to see the
way the party rushed to the refreshment
room at Hamilton on crossing the river.
They came to the conclusion that the Beef
Trust has no funds for irrigation..
A Brisbane daily advised that High Con-
ditioner Reid be asked to give a “humorous
entertainment in the Exhibition Hall” for
the benefit of the hospital. Reid’s self-in-
flicted reputation for buffoonery will stick
to him till his dying day—a dreadful, damn-
able asset.'
At the recent Islington show —the biggest
thing in England—-Queensland won more
prizes with its butter than any other State.
It scored 13; N. S. Wales’s little lot was 11,
Victoria’s <>. Maorijand’s 4 and S. Aus-
tralia’s 1. But while the experts had high
praise for Bananaland’s butter, they faulted
its bacon, the butchering of it principally.
Its cheese also fared badly, partly because
it had been stored with the butter in the
refrigerator. One Warwick cheese, in fact,
burst on the stand on the day of King
George’s visit, and might have stained the
royal vest but for a merciful dispensation
of Providence.
NO HURRY
NURSE (leading patient to the operating theatre): " They re waiting—hurry up!"
PATIENT : "W hat for! I've think I'm anxious to get a front seat!"
Warwick is still riven by a big industrial
dispute, although the daily press calls it by
another name. The Friendly Societies im-
ported a non-union doctor from Maoriland
(at £lOOO per annum), and duly installed
him in defiance of B.M.A, rules. This non-
unionist now demands the right to attend
patients in the general hospital, and the
five union doctors on the staff day they’ll
strike if he is allowed on the works. There
the matter stands for the present.
The City Council is the talk of the hour
in Brisbane. Even Propputy, whose creature
it is, has nothing but disgust for its latest
achievement. This is what is known as the
ferry scandal. Not long ago the Council set
out to build two steamers —one a horse punt,
the other a passenger boat. The former
has proved such a useless affair, due to
faulty design, that an expert from Sydney
has recommended that the engines be talmn
out and the hull sold for a hulk. The pas-
senger boat is not so bad, but many sugges-
tions for improvement are made regarding
that too. All this will involve an
expenditure running into about £5OOO.
Meantime the service is losing the rate-
payers about £3OOO a year. Socialism is the
Council’s nightmare; and if it judges Social-
ism by the sort of service which it gives to
the public itself, then it has every reason to
regard the thing as a nightmare. But an
ass has no right to say that sculpture is a
fraud just because it can’t kick out much of
a statue itself.
Doctrine.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Last-night the moon was like a threat.
It frowned across the world ;
The ship of clouds along the sky
Her sunny sails had furled;
The rivlr wept among the reeds
Like some pale widow in her weeds,
And through dark pools of mystery
The white mists upward curled.
Across the moon the black boughs scrawled
Strange words I might not read;
The very skies were leaning down
To puzzle o’er the screed ;
The wind went shuddering through the smoke
That from the factory chimneys broke ;
The quivering furnace by the town
With red tongue seemed to plead....
Last night the moon was great and round ;
It seemed to me the eye
Of some far, unforgiving God
Who wills that Man shall die;
And plans for him a narrow path
Down which to flee from Heaven’s wrath,
And, lest the way be lightly trod.
Paves it with agony !
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, December 13, 1913.
My Dear Thargomindah.—
When all else fails an ornamental Gov-
ernor can keep his job sweet by flitting from
school to school, patting the over-crammed
scholar on the examination marks, handing
over prizes and beaming on teachers and
proud mammas w-here they preen themselves.
The Macgregor and his missus have been
finding this out during the week at various
prize-givings. Ipswich Grammar School
cluttered a welcome on Thursday, All Hal-
lows’ Convent dittoed the same night, then
the State schools in turn.
On Monday evening president Coakes and
the Brisbane Club outdid all bidders and se-
cured Eye Commissioner Reid as guest at a
square feed. This was the only invitation
of a private nature that George accepted
(perhaps the others were only to light re-
freshments), and the Brisbane Club-ites now
walk with the conscious swagger of props of
Empire. “Bob” Philp proposed the 1 plump
man’s health, and George did the same kindly"
office for Digby 11., who leaves this week
for the Pawnshop Country.
Mrs. Denham and some four tons of lug-
gage left by the Orsova on Wednesday.
Digby and the others of the party will board
the same craft in Melbourne. The Pre-
mieress was not accorded any formal fare-
wells, but some of the charities she has
worked for lifted up an appreciative voice,
and through the secretaries murmured
“Good-bye.”
On Tuesday night president Coakes and
his attractive wife fed the members of the
Brisbane Club, their wives and best girls on
board the Emerald. This trusty vessel, bear-
ing some 300 appetites, put out from port
skilfully disguised in much bunting, several
rods of greenery, and a few hundred electric
lights. She looked quite skittish for a barge
of her years. Billy Crisp and Fred Wat*,*
made melodious noises on the main floor
while Bob Kaye thumped the accom Z'
ments in the teeth of the piano \p
Iyelleher’s band was responsible for the iW
ot the music*. I don’t know who was respon
sible for the supper, or I’d send you the
address in large type.
Mrs. Arthur Feez and daughter Mve
companied by handsome Marjorie Green
have departed to Coolangatta, 'whence thev
will make their way to Svdney late
they will take vessel to Maoriland, and re’
turn Marjorie to her family. Feez
also is visiting the South.
A bust of the founder of the Order of the
Sisters of Mercy was unveiled at the Mater
Misericordi* Hospital yesterday. It was
erected by Dr. Lilian Cooper and the other
members of the hon. medical staff, in memory
of the late Sisteh Mary Antonio and Sister
Mary Felix, two dear women who were uni-
versally beloved. Archbishop Duhig did the
unveiling, and I)rs. Taylor. Hopkins, Hirsch-
feld and Cooper expressed general regrets
What money is over when the memorial is
paid for goes towards an X-ray fund for thn
hospital.
On Tuesday afternoon Miss Large, the
popular headmistress of the Girls’ Central
State School, was handed a necklet of aqua-
marines as a sort of farewell. The lady-
lea ves next week for Sydney, where she wiil
join a I’, and O. boat for England.
To-night the Irish Association is indus-
triously farewelling P. J. McDermott, who
is a valuable member. He.is to stand at the
right hand of Digby Denham while the hat
is being sent round in England.
On Monday afternoon the annual display
of work by past and present pupils was made
at All Hallows. While the visitors wan-
dered about examining the paintings and
needlework, the school orchestra played good
music. ( I know it was music, because it
wasn’t ragtime.) Afterwards a band of
nice girls in white and blue handed round
tea and other edibles. The convent is noted
for the number of good musicians who have
first discovered and developed their musical
abilities under its roof, so it was to be ex-
pected that the annual concert on Thursday
evening would be largely attended. The
only thing wrong with the expectation was
that it was under-estimated. Even the ver-
andahs creaked.
Mrs. E. M. Irvine, of the Sydney Daily
Telegraph, who returned to this hamlet
last week, has been tea-ed to saturation. To-
day she retaliated on about two dozen of the
enemy before retreating on Toowoomba for a
spell.
Last night at the Dandies, while a large
audience was enjoying itself in the open air,
the Heavens were suddenly uncorked by the
Celestial Barman (presumably Jeremiah,
who on a great occasion attempted to souse
the house of the Rechabites). Just when it
appeared that somebody was in risk of being
drowned, the male Dandies arrived, swathed
in raincoats, and towed all females to the
covered stage.
Our songstress, Mrs. Gilbert Wilson, will
eat her Christmas pudding (if she ever
touches the , unpleasant delicacy her ap-
pearance much belies her) in Melbourne.
Contralto Lena Hammond and her mother
have taken a flat in Sydney, and will be away
for about three months; and pianiste Lucy
Baker will rusticate in the Blackall Ranges.
The only compensation offering for the tem-
porary loss of all this musical talent is Allen
Doone, who has decided to come and warble
to us, and next Saturday will start chanting
his little lays at His Majesty’s Theatre.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.

a rule, many applicants for public lands when they
are thrown open as farms in N. S. Wales ; but the
number is mostly exaggerated, and nobody promotes
or relishes the exaggeration more than the big land-
lord and the country land agent. Their business is to
circulate the idea that thousands of people are clam-
oring for soil, and thus justify their own extortionate
prices. As a matter of fact, when 50 farms are
thrown open and you read that there are 5000 appli-
cants, you can be pretty well sure that 100 men have
applied for every block (or thereabouts). It doesn’t
cost more to apply for 50 blocks than for one

Dr. Breinl, of the Townsville Institute of
Tropical Medicine, believes, like other men
who know, that the Northern Territory is a
tit home for a white population ; but he is
strongly of opinion that it will not be a fit
home if Asiatics are also allowed in. Apart
from the difficulty, amounting almost to
impossibility, of making them obey sanitary
laws, Breinl remarks of the Asiatic nations
that they are permeated with contagious
and infectious diseases, and the flow of dis-
colored people backwards and forwards
would always keep the country supplied
with small-pox, leprosy, plague and other
troubles.
“Ela”: Here’s another instance of the
decadence of the White Man who lives and
works in the tropics. I met him amongst
the mountains that form the watershed of
the Lakekamu River (Papua). I had just
reached the summit of a particularly steep
peak, and was stretched out getting my
wind back, when a voice behind me re-
marked, “Fine day, Mister.” I turned
around and saw a very old but vigorous-
looking man leaning on a sapling and fan-
ning himself with his hat. Responding to his
greeting I suggested a smoke, and the
ancient sat down and commenced to talk. I
gleaned these particulars about him. He
had been mining all over the world —Klon-
dike, Madagascar, Siberia, Maoriland, S’
Africa, Australia and Papua, and had
chased the elusive ’weight for 55 years, com-
ing to Papua for the first field —Sudest
Island —in 1887. He was a native of Den-
mark, and nearly 79 years of age, but was
still well and strong, and was expecting to
have “a bit o’ a spell in Sydney next Cris-
muss.” Talking about things Australian,
he showed himself as patriotic as if he had
been a native of the continent, and he was
l ooking forward to seeing some of the Aus-
tralian warships when he went south. He
planned to recruit a fresh team of “boys”
on his return and go out on a prospecting
trip among the: mountains across the border
in the German portion of the island, for he
thought “there was a bit o’ metal lying out
there waiting for somebody to come along
and pick up,” and he wanted to get “a bit
of stuff” put by against his old age!

For the Country Horseman
The saddle here illustrated is expressly designed
for country use, for stock riding, etc., and is built
of the best materials throughout. If any of our
friends are requiring such a saddle they will find
this a genuine bargain.
It has a hogskin seat, a plated bar-tree, extra
long flaps, with points to buckle below. An economi-
cal feature is the skirt, which extends all round
and thus adds years of wear to the saddle by pro-
tecting its interior from the weather. It has a
deep, “sweepy” seat, 4in. kneepads, nickel-plated
iron stirrups, and double buckle leather girth. The
price complete is £3/15/- Mention of Tub
Bulletin when ordering will ensure your having a
leather surcingle given in free, and the saddle will
be forwarded freight paid. It is to be obtained
from J. P, Talty, 388 Sussex Street, Sydney.

Dairying Commission suggested that the compulsory
testing of dairy cow's should be insisted upon, he was
to:d: “There was no necessity for such, a procedure;
our industry was carried on to-day under very strict
Government supervision and inspection, and the re-
ports of the dairy inspectors were to the effect that
very little tuberculosis existed in the dairy herds of
the State. He did not wonder at this, because the
dairyman of the present day was well educated as to
this disease.” The dairymen may be; but are the
inspectors ? Here’s a case, known to the writer. B,
a dairy inspector, sold a cow to C. C was at first
doubtful about her health ; but upon the inspector
assuring him that the lady was quite well, he took
her. A short time afterwards he sold her to D, a
dairyman in the same district; and upon his next
round the same inspector ordered the lady to be
destroyed. “Bad case of tuberculosis,” was his ver-
dict. He may have been suffering from loss of
memory or the cow may have but lately developed
the trouble. Anyway, D certainly suffered from loss
of money.
“A. 8.”: There is an instrument, article or weapon
which no self-respecting w'harfie is ever without. It
is known as “Riley.” There are different kinds of
“Rileys”-—bamboo, rubber, lead, glass, anything in
the shape of a tube. While the glass has its good
points, it is not popular because of its brittleness.
The rubber “Riley” is the most satisfactory because
of its pliability—it can be “smothered” so quickly
and so snugly. The rubber tubing which is used
on motor-car lamps is admirable, and many a car
lacks the gas tubes after a water trip. The manipu-
lation of “Riley” is simple. After the keg is nicely
stowed away, the high priest gently inserts his hook,
and while a couple of lusty-lunged sons of the
waterside sing to drown the noise, the bung is
knocked in. “Riley” is then brought into action.
Some of the filite are fastidious ami one
for the crowd and one for their private use. I even
struck a silver “Riley” one day in the hold of a coaster.
It came out of a package from Bremen. The package
contained surgical instruments. I was down the No.
4 hatch, of a “black German” one day, when “Riley”
was exceptionaly busy. Presently a “pannikin” boss
came over the hatch and yelled out, “What the
(sanguinary words) are you blones doing down
there ? Is there anybody working at all ?” A bibu-
lous wharfie lurched out into the square of the hatch
and, with a beery grin, answered, “Yes (hie),
‘Riley’ ” !
It is all right to advertise your goods, but there
are better ways of doing it, if you are a jeweller,
than by leaving a lot of gorgeous gauds in a brilli-
antly lighted window after the shop is closed for
the day. A P’ran (Melb.) jeweller found this out
when he returned to his shop at 10 o’clock one
recent evening to shut off the light and place the
brilliants in the safe for the night, someone having
been before him and removed £3OO worth of rings.
The locks and bars were untouched; but in an
18in. brick wall was a hole large enough for any
energetic thief to get through. The visitors left a
clue behind them; but a clue in the hands of a
Melbourne policeman is a poor substitute for £3OO
worth of saleable stock.
“Mr. Oeky”: What is this preference to unionists,
anyway? The attention of the Victorian Cabinet was
lately directed to the dealings of Melbourne paper
merchants with typothetae and non-typothet* master-
printers. The typothetae master-printers are union-
ists who have raised the charges for printing to a
uniform standard, and the paper merchants support
them to the extent of supplying goods at a cheaper
rate to them than to the non-typothetae people, who
are “free”-printers. It is mostly the big printers
who are mixed up in this scheme to get more from
the public. I don’t say that they should not do it,
because the undercutting in the printing trade of
Victoria during recent years was a disgrace and no
good to anybody. What I do say is that the differ-
ence between the preference to unionists shown by
the fat paper merchants, who agree with their Argus
regularly, and the preference to unionists which
makes Joe Cook and his crowd so enraged, is so
small as to be invisible to the naked eye.
O. Neitch, who recently took over an hotel at
Orange (N S.W.), belongs to a family of 27, of whom
19 are still alive. His father, Emanuel Neitch (now
deceased), had a unique record; his first pub license
was issued to him for the Black Dog, on the “Rocks,”
Sydney, as long ago as 1830, and in 1891 he obtained
his 61st. Fifty-seven of them were in respect of a
suburban hotel which is still kept by one of the
family.
“Machete”: The missionary and the Government
official are as great destroyers of vested interests as
the Socialistic Tiger. I once met, in Chad’s Bay, a
famous head-hunting district of Papua, a dignified
and most courteous ex-cannibal upon whom, in spite
of peace and several attractive young wives, a melan-
choly brooded which even gifts of kuku (tobacco)
could not remove. As our acquaintance mellowed—
we were sitting in the smoking-room of the local
Union Club —he confided to me that the country was
going to the Devil. From time immemorial his
family had held the high and honorable office of head-
boilers to the community. All heads collected had to
be prepared and bleached by the Lord High Head-
boiler, and only his hands could prepare it for ex-
hibition as a trophy. The valued perquisites of this
great hereditary office were the brains contained in
the skulls. Since my host had succeeded to the
office the missionary and the Government had between
them destroyed an" ancient and profitable institution,
and in doing so had taken his honor from him.
Nothing is sacred to these wreckers.
“C.J.”: The carefullest firm in Australia? Cer-
tainly. Its sign hangs out in a hamlet hot very
far from Ballgrat. There was to be a big excursion
of the villagers to Ballarat Gardens on the Saturday,
and the business houses had agreed not to open in the
morning. That is, they all did with the exception
of the local Anthony Hordern. This crowd finally
agreed to close on the Saturday morning, on condi-
tion that the excursion train did not reach Ballarat
until it was too late for the excursionists to do any
shopping there.
The dreadfulness of the uncertain. At the annual
meeting in London of the Booksellers’ Provident
Society, Herbert Jenkins, publisher, told a story of
an educated Jap who applied for a billet as clerk.
As a test, he was told to write politely, but
firmly, to 20 of the firm’s most long-winded debtors.
He did. By return post, 20 cheques! Then his
method was inquired into. He had simply written
to them all: —“Your Honorable account. We have
carefully regarded the case, and are pained to inform
you that, unless you send us a remittance by return,
we shall have to take a course of action that will
cause you the utmost astonishment.”
POOR RELATIONS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
W hen I have half an hour to spare
I go and visit at the Zoo,
And watch the many monkeys there
And marvel at the things they do.
W ith apes on their ancestral trees,
I feel entirely at my ease.
I find the grim gorilla fine,
And like the mandrill on his bar :
If these relations are of mine,
As Darwin has implied, they are
The only poor ones, truth to tell,
That I get on with very well.
But Darwin must have made a slip.
One trait they have has wiped away
All dread of poor relationship ;
I stand and watch them half a day,
And not a monkey have I known
To try and touch me for a loan !
VICTORIA.

of the world’s most noted acrobatic
dancers. The words of praise volun-
teered by such a well-known artiste
as Miss Rothert carry great weight,
and should be of interest to all.
12th November, 1913.
Rexona Proprietary Company,
15 O’Connell Street, Sydney,
Dear Sirs,
I do want to tell you how fine
your Rexona Ointment is. I have
travelled all over the world and
never have I come across anything
like it. It was in South Africa that
I first heard of it, when Master How-
ard had a nasty accident on the stage
injuring his face terribly. The poor
boy was suffering intensely and we
did not know what to do until we
were recommended by the Stage
Manager to use Rexona. We did;
and it was marvellous how soon he
was right again. Since then I have
never travelled without a pot of
Rexona Ointment, and I use it not
only as healer, but as an embroca-
tion, for it is a magical reviver to
strained muscles.
As for Rexona Skin and Facial
Soap, I use it every day—not only
to wash with, but for the hair—and
think it fine.
Sincerely Yours,
(Sgd.) GRACE ROTHERT.

THE DOWNFALL OF LITTLE WILLIE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I HAVE hinted at “Little Willie” in
another sketch. He wasn’t Tattle
Willie at all, but his mother always
called him that from the first. He was taller
than the other youngsters of Kid Terrace
and their mates, and older. He was big-
legged, narrow-shouldered and sloping from
the hips upwards, like some old laboring
men. But labor never threatened to be his
vice, though a most precocious cunning in
the pretence of always doing something
useful or being about to do it —was en-
giained in his nature. He was freckled and
sandy, and his eyes were small and reddish,
and close together, and his features were
screwed up with meanness and malicious-
ness. You see, he was a type. Naturally
vicious from the cradle. He always made
his appearance as if he didn’t intend to
make it; as if he were pursued by some
spirit of evil; and evil always followed, in
a small mean way, in Little Willie’s foot-
steps. He always walked in the manner of
something that was hunted, and yet hunting
something much smaller nnd weaker than it-
self—sidling round, warily, in circumambula-
tory directions, with the object of his sense-
less cruelty (a kitten, maybe) or unreason-
ing vindictiveness in the foreground. He
moved as if he were walking on dry grass
amongst a lot of dead burrs. In the matter
of stoning helpless and useful things, when
he thought it was perfectly safe, he was De-
puty, right out of “Edwin I)rood.” Dickens
plainly intended to make Deputy a decent
character, and an instrument in the hands
of Justice; but no living writer could have
made Little Willie anything except just
what he was. All that was good and re-
spectable and meritorious seemed evil to
him. He was a small edition of the “Ugly
Story” personified—The Ugly Story, sired
by Envy and darned by Uncharitableness —-
and fostered by the love of Sensationalism
that always gets whispered round, and
blazoned on the posters of the cheap press,
and hinted, in a cowardly manner, in the
columns thereof. Besides such senseless
annoyances as periodically spilling such of
the neighbors’ morning milk as he couldn’t
drink, and removing and destroying loaves
left by the baker, his chief amusements con-
sisted in knocking at doors, throwing stones
at cats and fowls, and on roofs, mimicking
the crippled and afflicted—and joining the
jim-jams in league against any stray and
harmless drunk that might be delivered into
his hands. There was not the slightest hint,
in the least harmful of that young fiend’s
monkey tricks, of the honest, rollicking,
good-humored and whole-hearted pranks of
that young rascal, “You Charley,” or his
brother, "That there Jimmy o’ mine”; and
how Little Willie came to be Ueorgie’s
brother, I don’t know. I don’t believe he
was. Perhaps he was only an adopted
brother—adopted by the parents in prema-
ture despair of there ever going to be a
Georgie. Strange how often, when a mar-
ried couple give it up, and adopt a child, the
real one hurries along; but the real one, as
often as not, turns out to be the devil of the
two.
It was during the Christmas holidays, and
Georgie, Charlie Stinker and Bugs had
gone out camping, after the independent
manner of young Australians, with their
swags and fishing tackle. Charlie, by the
way, used to take his father with him for a
few days; Dad being a good fisherman and
camp cook, and also handy for cricket when
they were tired of fishing and short-handed
in the field. _ Little Willie was left behind
because of his qualities, and maybe his sul-
len preference. Jimmy stayed to look after
mother. Mothers don’t get many holidays
in our streets; and, when they do, they
don t enjoy them—they’re always on the
ndget to get back to what they call “home”
mi l ' f e ar that something may be going wrong.
I hey always feel quite certain that some-
thing is going wrong—if it is only a window
allegedly left unfastened, or with the moult-
ing rooster and three dusty draggled hens,
left to the care of a neighbor.
But, during the boys’ absence, the neigh-
bors suffered annoyances and torment to the
limit from the unnatural vindictiveness and
nendishness of Little Willie. He seemed to
have me set, especially, though I had never
spoken to him, and had always taken care
to appear blissfully unaware of his very ex-
istence. Perhaps, with the unerring instinct
of his type, he had read disgust and con-
tempt in my manner. Suffice it that he
seemed to take the keenest enjoyment in
stealing a reel of cotton from his mother,
tying one end of the cotton to the door and
unwinding down to the cover of some bushes
on a steep, triangular rocky piece of vacant
ground opposite—from which he would pull
double knocks. He could keenly enjoy this
sort of thing strictly on his own—without
a boy audience, I mean. Add to this, he
aggravated matters by expressing, in pass-
lng, in every clumsy skip and movement, in
every screwing up of his narrow shoulders
in simulated silent laughter, and in every
sidelong evil grin, his exquisite enjoyment
of the situation. The police say, in such
circumstances, that the only thing that might
be done was to get hold of the boy and give
mm a good fright. But there was no
catching Little Willie, and Jimmy—our
only possible champion—was no match for
him. It was beyond the limit of human en-
durance, so, in despair, I laid the whole case
before Charlie, when the boys came back. I
had too much respect for the feelings of
Georgie—with whom his brother was a sore
subject—besides I’d a delicacy in talking to
one brother about another.
Charlie thought, and consulted the nape
of his neck with his little finger, and scraped
wisdom out of the dust with his toe.
. “Does he knock at their doors over there
m—in watyer call Skull Terrace?” he asked
at last.
“Yes,” I said.
At the middle ’un?” he asked again;
does he knock at their door?”
"At all the doors,” I answered.
Charlie thought some more. “All light,”
he said decisively. “We’ll fix him. I’m
thinkin’ it out.”
I’ve described Skull Terrace in another
sketch—a mean little row of five two-
storeyed houses, so called from the peculiar
formation of its doors and windows and the
general ghastliness of its dead-flat front in
the light of after-sunset from full over
Berry’s Kay.
Skull Terrace is furtively and tempor-
arily tenanted by sketches, shadows and
outlines, who seem to appear (that ex-
presses it) always on Saturday mornings,
and put up apologies for blinds and curtains,
in the lower windows at least; and appar-
ently (or not apparently) go to bed in the
dark. Ive never seen any furniture go in,
mu P erll hPs some came in the night before.
Ihey depart, or rather vanish, or fade
away, mostly on Saturday mornings, too.
1 ve never seen any furniture come out; but
perhaps some came out the night before,
also. But I’ve seen bundles of what looked
like bedding dropped from upper windows,
and mysteriously removed in bottle-o-looking
carts, together with a box of tinware and
rubbish and some pots and pans, and a hag
following with a clock and the looking-glass,
ihe blmds mostly remained up; and on
Monday morning the rent-collector would
come round, knock, knock again, inquire
next door, and depart—apparently unmoved.
But the new tenants in the middle house
to which Charlie referred, seemed in a bet-
ter case. Anyway, they sent in some furni-
ture which, if light, was new—perhaps it
was hired. It was. mainly of the spring
stretcher complexion. You know house
rents are very high, and houses hard to get
over here. I caught several glimpses of a
smallish woman, apparentlv young and
pretty, m a kimono; or it might have been a
different one each time —the girl and not
the kimono; two or three of them. Also,
one or two hefty-looking, clean-shaven,
youngish fellows, seemed to go there, or
come away from there, casual-like. There
was also a big, fat, old woman who answered
knocks in a very leisurely manner, inter-
viewed tradesmen, and seemed to run the
house generally. I formed my own opinions,
and my own opinions were totally wrong, as
usual. Most opinions that you form are
wrong.
Running up at the end of our terrace, and
communicating with our little back lane,
and extending round a little paddock at
the back, where are some tiny cottages in
greenery, is a ten-foot lane called Isabel
Avenue; and there were new tenants also
in one of those cottages; and at least one of
the new tenants in Skull Terrace was ac-
quainted with them, for a trim little figure
in black tripped up across the green occa-
sionally, about dusk, and entered Isabel
Avenue. This acquaintanceship came in
handy later on. Make a note of it.
Charlie’s acquaintance with men and mat-
ters on the Point was extensive —and pecu-
liar. It ranged from Sin Quick, mildest of
vegetable Chinamen, to our Liberal member,
.therefore, very early in the morning after
our interview, I was not surprised to see
him’ with, his go-cart, outside the window
of the Middle House, and in apparently
close and earnest conversation with some-
body in pyjamas, who had raised the sash.
I thought perhaps Charlie was on business,
but I had only time to be vaguely puzzled
at the hour he chose, when the window and
blind were drawn down, the door opened,
and a hand and wrist beckoned Charlie in—
and nothing remained but his truck. I meant
to tell you that Little Willie had paid par-
ticular attention to this house during the
holidays, if only for the pleasure of catch
a glimpse, from a safe distance, of a very
white, kimonoed (or rather tmkimonoed)
arm, and a pretty but scared face. He used
to watch the men folk out, I suppose, and I
always ached to wring his scraggy young
neck. That evening Charlie had an inter-
view with some of his friends down under
the rock on the green. I noticed that neither
Little Willie nor his brother was present
at the conference.
Next afternoon was on a dull, dead,
smothering, smoke-cloudy Saturday. The
shops were closed on account of the Act.
The trams were held up on account of an
explosion in the power-house. The Shore
was apparently depopulated, and the last
policeman seemingly dead and buried. Once
again Charlie and his friends foregathered.
This time Little Willie was present. Georgie
had taken his mother and a little sister to
Manly; so Little Willie had the house to
himself._ Charlie gave quick and explicit
instructions, and each man his allotted posi-
tion. Then they dived down into the road
as one, in extended order, and across to
Skull Terrace, where each stood by a door.
Little Willie had the door of Middle House ;
Charlie the door of the upper end house;
Jimmy the lowest door in the terrace; and
Stinker and Bugs the doors in between.
Then Charlie, seeing that the firing-line was
in position and ready, reached up for the
knocker and gave a great double knock ; and
almost simultaneously with his second
knock, they all knocked hard —and scooted.
They slithered right and left, and round
into a lane at the lower end of the terrace,
and dived down under the rails of a vacant
lot at the upper end ; and scattered in all
directions. Little Willie had the longest
run, but his legs were the only good things
about him —and there was nlenty of time.
Seconds passed—a whole minute—then
two. Then there was a sinister movement
of the blind in the top window of the upper
end house, the sash was slowly raised, and
a furtive male in short sleeves laid first one,
and then the other arm carefully on the sill,
projected head and shoulders cautiously and
glowered up and down the narrow pave-
ment, the dirty green blind hanging down
behind him. In somewhat similar wise an-
other window opened at the lower end, and
a h Ssr??, ad appeared. Likewise, next door
to Middle House opened gingerly; then
wider, and another hag bent outwards hug-
ging her flat breast with skinny, tightly-
folded arms, screwing chin and nose to-
gether, and turning her head on a long
scraggy neck, iguana-like, up and down the
street. Then other doors and windows.
They wanted to see if it was the rent-collec-
tor, perhaps, or maybe the police—or a pre-
vious landlord, time-payment man, or other
creditor that had got on their track. Then
the worms and maggots withdrew into their

skull, doors and windows closed, and all was
blank.
A quarter of an hour passed, twenty
minutes, half an hour. Then the boys turned
up singly and in quick succession from all
directions, except across the green. Lastly,
Little Willie, from some safe hole known to
himself, his whole body writhing in impish,
malicious enjoyment. They gathered safe
handy to the lower end of our Isabel Avenue.
A short conference, and they separated into
two detachments, up and down our street
and round down into the road again. I was
down on the front verandah this time, and,
as Charlie passed, he took his opportunity
to whisper hoarsely —-
“Now watch! Mr. Lawrence! Go up
inter yer balkenny en’ watch.” I went up.
They gathered opposite the ends of Skull
Terrace, or a little higher up and lower-
down. Then they darted across, extending
in open order as they ran ; and, with military
precision, ranged themselves alongside their
respective doors. Little Willie was to give
the signal knock this time. He wanted to,
because he had a shrewd suspicion that at
least one of the young men was at home.
He had furthest to run and wanted more
time. He claimed it as his right, as Charlie
told me afterwards, so they gave it to him.
He peered up and down the firing line to
see if all were in position and ready, and, as
one man, they raised their hands to their
respective knockers. But none of them
knocked. Neither did Little Willie. Just
as he grabbed his knocker, that door was
snatched open. It hadn’t been on the latch !
And Little Willie went in with it. There
was a fearsome moment, and Little Willie
shot out onto the pavement, and landed on
his back, curled up like a hard kicked cur.
Then, from that doorway swooped Good
Mrs. Brown. Yes, Good Mrs. Brown —
straight out of Dickens’s “Dombey & Son”
-—beak, claws and all. She looked a fear-
some bag. She swooped on Little Willie
before Little Willie had time to recover him-
self and regain his feet. And Little Willie
played the part of “Biler” to perfection.
He held up his arms to shield his head and
face, while she clawed and tore at him ; and
even I heard her screech. She took her
claws ou.t of him for one moment, and he sat
up and glared at her in helpless, speechless,
paralysed terror. He was trying to edge off
on his hands and heels, when out from that
doorway bundled the First Witch from
“Macbeth” —none other. She had a kero-
sene bucket full of soapsuds. She was a
wiry witch. With a sloosh! she washed
Little Willie off the narrow pavement into
the gutter. Then in that fateful doorway
appeared Satan’s own Mother-in-Law with
another bucket of dirty water. The First
Witch seized it and slooshed Little Willie
some distance down the gutter. Then she
pounced on him again, got her talons in his
collar, wrenched him to his feet facing the
house, clawed his chin and hooked his face
upwards and battered him generally. Then
one of the top windows went up with a
bang, and Bill Sikes himself, in shirt sleeves,
thrust out his head and shoulders. Or was
it Gabbet? I couldn’t be sure at that dis-
tance.
“’OLD ’IM!” he boomed. “’OLD IM,
TILL —I GIT ME TROWSIS ON!”
That was the stimulant! There was no
holding Little Willie then. He broke from
the hag, and his legs ran away with him
like those of a champion cyclist, training
hard. It was all so quick that I couldn’t
be sure which way he went.
I was dazedly wondering whether it was
all a dream —or whether the new tenants
had really vanished after the manner of
others, and newer ones had taken their place
in like manner.
A mighty pall of rain cloud had swooped
over, the flood came down and ceased, the
gutters emptied and the street was fresh
and clean and cool, a clear arch rose over
sunset, the doors and windows of Skull
Terrace were closed, the front of Skull
Terrace was blanker and ghastlier in the
after-glare than ever.
A drenched and draggled something came
sidling fearfully up our street, keeping close
to the walls, and slipped into the house of
Little Willie’s parents. It was Little Willie
himself, and Little Willie’s people were not
home from Manly yet. Two strange, over-
coated, macintoshed and slouch-hatted
figures came out of the shadows from some-
where and slipped into Isabella Terrace.
And they had scarcely vanished when round
the corner, out of the lane, slouched Bill
Sikes and Gabbet, both —their own selves.
They lifted the latch of the little gate and
knocked at Little Willie’s door. I suppose
it was opened. / heard it bang. Messrs.
Sikes and Gabbet came out again. Their
backs were to me and their shoulders were
jerking suspiciously. They went round into
the lane again. I went to my back window
to see what might be going on out there.
Outside Little Willie’s back gate I saw
Good Mrs. Brown crouching, her witch chin
fitted between two of the pickets, her evil
eyes peering from each side of her beak nose
into Little Willie’s yard. She seemed to be
waiting and watching for something.
Up that yard I saw Little Willie come
creeping. He was going to bolt. He kept
well under cover of a small wooden erection
at the top of the yard, until lie reached it.
Then he broke cover, made for the gate, and
was confronted by the Visitation ! With a
yelp he fled inside, and the back door banged.
Another second and he burst out at the
front and ran right into Gabbet.
I dropped into the School of Arts that
night to see a variety performance, and was
£o see my friends, Charlie, Jimmy,
Stinker and likewise Bugs, in the best seats.
And next morning Little Willie was gone
-—bolted, cleared out, run away, to the great
grief and consternation of his mother. But
late that evening I saw his father sitting
on the balcony, chair well tilted back and
heels on the rail, contemplatively smoking
the pipe of peace and contentment, and by
these tokens I concluded that all was well —
Little Willie hadn’t returned. He fled to
an uncle who had a small farm near Gos-
ford, and that uncle wired down next day
that Little Willie was there, and that he
was either going to make a man or a cripple
of him.
And verily, my friends, I wrote all that
Sunday night, and looking forth from the
balcony early on Monday morning. I saw
Charlie delivering a truck-load of kindling
wood to a face and a big suit of pyjamas
in the lower window of Middle House, Skull
Terrace, And when Charlie was turning
away, he seemed by his gestures to refuse
money.
“Yes,” said Charlie, some time after
they’d gone, “Those tenants was that
Variety actor company. They travelled all
over the suburbs an’ to Manly ’n’ Parra-
matta. They was saving up to go hong
tore, so one of the jokers called it; an’ it
wouldn’t run to hotels en boardin’ houses.
The big, old woman does the cookin’ an’
cleanin’. She’s the mother of them —at least,
she’s the mother of most of them. She
owns the ferniclier. The other women all
call tlieirselves ‘misses,’ but they’re all
married to each other. So that’s all right.”
HENRY LAWSON.
Remarks on the Present State
of Peru.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ON the fringe of the meadows of ages.
Aglow with pale blossoms of deed
That ripple and turn like the pages
The slow worm at midnight may read,
I saw a tall gentleman filling
His pipe, while the sunset aflood
O’er the mountain of evening was spilling
A flame-hearted glory of blood.
Though all unexpected our meeting,
llis face and his bearing were known;
As friends of the past was our greeting,
I clasped his brown hand in my own —
Old comrades in ventures afar —oh,
The glance of those hawk eyes I knew,
For the form was the form of Pizarro
And the bond of our souls was Peru.
The winds of the dark in their motion
Strange dreams to our pillows had blown,
The whispers of forest and ocean
Breathed low in our hearing alone
Of mountain peaks rent with the thunder
Where death had been king from of old,
And palaces shrouded in wonder •
Aflame with their hangings of gold.
Pizarro ! The doom and the splendor
Blent still in his terrible name.
Shall kings in their battle strength render
Account of their glory and shame?
Oh, heavy the hand of our smiting
And bitter the fame that we won ;
Hot blood was the ink of our writing—-
The blood of the Child of the Sun.
The hero seemed joyed at our meeting,
I saw the dark smile in his eyes.
“What! Senor de Moses ! Fair greeting !
Well, .this is a pleasant surprise.”
I remarked, “Is there anything doing?”
My fancy was spreading her wings.
I admit I had visions of strewing
Hyde Park with some corpses and things.
He laughed in his beard and made answer.
“There is gold for the winning,” he cried,
“’Tis a job for a resolute man, sir,
Who has little regard for his hide.
I have seen in one window aglitter,
All polished and shining and new,
Such wealth as we won in the bitter
Brief conquest of sunny Peru.
“The plates are a trifle more slender,
The weight is perhaps somewhat less,
But the feelings the baubles engender
Are like the young south wind’s caress.
There are diamonds sleeping on velvet,
And jewels of ruby and pearl,
And the bright golden stuff as they shelve it
Is fit for a monarch’s best girl.
“Some resolute persons by smiting
The heads from a dozen police,
Without any serious fighting
Might bear off five bushels apiece
As easy as gathering roses;
Our friend William Sikes will assist
And I fancy that you, Senor Moses,
In a hazard like this might enlist.”
I smiled at the hero’s suggestion.
“Not so, sir, may fortune be won.
Beyond all debating or question
The thing you propose could be done,
But the prospect of speedily hanging
At the end of a long piece of thread
We associate now with the banging
Of kindly police on the head.
“And a thing that our spirit embitters,
The copy-books taught us to write,
For all is not gold though it glitters
In the jewellers’ windows at night;
But if you have thought of adventures
In places far distant and new,
By issuing shares or debentures
Some bunches of gold might accrue.
“We cannot send folk to perdition
By using a sword or a knife ;
Our exploits we conduct on commission
When leading the strenuous life;
Our Peruvian wealth is on paper,^
And the Incas whose homes we traspoi)
Are the butcher and grocer and draper,
And persons attached to the soil.”
The last ray of red was departing;
The purple hindquarters of day
From the wind-whip of evening were smart-
ing
As the chariot of light rolled away ;
The pipe that the hero was smoking
Had burned to the last flake of ash,
As in doubt our high spirits stood yoking
Their waggon of fancy to cash.
Bright casli that the world .wheels were
spinning,
To weave on the looms of delight,
The hard, simple stuff was worth winning,
But no Inca throne was in sight.
For the world since our days of adventure
Is a dull, prosy place where at best
By striving and toil you may rent your
Allotment and pay like the rest.
We passed from that meadow in sorrow,
We went with a sigh and a tear
Some trace of old gladness to borrow
From young and regenerate beer.
Twin mugs that were bearded and gleaming
We drained till there splashed on our view
An aureate ocean outstreaming
From a drunk and disordered Peru.
N.S.W.

as the hallmark on silver. The frame is
cast in one piece, giving great strength
and rigidity. The horizontal cutter block
is of solid steel, and grooved on all four
sides to take the various cutters. The
machine also has an improved variable
feed motion, with three speeds, which can
be stopped or started instantly. Very
handy it is, too.
Other details will be explained by our
expert if you write him.
